Can Permission to a Minor Be Restricted?
Dispute on restriction of minor's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi and Hanbali positions on restricting a minor's permission, evaluate the rational proofs of each, and apply the preferred view in a modern fatwa scenario.

Core Idea
A guardian's permission to a minor is either an annulment of interdiction (unrestricted) or a grant of agency (restrictable) — the Hanbali view aligns better with maslahah.

This passage presents a key ikhtilaf between the Hanafi and Hanbali schools regarding the nature of permission (idhn) given to a minor to engage in trade. At stake is whether a guardian can limit the minor's activities to specific types, persons, or times. The resolution of this dispute has practical implications for modern guardianship and financial empowerment of minors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
Locate the Hanafi view that permission cannot be restricted (general until revoked) and the Hanbali view that it can be restricted to specific types, persons, times, or places.
2
Extract the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is that permission is an annulment (isqat) of the original interdiction, and annulment does not admit restriction or partiality.
This is a rational (aqli) proof based on the nature of annulment.
3
Extract the Hanbali proof
The Hanbali proof is analogical: permission is like agency (wakalah), which admits restriction. Additionally, maslahah (benefit) demands flexibility to tailor permission to the minor's needs.
This is a qiyas based on wakalah and a teleological argument from purpose.
4
Analyze the author's tarjih
The author (Zarqa) states the Hanbali view is 'awjah' (more sound) and more in line with maslahah. He also critiques the Hanafi premise: annulment of a restriction can be partial.
This is an example of tarjih (preferencing) based on rationality and purpose.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under the Hanafi view, any attempt to restrict results in general permission until revoked; under the Hanbali view, restrictions are binding and the minor remains interdicted beyond them.
Practical difference: e.g., a guardian allows only wheat trading; Hanafi says minor can trade anything; Hanbali says only wheat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٣/٦٢ - قابلية الإذن للتخصيص وعدمها : وهذا الإذن في الاجتهاد الحنفي لا يقبل التخصيص. فإذا أذن للصغير ممثله الشرعي ببعض التصرفات من هذا النوع كالبيع مثلاً، وقيده بنوع من البضاعة أو شخص أو سوق أو زمان مخصوص، أصبح الصغير مأذوناً إذناً عاماً بالتجارة والتعامل في جميع الأنواع ومع جميع الأشخاص في جميع الأزمنة والأمكنة حتى يحجره ممثله من جديد، لأن الإذن يقبل الاسترداد. وذهبت اجتهادات أخرى كالاجتهاد الحنبلي إلى أن هذا الإذن يقبل التخصيص ويتقيد بما يقيده به الممثل الشرعي للصغير نوعاً وشخصاً وزماناً ومكاناً. فيبقى الصغير المميّز فيما وراء حدود إذنه محجوراً غير نافذ التصرف . وحجة الاجتهاد الحنفي أن الإذن رفع وإسقاط لقيد الحجر الأصلي، وأن الإسقاط في نظرهم لا يقبل التقييد. وحجة الاجتهادات الأخرى أنَّ الإذن كالتوكيل والتوكيل يقبل التقييد، فيتقيَّد الوكيل بما يقيده به الموكل لأن سلطة الوكيل مستمدة منه ولا يخفى أن هذا النظر أوجه وأجرى مع المصلحة، فإن الإذن للصغير بمباشرة التجارة والتعامل إنما شُرعَ لتمرينه واختباره وقد يكون له بصيرة في نوع دون نوع، وقد يؤمن عليه من التعامل مع شخص دون غيره. فيجب أن يصح تخصيص الإذن، وإلا ضاعت الحكمة منه . على أن كون الإذن إسقاطاً لقيد الحجر لا يستلزم عدم إمكان تخصيصه لأن المقيد يمكن أن تطلق منه ناحية فقط ويبقى غيرها على قيده .
13/62 - The ability of permission to be restricted or not: This permission, in the Hanafi ijtihad, does not admit restriction. So if the legal guardian of the minor authorizes him to some acts of this type, such as selling, and restricts it to a type of merchandise, a person, a market, or a specific time, the minor becomes generally permitted (ma'dhun) in trade and transactions in all types, with all persons, in all times and places, until his guardian places him back under interdiction, because permission can be revoked. Other ijtihads, such as the Hanbali, held that this permission admits restriction and is limited by what the legal guardian specifies in terms of type, person, time, and place. So the discerning minor remains interdicted beyond the limits of his permission, and his transactions are not effective. The proof of the Hanafi ijtihad is that permission is a removal and annulment of the original interdiction, and annulment in their view does not admit restriction. The proof of the other ijtihads is that permission is like agency (wakalah), and agency admits restriction; the agent is bound by what the principal restricts him to, because the agent's authority derives from the principal. It is clear that this view is more sound and more in line with benefit, for permission for the minor to engage in trade and transactions was legislated to train and test him. He may have insight in one type but not another, and it may be feared for him in dealing with one person but not another. So it must be valid to restrict permission; otherwise, the wisdom behind it is lost. Moreover, the fact that permission is an annulment of the interdiction does not necessitate that it cannot be restricted, because a restrictable thing can have one aspect released while the rest remains restricted.
Source pages p. 762

Commentary
This passage discusses whether a guardian's permission (idhn) to a minor can be restricted to specific types, persons, times, or places. The Hanafi position is that permission is a removal of the original interdiction (raf' al-hajr) and thus cannot be partial; it is either full permission or none. If a guardian attempts to restrict, the minor becomes generally permitted in all areas until the guardian explicitly revokes permission (since idhn is revocable). The Hanbali view, on the other hand, treats permission as similar to agency (wakalah), which is inherently restrictable. The Hanbali argument notes that the purpose of permission is training and testing, which suggests flexibility to limit the minor's exposure. The author (Zarqa) notes that the Hanbali view seems more sound (awjah) and more aligned with maslahah, and he offers a critique of the Hanafi proof: an annulment of a restriction need not be absolute; one can release one aspect while keeping others restricted. This passage thus presents a difference of opinion (ikhtilaf) between the Hanafi and Hanbali schools, with the author leaning toward the Hanbali position.

Key Terms
إذن (idhn): Permission granted by the legal guardian to a minor to engage in trade and financial transactions.
تخصيص (takhsis): The act of restricting or particularizing a permission to certain types, persons, times, or places.
حجر (hajr): Interdiction; legal restriction on a person's capacity to act due to minority, insanity, or other reasons.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school presented in this passage, if a guardian permits a minor to trade only in wheat, what is the legal effect?
A) The minor can only trade in wheat; other trades are void.
B) The minor becomes generally permitted in all trade until revoked.
C) The permission is invalid because it is restricted.
D) The minor must seek a court order to expand the permission.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school views permission to a minor as an absolute annulment of interdiction, thus unrestricted; the Hanbali school sees it as a grant of authority that can be tailored to the minor's best interest.

Explicit and Implicit Permission for a Minor
Definition of explicit and implicit permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between explicit (sarahah) and implicit (dalalah) permission for a minor and apply each type to a scenario.

Core Idea
A minor's permission to trade may be given by express words or implied by the guardian's conduct.

In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of a minor's commercial transactions hinges on the guardian's permission. This lesson introduces the two primary forms such permission can take: express verbal authorization (sarahah) and implicit permission inferred from the guardian's conduct (dalalah). Understanding this distinction is crucial for muftis evaluating the legal capacity of minors in trade.

Guided Path
1
Define explicit permission (sarahah)
Identify that sarahah requires a clear verbal statement from the guardian, such as 'I permit you to trade' or 'Go to the market and sell and buy.'
The command must directly authorize commercial activity.
2
Define implicit permission (dalalah)
Recognize that dalalah is inferred from actions that unmistakably indicate consent, like providing the minor with trading capital or not objecting to known trading activity.
The conduct must be clear enough to leave no doubt about the guardian's consent.
3
Apply the distinction
Classify given scenarios as sarahah or dalalah based on the examples in the text and general Hanafi principles.
Silence alone is not sufficient; positive conduct is required for implicit permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٤/٦٢ - الصراحة والدلالة في الإذن : والإذن للصغير يكون إما صراحة بأن يقول له ممثله : أذنت لك بالتجارة، أو اذهب إلى السوق فبغ واشتر، ونحو ذلك؛ وإما دلالة، كما لو
14/62 - Explicitness and implication in permission: Permission for the minor is either explicitly, by his guardian saying: 'I permit you to trade,' or 'Go to the market and sell and buy,' and the like; or implicitly, such as if
Source pages p. 762

Commentary
This short passage introduces the two ways in which permission (idhn) can be given to a minor: express (sarahah) by clear words, and implicit (dalalah) by actions or circumstances that indicate permission. The text provides examples of express permission: 'I permit you to trade' or 'Go to the market and buy and sell.' It then begins to mention the implicit type but is cut off. In Hanafi fiqh, implicit permission is established if, for example, the guardian provides the minor with capital and does not object to his trading, or similar conduct that clearly implies consent. The passage serves as a definitional or rule-stating segment, likely to be memorized.

Key Terms
صراحة (sarahah): Explicit or express statement of permission by the guardian.
دلالة (dalalah): Implicit indication; permission inferred from conduct or context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of explicit (sarahah) permission for a minor to trade?
A) The guardian provides the minor with a shop to manage.
B) The guardian says, 'Go to the market and sell and buy.'
C) The guardian remains silent while the minor trades.
D) The guardian gives the minor money for school supplies.

Key Takeaway
Permission to a minor may be given either expressly by words or implicitly by conduct that clearly indicates consent.

Distinguishing Permission from Employment
Legal rule distinguishing idhn from istikhdām.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between idhn (permission) and istikhdām (employment) in Hanafi law and apply the distinction to determine whether a minor becomes ma'dhūn (permitted to transact).

Core Idea
Permission must be for a type of transaction; a one-time errand is mere employment, not permission.

This lesson addresses a crucial distinction in Hanafi law regarding minors: when does a guardian's action make the minor a ma'dhūn (permitted to engage in trade)? The text draws a sharp line between granting general permission (idhn) for a category of transactions and merely employing the minor (istikhdām) for a specific, one-time task. Understanding this difference is essential for determining the legal validity of transactions conducted by minors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two concepts
Locate the terms 'idhn' (إذن) and 'istikhdām' (استخدام) in the passage. Note that the passage emphasizes the need to distinguish them.
The passage begins with 'التمييز بين الإذن والاستخدام'.
2
Define permission (idhn)
Extract the conditions for valid permission: it must be explicit or implicit, and it must release the right for the minor to engage in a 'type of transaction' (taṣarruf nawʿī). This means a category like buying or selling, not a single instance.
The passage uses 'تصرف نوعي' to indicate a category.
3
Define employment (istikhdām)
Understand that sending the minor for a specific item (e.g., food, clothing) is a one-time personal task. This is employment, not permission, because it is limited and not an authorization for a type.
The passage gives the example of sending the minor to the market for a specific purchase.
4
Apply the rule
Given a scenario, classify whether it constitutes idhn or istikhdām. Use the condition: if the guardian authorizes repeated transactions of a type, it is idhn; if only a specific errand, it is istikhdām.
If a guardian says 'buy and sell for me in the market,' that is permission. If he says 'buy this bread,' it is employment.
5
Understand the consequence
Realize that only idhn makes the minor ma'dhūn. If istikhdām were considered permission, then every errand would grant legal capacity, which is untenable.
The passage warns 'وإلا انسد باب الاستخدام المجرد'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٥/٦٢ - التمييز بين الإذن بالتصرف وبين الاستخدام:
ولكن على كل حال يجب التمييز بين الإذن والاستخدام، فلا يتحقق الإذن إلا بما يفيد صراحة أو دلالة إطلاق الحق للصغير المميز من ممثله الشرعي في تصرف نوعي، أي ببعض أنواع التعامل. أما لو أرسله إلى السوق ليشتري له شيئاً معيناً من طعام أو كسوة أو غيرهما، فهذا استعانة واستخدام في تصرف شخصي محدود وليس إذناً في تصرف نوعي متكرر، فلا يصير به الصغير مأذوناً، وإلا انسد باب الاستخدام المجرد (ر: المجلة ٩٦٩ ، والهداية وشروحها كتاب المأذون ،۲۱۹/۸ ، ورد المحتار ٩٨/٥).
15/62 - Distinction between permission to transact and employment: In any case, one must distinguish between permission and employment. Permission is only realized by what expressly or implicitly conveys the release of the right for the discerning minor from his legal representative in a type of transaction, i.e., in some kinds of dealings. But if he sends him to the market to buy a specific item of food, clothing, or the like, this is assistance and employment in a limited personal transaction, not permission for a repeated type of transaction. Hence, the minor does not become a permitted (ma'dhūn) through this; otherwise, the door of mere employment would be closed. (See: Majalla 969, al-Hidaya and its commentaries, Kitab al-Ma'dhun, 8/219, and Radd al-Muhtar 5/98)
Source pages p. 763

Commentary
This passage draws a sharp line between two concepts: permission (idhn) and employment (istikhdām). Permission must be general in nature, granting the minor authority to engage in a category of transactions repeatedly. It can be given explicitly or implicitly, but it must convey the release of a right to engage in a type of dealing (taṣarruf nawʿī). In contrast, sending the minor on a one-time errand to buy a specific item does not confer any ongoing authority. Doing so is merely using the minor for a specific task (istikhdām). If such errands were considered permission, then every time a guardian sends a child to buy something, the child would be deemed a permitted minor, which would abolish the distinction and leave no room for simple assistance. The sources cited (Majalla, Hidaya, Radd al-Muhtar) confirm this distinction. This is important for determining the legal capacity of the minor's transactions: a permitted minor's transactions are binding, whereas a non-permitted minor's transactions require guardian approval.

Key Terms
استخدام (istikhdām): Using the minor for a specific, one-time task, not granting general authorization to transact.
تصرف نوعي (taṣarruf nawʿī): A category or type of transaction (e.g., selling, buying) as opposed to a specific singular act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following would make a minor ma'dhūn (permitted to transact)?
A) Sending him to buy a specific item of clothing.
B) Telling him to purchase groceries every day without specifying what.
C) Giving him general authority to buy and sell goods of a certain type.
D) Asking him to deliver a message to a shopkeeper.

Key Takeaway
Permission (idhn) requires authorization for a type of transaction; a one-time specific errand is mere employment (istikhdām) and does not grant a minor legal capacity to transact.

Classification of Minors: Permitted (Ma'dhūn) vs Interdicted (Mahjūr)
Summary categorization of discerning minors into permitted/interdicted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify discerning minors into permitted and interdicted and state the legal effect of permission in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Permission to trade only grants full capacity to a discerning minor; non-discerning minors remain unaffected.

In Hanafi law, a minor's ability to enter binding contracts depends on two factors: reaching the age of discernment (tamyīz) and receiving explicit permission (idhn) from the legal guardian. This concluding passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's work provides a crisp classification: the permitted minor (ma'dhūn) is treated as fully capable in the permitted transactions, while the interdicted minor (mahjūr) remains under guardian oversight. Importantly, permission has no effect on children below the age of discernment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the classification: permitted (ma'dhūn) is one whose guardian has given permission to engage in transactions; interdicted (mahjūr) is one without such permission. Note the Arabic terms.
The permission is specific to the type of transaction (e.g., trade).
2
Understand the legal consequence of permission
For the permitted minor, transactions are binding and valid even if there is gross exploitation (ghubn fāḥish). For the interdicted minor, transactions are suspended (mawqūf) pending guardian approval.
The text explicitly says the permitted minor is 'like a fully capable person' in those transactions.
3
Distinguish the role of tamyīz
The rule only applies to minors who have reached the age of discernment (ṣighār mumayyizūn). Below that age, performance capacity (ahliyyat al-adā') is absent, and all transactions are void. Permission does not change that.
This reinforces the Hanafi theory of gradual capacity: the stage of tamyīz is the threshold for suspended (not void) transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٦/٦٢ - والخلاصة : أنه بالنظر إلى الأذن وعدمه يصنف الصغار المميزون إلى صنفين: مأذونين ومحجورين:
فالمأذون هو الذي أطلق له ممثله الشرعي حق التعامل في التصرفات التي تتوقف في الأصل على موافقته وهو النوع الثالث المتقدم. وعندئذ الصغير في هذه التصرفات ككامل الأهلية تنفذ عليه وتلزمه ولو كان فيها غبن فاحش.
والمحجور هو الذي لا يزال تصرّفه في هذا النوع خاضعاً لإجازة ممثله الشرعي لقصور أهلية الأداء لديه.
ويتضح من ذلك أن الإذن بالتجارة يختص تأثيره بالصغار المميزين. أما الذين دون سن التمييز فأهلية الأداء معدومة لديهم؛ وكل تصرفاتهم باطلة كما تقدم. فالإذن لهم وعدمه سواء.
16/62 - Summary: Considering permission or its absence, discerning minors are classified into two categories: permitted (ma'dhūn) and interdicted (mahjūr). The permitted is the one whose legal representative has released the right to engage in transactions that originally depend on his consent—this is the third type mentioned earlier. In such transactions, the minor is like a fully capable person; they are binding upon him and he is liable, even if there is gross exploitation (ghubn fāḥish). The interdicted is one whose transactions in this type remain subject to the approval of his legal representative, due to deficiency in his performance capacity (ahliyyat al-adā'). From this, it becomes clear that permission to trade specifically affects discerning minors. As for those below the age of discernment, they have no performance capacity; all their transactions are void, as previously stated. Permission makes no difference to them.
Source pages p. 763

Commentary
This concluding passage provides a clear classification of discerning minors (ṣighār mumayyizūn) into two categories based on whether they have received permission (idhn) from their guardian. (1) The permitted minor (ma'dhūn): once permission is given, the minor has full capacity to engage in transactions that originally required guardian consent. His transactions are valid and binding, even if the price is unfair (ghubn fāḥish). He is treated like an adult in contractual matters. (2) The interdicted minor (mahjūr): if no permission has been given, his transactions are not automatically valid; they require the guardian's subsequent approval (ijāza). This is due to a deficiency in his capability to perform legal acts (qusūr ahliyyat al-adā'). The text also emphasizes that permission only matters for minors who have reached the age of discernment (tamyīz). Minors below that age have no legal capacity for transactions at all; their actions are void ab initio. Thus, granting permission to such young children has no effect. This classification is fundamental in Hanafi family and commercial law. It derives from the theory of legal capacity (ahliyya) and is part of the third stage of capacity development (the stage of tamyīz).

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhūn): A discerning minor who has been granted permission by his guardian to engage in a type of transaction, giving him full contractual capacity.
محجور (mahjūr): A discerning minor who has not been granted permission; his transactions are suspended pending guardian approval.
غبن فاحش (ghubn fāḥish): Gross exploitation or unfairness in price, which would normally invalidate a transaction for a minor but not for a permitted minor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A 12-year-old discerning minor is given permission by his guardian to trade. He sells his watch for half its market value. What is the ruling?
A) The sale is void because of gross exploitation.
B) The sale is binding and cannot be rescinded.
C) The sale is suspended pending guardian approval.
D) The sale is valid only if the buyer agrees to adjust the price.

Key Takeaway
Permission to trade only empowers discerning minors; non-discerning minors' transactions are void ab initio.

The Fourth Stage of Legal Capacity: Puberty
Explains puberty as the fourth stage of capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi school determines puberty (by natural signs or by maximum age) and identify the mufta bihi position on the age limit.

Core Idea
Puberty is established by natural signs; if absent, the person becomes legally pubescent at 15 years in the Hanafi school.

Puberty marks the transition from childhood to full legal responsibility. In this lesson, we explore how the Sharia defines puberty, the consensus on its legal effects, and the Hanafi ikhtilaf on the maximum age when natural signs are delayed. You will learn why the school adopts 15 years as the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the significance of puberty
Understand why puberty is the most important natural stage: it signals the completion of both physical and mental capacity, making one mukallaf (legally responsible).
2
Distinguish between actual and legal puberty
Note that actual puberty is determined by natural signs (ejaculation for males, menstruation for females). If signs appear, they rule. If delayed, legal puberty is presumed at the end of the usual age range.
3
Trace the ijma on legal effects
All schools agree that upon puberty, a person becomes subject to all obligations of the Sharia: faith, worship, social duties, and penal responsibility.
4
Analyze the ikhtilaf on maximum age
Examine the dispute between Abu Hanifa (18/17) and the Sahibayn (15 for both). Identify the dalil for each and the reason for tarjih.
The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث والستون
الطور الرابع للأهلية طور البلوغ
١/٦٣ - البلوغ أهم المراحل الطبيعية التي تمر بها حياة الإنسان، لأنه ينتقل فيها من طور الصغر إلى طور الكبر، ويصبح من مبدئها مكلفاً يلقى على عاتقه نظير ما يحمله سائر الكبار المكلفين من التكاليف الشرعية، ومن المسؤوليات عنها.
ذلك أن البلوغ في الحالات العادية السليمة يصل فيه نمو الجسم وقدرته إلى الحد الكافي لتحمل التكاليف الشرعية البدنية، كما يصل فيه نمو العقل والإدراك إلى الحد الكافي من معرفة الخير والشر، والنافع والضار، وعواقب الأعمال. وإنما يتفاوت الأشخاص بعد ذلك - فيما وراء هذا النصاب الكافي من قدرتي الجسم والعقل - تفاوتاً طبيعياً بدرجات الذكاء والمواهب الفطرية التي لا يتساوى فيها اثنان من الناس. وهذا التفاوت في الزيادة عن لا الحد الكافي [؟] عبره له ولا يمنع تساوي الجميع في القدرة على فهم التكاليف وحمل المسؤوليات بعد توافر النصاب اللازم من مؤهلاتها لديهم. وبناءً على ذلك اتفق فقهاء الشريعة على أنَّ الإنسان بالبلوغ يتوجه إليه خطاب التكليف الموجه من الشارع إلى الناس، فيصبح البالغ مشمولاً بالخطاب التشريعي ومكلفاً بما يفرضه ذلك الخطاب على الناس من واجبات عامة بشرائطها الشرعية من الإيمان بالله تعالى وكتبه ورسله وسائر شعب الإيمان، ومن العبادات المفروضة وتحصيل القدر الضروري من العلم لإقامة الدين والأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر، والجهاد، والمشاركة بالمال والعمل في كل ما تتوقف عليه مصالح المجتمع الإسلامي، إلى سائر التكاليف والواجبات كل ذلك بقدر الاستطاعة. وكذلك يصبح البالغ مسؤولاً عن طاعة النظام الشرعي الذي يفرض الأمن الداخلي، واجتناب العدوان والثأر الشخصي، والخضوع للحكام في حدود سلطاتهم المشروعة والرجوع إليهم في حل الخصومات. فيصير الإنسان بالبلوغ خاضعاً لنظام العقوبات الشرعي العام فيما يرتكبه من جرائم ومخالفات.
٢/٦٣ - حد البلوغ:
والبلوغ يُعرف ويعتبر بظهور علاماته الطبيعية (1). فمتى ظهرت علاماته أخذ البلوغ حكمه بمجرد ظهورها دون التقيد بسن معينة. فإذا تأخر ظهور علاماته اعتبر الشخص بالغاً حكماً متى وصل إلى نهاية سن البلوغ المعتادة. فلسن البلوغ امتداد بين بداية ونهاية يكون البلوغ الفعلي بينهما بحسب العادة. فبدايتها اثنتا عشرة سنة في الذكور وتسع سنوات في الإناث، لأن ذلك يختلف باختلاف الأقاليم: ففي البيئات الحارة يبكر البلوغ وفي الباردة يتأخر. فاعتبر أدنى الحدود الطبيعية فيه. وأما نهايتها فقد اختلفت فيها الاجتهادات فذهب أبو حنيفة إلى أنها ثماني عشرة سنة في الذكور وسبع عشرة سنة في الإناث، وهو المروي عن ابن عباس ولكن جمهور الأئمة وصاحبي أبي حنيفة على أنها خمس عشرة سنة في الذكور والإناث جميعاً، وهو الرأي الراجح المفتى به في المذهب الحنفي، وعليه المجلة. والشخص بين بداية سن البلوغ ونهايتها يسمى: مراهقاً. والمراهق إذا ادعى البلوغ أو أقرَّ به وكان نمو جسمه محتملاً. (1) وهي في الغلام الإنزال وفي الفتاة الحيض أو الحبل.
Chapter Sixty-Three: The Fourth Stage of Capacity: The Stage of Puberty
1/63 - Puberty is the most important natural stage that human life passes through, because in it one transitions from the stage of childhood to the stage of adulthood, and from its beginning becomes a legally responsible person (mukallaf) upon whom falls the same as what all other adult responsible persons bear of legal obligations (takalif shariyyah) and responsibilities for them. That is because in normal healthy cases, puberty reaches the point where the body's growth and ability reach the sufficient limit to bear physical legal obligations, and likewise the growth of intellect and perception reaches the sufficient limit to know good and evil, benefit and harm, and the consequences of actions. People then vary - beyond this sufficient threshold of bodily and mental capacities - naturally in degrees of intelligence and innate talents in which no two people are equal. This variation in excess beyond the sufficient limit [؟] does not prevent everyone from being equal in the ability to understand obligations and bear responsibilities after the necessary threshold of their qualifications is available. Based on this, the jurists of the Sharia agreed that upon puberty the address of obligation (khitab al-taklif) from the Lawgiver to people is directed to the person, so the pubescent becomes included in the legislative address and obligated by what that address imposes on people of general duties according to their Sharia conditions: faith in Allah Almighty, His Books, His Messengers, and all other branches of faith; and the prescribed acts of worship; and acquiring the necessary amount of knowledge to establish religion, commanding good and forbidding evil, jihad, and contributing with wealth and work in everything on which the interests of the Islamic community depend, up to all other obligations and duties, all within capacity. Likewise, the pubescent becomes responsible for obeying the Sharia system that enforces internal security, avoiding aggression and personal revenge, submitting to rulers within the limits of their legitimate authority, and referring to them in settling disputes. Thus, upon puberty, a person becomes subject to the general Sharia penal system regarding any crimes and violations they commit.
2/63 - The Limit of Puberty:
Puberty is known and considered by the appearance of its natural signs (1). So when its signs appear, the ruling of puberty takes effect immediately upon their appearance without being tied to a specific age. But if its appearance is delayed, the person is considered legally pubescent when they reach the end of the usual age of puberty. For the age of puberty has a range between a beginning and an end; actual puberty occurs within those limits according to custom. Its beginning is twelve years for males and nine years for females, because this varies by region: in hot climates puberty comes early, and in cold climates it is delayed. So the lower natural limit was considered. As for its end, scholarly opinions differed: Abu Hanifa held that it is eighteen years for males and seventeen years for females, which is narrated from Ibn Abbas; but the majority of imams and the two companions (Sahibayn) of Abu Hanifa held that it is fifteen years for both males and females, and this is the preponderant view that is given fatwa upon in the Hanafi school, and upon it is the Majalla. The person between the beginning and end of the age of puberty is called a marahiq (adolescent). And if the adolescent claims or admits puberty while his bodily growth is plausible (it is possible). (1) In a boy it is ejaculation, and in a girl it is menstruation or pregnancy.
Source pages p. 766

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm explains the fourth stage of legal capacity (ahliyyah): the stage of puberty (bulugh). It opens by describing the significance of puberty as the transitional phase from childhood to adulthood, where one becomes fully mukallaf (legally responsible). The author stresses that puberty is a natural stage where both physical and mental development reach a sufficient threshold to bear the duties of the Sharia. While individuals vary in intelligence and talent beyond that threshold, all are equally capable of understanding obligations once the minimum is met. The passage then notes the unanimous agreement (ijma) of Sharia jurists that upon puberty, a person becomes subject to the address of the Lawgiver (khitab al-taklif). This includes all the obligations of faith, worship, social duties, and submission to the legal system, including penal consequences.

Section 2/63 discusses the legal limit (hadd) of puberty. The primary criterion is the appearance of natural signs: for a male, ejaculation (inzal); for a female, menstruation (hayd) or pregnancy (haml). Once these signs appear, the person is considered pubescent regardless of age. However, if these signs are delayed, the person is deemed legally pubescent upon reaching the maximum age of puberty, which is determined by custom. The beginning of the typical age range is 12 for males and 9 for females, as this varies by climate. The end of the range is subject to ijtihad: Imam Abu Hanifa set it at 18 for males and 17 for females, based on a report from Ibn Abbas. However, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and the majority of imams hold that the maximum is 15 years for both genders. In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is 15, as adopted by the Ottoman Majalla. The person between the lower and upper age limit is called a marahiq (adolescent). The text adds that if an adolescent claims or admits to puberty and his physical development is plausible, his claim may be accepted—this last sentence seems incomplete but implies that such a claim is subject to verification.

Key Terms
البلوغ (al-bulugh): المرحلة التي يصل فيها الشخص إلى النضوج الجنسي والعقلي الكافي لتحمل التكاليف الشرعية
التكليف (al-taklif): خطاب الشارع المتعلق بفعل المكلف اقتضاء أو تخييراً
المراهق (al-murahiq): الشخص الذي بين بداية سن البلوغ ونهايته (12-18 أو 12-15)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school's relied-upon position (mufta bihi), what is the maximum age at which a person is considered legally pubescent if natural signs have not appeared?
A) 18 years for males, 17 for females
B) 15 years for both males and females
C) 15 years for males, 13 for females
D) 12 years for males, 9 for females

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, puberty is determined by natural signs; if absent, the person becomes legally pubescent at 15 years (mufta bihi).

Puberty as the Fourth Stage of Legal Capacity: Rules and the Mufta Bihi Age
Explaining the rules of puberty and the mufta bihi age.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the natural signs of puberty, identify the mufta bihi age for legal puberty in the Hanafi madhhab, and apply the rule to determine legal capacity for taklif.

Core Idea
Puberty is established by natural signs; age is a default—the mufta bihi is 15 for both genders.

Puberty (bulugh) is the fourth stage of legal capacity, marking the transition to full accountability under Shariah. This passage by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa clarifies how puberty is determined: primarily by natural signs, and secondarily by age when signs are absent. It also presents an important intra-Hanafi dispute over the end age of puberty and settles on the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the natural signs
Find the explicit signs for males and females mentioned in the passage. These are the primary indicators of actual puberty (bulugh fi'li).
For males: ejaculation (inzal). For females: menstruation (hayd) or pregnancy (haml).
2
Understand the age range
The passage states a beginning and an end for the usual age of puberty. Note why the beginning is 12 for males and 9 for females (regional variation), and that the end is disputed.
If signs appear within this range, the person is adult immediately upon appearance.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Identify the two positions on the end age: Abu Hanifa's view (18 males/17 females) and the Sahibayn's view (15 for both). Locate each position and its evidence as stated in the passage.
The Sahibayn's view is also the view of the majority of imams.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The passage explicitly declares which opinion is the preponderant and relied-upon for fatwa. State that age and confirm its source (the Majalla).
Mufta bihi: 15 years for both genders.
5
Connect to taklif
Explain why puberty, rather than a specific age, is the criterion for legal obligation. Relate it to the two capacities: physical (jismiyyah) and mental ('aqliyyah).
Mental capacity is presumed alongside physical growth; puberty is a proxy for ease.

مما ٣/٦٣ - يتضح تقدم أن المناط الأصلي للتكليف الشرعي ليس سناً معينة من عمر الإنسان، بل هو توافر النصاب الكافي من القدرتين : هو قدرة الجسم، وقدرة العقل .
بيد أن نصاب قدرة الجسم يتجلى محسوساً في الحالات العادية السليمة بالبلوغ فعلاً أو بالبلوغ حكماً واعتباراً في سنه المعتادة وهي الخامسة عشرة .

(1) وهي في الغلام الإنزال وفي الفتاة الحيض أو الحبل.
(1) يلحظ هنا أن الفقرة الثانية من المادة الثانية من نظام إدارة أموال الأيتام العثماني الذي لا يزال جانب من أحكامه نافذاً إلى اليوم في سورية نصت صراحة أن حكام الشرع ممنوعون من استماع دعوى البلوغ الفعلي ممن لم يكملوا الخامسة عشرة من العمرة. وبذلك أصبح إثبات البلوغ الفعلي قبل نهاية سن البلوغ المذكورة غير مقبول في الجنسين الذكور والإناث على السواء.
ثم جاء أخيراً قانون الأحوال الشخصية السوري الجديد الصادر لدينا في ١٩٥٣/٩/١٧ فاشترط في المادة /١٥ منه لأهلية الزواج البلوغ الفعلي في الجنسين. ولكنه في المادة / ١٨ منه نص على قبول القاضي دعوى البلوغ من المراهق الذي أكمل الخامسة عشرة، ومن المراهقة التي أكملت الثالثة عشرة لأجل الإذن لهما بالزواج إذا تبين للقاضي صدق دعواهما واحتمال جسميهما.

Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. And contribution with wealth and work in everything upon which the interests of Islamic society depend, up to the rest of obligations and duties, all of that according to ability. Likewise, the adult becomes responsible for obeying the Shariah system that imposes internal security, avoiding aggression and personal revenge, and submitting to the rulers within the limits of their legitimate authority and referring to them in resolving disputes. So by reaching puberty, a person becomes subject to the general Shariah penal system regarding the crimes and violations he commits... 2/63 - The Definition of Puberty: Puberty is known and considered by the appearance of its natural signs (1). When its signs appear, puberty takes its ruling immediately upon their appearance without being tied to a specific age. If its signs are delayed, a person is considered legally adult when he reaches the end of the usual age of puberty. For the age of puberty has a range between a beginning and an end, with actual puberty occurring between them according to custom. Its beginning is twelve years for males and nine years for females, because this varies by region: in hot environments, puberty is early, and in cold, it is delayed. So the lower natural limit is considered. As for its end, there was disagreement in ijtihad: Abu Hanifa held that it is eighteen years for males and seventeen years for females, which is narrated from Ibn Abbas. But the majority of imams and the two companions of Abu Hanifa held that it is fifteen years for both males and females, which is the preponderant and fatwa-giving opinion in the Hanafi madhhab, and upon it is the Majalla. The person between the beginning and end of puberty is called: an adolescent (muradiq). If the adolescent claims or admits puberty and his physical development is plausible, he is believed; otherwise not (see Majalla 985-989). 3/63 - It becomes clear from the above that the original criterion for Shariah obligation is not a specific age of a person's life, but rather the availability of sufficient capacity in two abilities: physical ability and mental ability. However, the level of physical ability is manifestly evident in normal healthy cases by actual puberty or by legal puberty at its customary age, which is fifteen. As for the level of mental ability, it is known only by its effects—the balance of a person's actions—but these effects are not as apparent as the sensory signs of puberty. If these effects themselves were made the criterion upon which obligation is tied, they would be an ambiguous and unregulated criterion. Since the natural apparent presumption of mental growth is like bodily growth according to custom, obligation is tied to it for facilitation and ease, as required by the wisdom of legislation. (1) In boys, it is ejaculation; in girls, menstruation or pregnancy. (1) It is noted here that the second paragraph of Article Two of the Ottoman Orphans' Property Administration System, part of whose provisions are still in force in Syria today, explicitly states that Shariah judges are prohibited from hearing a claim of actual puberty from those who have not completed fifteen years of age. Thus, proving actual puberty before the mentioned end of puberty age became unacceptable for both genders equally. Then finally, the new Syrian Personal Status Law issued on 17/9/1953 stipulated in Article 15 for marriage eligibility actual puberty in both genders, but in Article 18 it stipulated acceptance by the judge of a claim of puberty from an adolescent who has completed fifteen years, and from an adolescent girl who has completed thirteen years, for the purpose of granting them permission to marry if the judge deems their claim truthful and their bodies plausible. (2) Imam Hafiz al-Din al-Nasafi said in his commentary on his book al-Manar in Usul al-Fiqh, summarized: The essence of intellect is known by ocular evidence: that a person chooses what is more beneficial for him and knows the hidden consequences of what he does and abandons. Likewise, deficiency of intellect is known by the incoherence and imbalance of his actions. Human states vary in perfection of intellect, so the Shariah established the moderate state of a person upon reaching adulthood as an intelligent person in place of complete intellect in constructing obligatory address (i.e., obligation) upon him for facilitation and ease. It did not consider the possibility of complete intellect before puberty, nor the persistence of its deficiency after puberty, because that is speculative. And because the rules of legislation are that when an apparent sign is established in place of an inner description for facilitation, the ruling revolves with it in existence and non-existence.
Source pages p. 767

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical exposition on the concept of puberty (bulugh) as a determinant of full legal capacity. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by explaining that puberty is recognized by natural signs: ejaculation in boys and menstruation or pregnancy in girls. Once these signs appear, the person is considered legally adult immediately, regardless of age. If signs are delayed, a person is deemed legally adult upon reaching the end of the customary age range for puberty. The beginning of this range is set at 12 for males and 9 for females, reflecting regional variations. The end of puberty is disputed: Abu Hanifa set it at 18 for males and 17 for females, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the majority of imams set it at 15 for both. The latter is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi madhhab and is adopted in the Ottoman Majalla. A person between the beginning and end of puberty is called a 'muradiq' (adolescent). If an adolescent claims puberty, he is believed if his physical development is plausible; otherwise, he is not believed. The author then clarifies that the true basis for taklif (legal obligation) is not a specific age but the possession of sufficient physical and mental capacity. Physical capacity is evidenced by actual puberty or by reaching the customary age of 15 (legal puberty). Mental capacity is harder to gauge directly; since it normally develops alongside physical growth, the Shariah uses puberty as a presumptive indicator (zann) for convenience, as per the wisdom of legislation. The passage includes two footnotes: one detailing Syrian Ottoman-era and modern positive law regarding puberty claims, and another quoting Imam al-Nasafi's rational for using external signs as proxies for inner qualities.

Key Terms
بلوغ (bulūgh): الوصول إلى سن الرشد الشرعي بظهور علامات طبيعية أو ببلوغ سن معينة
مراهق (murāhiq): الشخص بين بداية سن البلوغ ونهايته (12-15 أو 9-15)
تكليف (taklīf): الخطاب الشرعي الموجب للأحكام على المكلف
أهلية (ahliyya): صلاحية الشخص لتوجيه الخطاب الشرعي عليه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi for legal puberty is 15 years for both genders, based on the Sahibayn's view, making physical signs the primary criterion and age the default.

The Basis of Legal Obligation: Puberty and Capacity
Legal-philosophical explanation of taklif's basis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi legal tradition uses puberty as an apparent sign (al-'alamah al-zahirah) for the hidden attribute of reason in establishing legal obligation (taklif), and identify the supporting legal maxim.

Core Idea
The sign is not the cause but a legal proxy; the ruling follows the sign as a facilitation.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa clarifies that taklif (legal obligation) is not tied to a chronological age but to the presence of physical and mental capacity. Since mental capacity is not directly observable, the law uses natural physical signs of puberty as a practical proxy. This is an application of a fundamental Hanafi maxim: the apparent sign stands in for the hidden attribute for ease of application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two capacities required for taklif
The passage states that the original basis (al-manat) for taklif is the availability of sufficient physical capacity (qudrat al-jism) and mental capacity (qudrat al-'aql).
These are not fixed ages but actual capacities.
2
Distinguish how each capacity is known
Physical capacity is known by actual puberty or by a legal presumption at the customary age of fifteen. Mental capacity is known only by its effects (balanced actions), which are not directly observable.
The difficulty of directly assessing mental capacity leads to the legal proxy.
3
Understand why the law uses puberty as a proxy
Since mental capacity is hidden and impractical to assess in every individual, the law uses the natural correlation between physical maturity and mental growth. The apparent sign (physical puberty) is substituted for the hidden attribute (reason) as a facilitation (taysir).
This is derived from the maxim quoted from al-Nasafi.
4
Extract the legal maxim
The passage concludes with the maxim: 'The apparent sign, when it is substituted for the hidden attribute as a facilitation, the ruling revolves with it in existence and non-existence.'
This is a wusta-level qawida governing legal capacity.
5
Consider the exception to the maxim
The maxim applies unless the hidden attribute (reason) is definitively absent. If there is clear evidence of mental deficiency (junun) despite the sign, the presumption is rebutted.
This exception is governed by the principle 'certainty is not removed by doubt'.

Chapter (63) The Fourth Stage of Capacity: The Stage of Puberty. He is believed, otherwise not. (R. al-Majalla / 985-989). It becomes clear from what preceded that the original basis for legal obligation (taklif) is not a specific age of a person, but rather the availability of sufficient capacity of two abilities: physical ability and mental ability. Fifteen. But the physical ability is manifested sensibly in normal healthy conditions by actual puberty or by puberty by legal fiction (hukman) and consideration at its customary age. As for the mental ability, it is known only by its effects, such as the balance of a person's actions. But these effects are not like the physical signs of puberty in their appearance. If they were made the basis upon which obligation is linked, they would be a dubious and unregulated basis. And since the natural apparent presumption for the growth of the mind is like the growth of the body, therefore obligation was linked to it for facilitation and ease, as required by the wisdom of legislation. Puberty is the customary. (1) It is noted here that the second paragraph of the second article of the Ottoman orphans' fund administration system, some of whose provisions are still in force today in Syria, explicitly stated that Shari'a judges are prohibited from hearing a claim of actual puberty from those who have not completed fifteen years of age. Thus, proving actual puberty before the end of the mentioned age of puberty is not accepted for both males and females equally. Then recently the new Syrian Personal Status Law issued in 1953/9/17 stipulated in Article 15 the requirement of actual puberty for marriage eligibility in both sexes. But in Article 18 it stipulated the acceptance by the judge of a claim of puberty from a boy who has completed fifteen years and a girl who has completed thirteen years for the purpose of permitting them to marry if the judge finds their claim truthful and their bodies capable. (2) Imam Hafiz al-Din al-Nasafi said in his commentary on his book al-Manar on usul al-fiqh, summarizing: The origin of reason ('aql) is known by direct observation, such that a person chooses what is more beneficial for himself and knows the hidden consequences of what he does and what he leaves. Likewise, deficiency of his reason is known by the lack of coherence and balance in his actions. The conditions of people vary in perfection of reason. So the Law established the moderate state of a person upon his reaching puberty as a rational person as representing complete reason in constructing the binding address (i.e., obligation upon him) for facilitation and ease. And it did not consider the possibility of complete reason before puberty, nor the persistence of deficiency after puberty, because the rules of legislation are that the apparent sign, whenever it is substituted for the hidden attribute, the ruling revolves with it in existence and non-existence as a facilitation.
Source pages p. 768

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical explanation of the basis of taklif (legal obligation) in Hanafi thought. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, clarifies that the manat (basis) for being held legally accountable is not a fixed chronological age but the attainment of sufficient physical and mental capacity. Physical capacity is determined by actual puberty or, as a legal presumption, by reaching the customary age of fifteen (lunar years). Mental capacity, however, is not directly observable; its presence is inferred from balanced actions. Since direct assessment of reason is impractical for every individual, the Law uses the natural correlation between physical maturity and mental growth to presume that a person who has reached puberty possesses sufficient reason for taklif. This is a facilitation (taysir) from the Shari'ah. The footnote quotes Hafiz al-Din al-Nasafi from his work al-Manar, who defines 'aql (reason) as the ability to choose what is beneficial and perceive consequences. The ruling revolves with the visible sign (puberty) in its presence and absence, an application of the legal maxim: 'The apparent sign, when substituted for the hidden attribute, the ruling revolves with it in existence and non-existence as a facilitation.'

Key Terms
المناط (al-manat): The basis or criterion for applying a legal ruling
التكليف (al-taklif): The imposition of legal duties on a person
العقل (al-'aql): The faculty of reason that allows a person to choose beneficial actions and understand outcomes

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Zarqa, why does the Shari'ah use puberty (al-bulugh) as the sign for taklif rather than directly assessing mental capacity?
A) Because mental capacity cannot be known at all
B) Because assessing mental capacity in every individual is impractical
C) Because physical signs always perfectly correlate with mental capacity
D) Because the age of fifteen is explicitly mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
The basis of taklif is physical and mental capacity; the law uses the apparent sign of puberty as a proxy for mental capacity for facilitation.

Rushd: Financial Prudence, Not Religious Piety – The Fifth Stage of Legal Capacity
Defines rushd as financial prudence, not religious piety, with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definition of rushd as financial insight, distinguish it from religious piety, and apply it to determine when a person may be restricted (hajr) from financial transactions.

Core Idea
Rushd is financial competence, not piety—a sinner may be legally mature if he manages wealth well.

This lesson explores the essential concept of rushd (maturity) as defined in Hanafi jurisprudence. While physical puberty (bulugh) grants basic legal capacity, full financial autonomy requires an additional quality: the ability to manage wealth prudently. The text clarifies that rushd is not about religious devotion but about worldly financial insight, supported by Quranic evidence and classical Hanafi authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning of rushd
Examine the Arabic root r-sh-d and its use in Quran 2:256 to understand the general meaning of guidance and rectitude.
Linguistic meaning provides the foundation, but the legal meaning is more specific.
2
Distinguish rushd from religious piety
Note the explicit statement: rushd is not wara' (piety) or taqwa (God-consciousness), but rather financial insight.
This is the key conceptual shift.
3
Define the legal meaning: financial insight
Understand that rushd in financial matters is al-basirah al-maliyyah – the practical ability to manage money properly from a worldly perspective.
Even a sinner can possess this quality.
4
Trace the evidence: Quranic verse and scholarly transmission
Analyze how jurists derive the legal definition from the Quranic verse and the interpretation attributed to Ibn Abbas.
The dalil is the Quran, but the tafsir by Ibn Abbas shapes the Hanafi stance.
5
Apply the definition to legal practice
Understand that a judge may place a person under hajr (financial restriction) if he lacks rushd, even after puberty, and conversely, a financially competent person is not restricted.
This is the practical consequence for fatwa.

Chapter Sixty-Four: The Fifth Stage of Capacity: The Stage of Maturity (Rushd). 1/64 - Indeed, a person's becoming legally obligated (mukallaf) through puberty does not mean, in the sense that preceded, the perfection of his civil capacity (ahliyyat al-ada') and the enforcement of his financial transactions. Rather, the perfection of this capacity in a person depends on another quality above puberty, which is the quality of rushd. Rushd and rashaad linguistically mean rectitude and guidance to correct actions, and the adjective is rashid and rashid, and it is the opposite of error (ghayy) and misguidance (dalal), as in the Almighty's saying: {There is no compulsion in religion; indeed, the right path has become distinct from error} (1). (1) Reference: al-Misbah by al-Fayyumi, and Mufradat al-Qur'an by al-Raghib al-Isfahani, under the root r-sh-d. And the reality of rushd is the attainment of reason, for physical attainment does not necessitate it (see: Kitab al-Ahliyyah wa al-Majalis al-Hisbiyyah by lawyer Muhammad Hashim al-Muhanna). And rushd according to the jurists in this context - i.e., in financial matters - is not piety (wara') and God-consciousness (taqwa), but rather financial insight by which a person is good at managing money from a worldly perspective, even if he is a sinner from a religious perspective. And this is the interpretation transmitted on this subject from Ibn Abbas (2). (2) Reference: al-Majalla 946, 947, 963; al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar in the Book of Hajr, 5/95; and in the Book of Waqf, 3/430; and Tanqih al-Fatawa al-Hamidiyya, 1/198, quoting from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq by Ibn Nujaym.
Source pages p. 769

Commentary
This passage describes the fifth stage of legal capacity: rushd (maturity/ sound judgment). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that attaining puberty (bulugh) does not automatically grant full 'civil capacity' for financial matters. A person may be physically mature but still lack the judgment to manage his wealth wisely. Thus, Shari'ah requires an additional quality: rushd. Linguistically, rushd means rectitude and guidance to correct action, as in Quran 2:256. The author emphasizes that in this legal context, rushd is not about religious piety (wara' or taqwa) but rather 'financial insight' (al-basirah al-maliyyah) – the practical ability to handle money well. A person can be a sinner yet still be considered rashid (mature) if he manages his finances prudently. This interpretation is attributed to Ibn Abbas (RA). The footnote cites classical Hanafi sources: al-Majalla, al-Durr al-Mukhtar, Radd al-Muhtar, and Tanqih al-Fatawa al-Hamidiyya, affirming that this definition is standard in the Hanafi school. The ruling implies that a judge may restrict a person's financial autonomy (hajr) if he lacks rushd, even after puberty.

Key Terms
الرشد (al-rushd): Financial prudence and sound judgment in managing one's wealth
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-ada'): Capacity for execution; the legal ability to perform actions with legal consequences
الحجر (al-hajr): Legal restriction on a person's financial autonomy due to lack of rushd

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Rushd in Hanafi law is financial prudence, not piety; a person may be a sinner but legally mature if he manages wealth wisely.

The Stage of Rushd: Defining Financial Maturity
defining rushd and its legal implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of rushd (financial maturity) and identify its role in achieving full civil capacity (ahliyyah).

Core Idea
Rushd is financial prudence, not religious piety; without it, even a mature adult may be interdicted.

Puberty (bulugh) makes a person legally responsible, but full capacity to manage wealth requires an additional quality: rushd. This lesson unpacks the juristic definition of rushd, its distinction from religious piety, its opposite (safah), and the practical consequence of interdiction for those lacking it.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic from juristic meaning
Identify how the author moves from the general Arabic meaning of rushd (guidance, righteousness) to the specific fiqh definition (financial prudence).
The Quranic verse (2:256) is used for linguistic context only.
2
Locate the Quranic proof
Recognize the cited verse (Quran 2:256) as a textual basis for the linguistic meaning, not as direct evidence for the fiqh ruling.
This is an example of dalil naqli for definition.
3
Contrast rushd with safah
Understand that safah (profligacy) includes wasteful spending even on good causes, and triggers interdiction (hajr).
The author provides examples: spending on wrongdoers vs. building an unnecessary mosque.
4
Determine the condition for full capacity
Rushd depends on experience and control of reason over desire; it may be delayed after puberty.
Full civil capacity is contingent on proving rushd, not just reaching puberty.
5
Apply the legal consequence
If rushd is absent, the person remains under guardianship; once proven, guardianship ends and the person gains free disposal of wealth.
This operationalizes the definition.

الباب الثامن : نظرية الأهلية والولاية
ويقابله السَّفَه (بفتحتين)، وهو تبذير المال وإتلافه في غير حكمة، سواء أكان في أمور الشر كجمع الفساق والإنفاق عليهم، أو في أمور الخير كما لو صَرَفَ الشخص جميع ماله في بناء مسجد، في بناء مسجد في غير حاجة عامة، فإنه يعتبر سفيهاً في نظر فقهاء الشريعة ويستحق الحجر.
فتلك البصيرة المالية تتوقف على قدر من التجربة والممارسة العملية وسيطرة النهى على الهوى فوق أصل النصاب العقلي اللازم لإدراك الخير والشر والنفع والضر بالقدر الكافي للتكليف. فقد يكون الإنسان على ملكة عقلية كافية لمخاطبته بالتكليف الشرعي ومسؤوليته، ولا يكون رشيداً في إدارة المال لجهله بذلك، أو لغلبة هواه على عقله ومعرفته، وذلك كالرجل السفيه المبذر، فإنه مكلف شرعاً ومسؤول عن واجبات التكليف، وعن الجرائم، ولكنه يُحجر عن التصرُّف بأمواله لعدم رشده، ويقام عليه وصي يتصرف عنه كما يقام على القاصرين.
فهذا الرشد المالي قد يرافق البلوغ، وقد يتأخر عنه. وذلك بحسب فطرة الشخص وما يكون له من سابقة تمرين وخبرة في شؤون المال وأحوال الناس.
وبهذا الرشد لا بمجرد البلوغ يُناط كمال الأهلية المدنية الشخص، وما يَسْتَتبعه هذا الكمال من إطلاق حرّية التصرف له وتسليم أمواله إليه.

The sixty-fourth chapter: The fifth stage of capacity: the stage of rushd. 1/64 - Indeed, a person becoming legally responsible upon puberty, in the sense that his full civil capacity of execution and the validity of his financial transactions are [presupposed], but the completion of this capacity in an individual depends on another quality above puberty, which is the quality of rushd. And rushd, and rishad, in language mean righteousness and guidance to correct actions; the adjective is rashid and rashid, and it is the opposite of ghayy and dalal, as in His saying, Exalted: {There is no compulsion in religion; indeed, right conduct has become clear from error.} (1). The reality of rushd is the attainment of intellect, for attainment of the body does not necessitate it. And rushd, according to the jurists in this context – i.e., in financial matters – is not piety and righteousness, but rather financial insight by which a person is good at managing wealth from a worldly perspective, even if he is a sinner from a religious perspective. This is the interpretation transmitted on this subject from Ibn Abbas. The eighth section: The theory of capacity and guardianship. Its opposite is safah (with two fatha signs), which is squandering money and destroying it without wisdom, whether in evil matters like gathering wrongdoers and spending on them, or in good matters like when a person spends all his wealth on building a mosque, building a mosque when there is no general need, then he is considered a prodigal in the view of the jurists of the Sharia and deserves interdiction. That financial insight depends on a degree of experience and practical practice, and the dominance of reason over desire, above the basic mental capacity necessary to perceive good and evil, benefit and harm, to the extent sufficient for legal responsibility. A person may have sufficient mental faculty to be addressed by legal responsibility and to be accountable, but he is not mature in managing money due to ignorance of that, or because his desire overcomes his intellect and knowledge, such as a prodigal squanderer. He is legally responsible and accountable for the obligations of responsibility, and for crimes, but he is interdicted from disposing of his wealth due to his lack of rushd, and a guardian is appointed over him to act for him just as is appointed over minors. This financial maturity may coincide with puberty, or may be delayed after it. That depends on the individual's innate nature and his prior training and experience in financial matters and people's affairs. And it is by this rushd, not merely by puberty, that the completion of a person's civil capacity is contingent, and what follows from this completion of granting him freedom of disposition and handing over his wealth to him. And based on that, it is obligatory by Sharia, when a person reaches puberty and becomes legally responsible for the general rulings, to examine his rushd. If he proves that he has reached puberty while being mature, then he is considered from the time of puberty to have full capacities in all respects, and he is freed from guardianship or curatorship.
Source pages p. 769

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth stage of legal capacity: the stage of rushd (financial maturity). The author begins by explaining that while puberty (bulugh) confers legal responsibility (taklif) and basic civil capacity, full capacity to manage one's own financial affairs requires an additional quality: rushd. Linguistically, rushd means guidance and righteousness, but juristically it is specifically about financial prudence, not religious piety. The Quranic verse (2:256) is cited to illustrate the linguistic meaning. The opposite of rushd is safah (profligacy), which includes wasteful spending even on seemingly good causes like building an unnecessary mosque. Such a person is considered a safih and deserves interdiction (hajr). The passage emphasizes that rushd involves practical experience and control of desire over intellect, and may not be present at puberty. If a person reaches puberty but lacks rushd, he remains under guardianship. Once rushd is established, full civil capacity is granted and guardianship ends. This is a foundational concept in Hanafi fiqh for issues of financial transactions and guardianship over property.

Key Terms
الرشد (al-rushd): في هذا النص: البصيرة المالية وحسن التصرف بالمال من الوجهة الدنيوية، وليس التقوى الدينية.
السفه (al-safah): تبذير المال وإتلافه في غير حكمة، سواء في الشر أو الخير غير المحتاج إليه.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following actions would be considered safah (profligacy)?
A) Spending all one's wealth on a hospital for the poor.
B) Spending all one's wealth on a non-essential mosque in an area with many mosques.
C) Donating a portion of one's wealth to a reliable charity monthly.
D) Investing in a halal business after consulting experts.

Key Takeaway
Rushd is a distinct quality from puberty; full civil capacity only arises when both bulugh and rushd are present.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Rashd (Financial Prudence) as a Requirement for Full Legal Capacity
Defining safah/rashd and their legal effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define safah and rashd according to the Hanafi school and explain why rashd is a separate condition from puberty for complete financial legal capacity.

Core Idea
Safah is wasting wealth unwisely; rashd is financial maturity beyond mere legal age.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm clarifies that reaching puberty does not automatically grant a person full control over their wealth. The Sharia requires a separate condition: financial prudence (rushd). The text defines its opposite, safah, with a striking example: spending all one's wealth on building a mosque without public need. Understanding this distinction is essential for anyone dealing with guardianship and interdiction in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Define the key terms
Locate the definitions of safah and rashd in the Arabic passage. Note that safah includes both evil and good spending without wisdom. Rashd is described as 'financial insight' requiring experience and self-control.
Safah is the opposite of rashd; both relate to financial management, not mental capacity.
2
Identify the legal consequences
From the text, determine what legal effect safah has (hajr/interdiction) and what rashd enables (full capacity, end of guardianship). The passage states that a safih is interdicted from his wealth, while a rashid gains full disposal rights.
The consequence of safah is hajr on wealth only, not on the person's other legal capacity (e.g., criminal responsibility).
3
Locate the Quranic evidence
Find the verse cited: Surah an-Nisa 4:6, which conditions handing over orphans' wealth upon the appearance of rushd. This is the primary textual basis for requiring rashd, not just puberty.
The verse says 'And if you perceive in them right conduct (rushd), then hand over their property to them.'
4
Understand the separation of rashd from puberty
The text explicitly states that rashd may coincide with puberty or be delayed. It is not automatic. A person can be mukallaf (legally responsible for religious duties) but still not rashid in finances.
This separation is a key Hanafi position: mental capacity for taklif does not guarantee financial prudence.
5
Explain the purpose of interdiction
The passage argues that preventing access to wealth would be futile if the person's transactions over that wealth remained valid. Therefore, financial guardianship must block all dispositions. This is a teleological reasoning (maqasid).
The purpose is to protect the wealth from mismanagement until the person demonstrates rashd.

المطبعة الرحمانية
۸۲۱
Chapter Eight: The Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. Its opposite is safah (with two fatha), which is wastefulness of wealth and squandering it without wisdom, whether in evil matters, like gathering immoral people and spending on them, or in good matters, as if a person spends all his money building a mosque where there is no public need, then he is considered a safih in the view of jurists of the Sharia and deserves to be interdicted. That financial insight depends on a degree of experience and practical practice, and the control of reason over desire, beyond the basic intellectual capacity necessary for perceiving good and evil and benefit and harm to the extent sufficient for taklif. A person may have sufficient mental faculty to be addressed by the legal obligation and responsibility, yet not be prudent in managing wealth due to ignorance of that, or due to his desire overcoming his intellect and knowledge, such as the foolish spendthrift man, for he is legally obligated and responsible for duties of taklif and for crimes, but he is interdicted from disposing of his wealth due to his lack of rashd, and a guardian is appointed over him to act on his behalf just as is done for minors. This financial rashd may coincide with puberty, or may be delayed after it, depending on the person's nature and any prior training and experience he has in financial matters and people's affairs. It is based on this rashd, not merely puberty, that a person's complete civil capacity is tied, and what follows from this completeness of giving him freedom of disposition and handing over his wealth to him. And accordingly, it is legally obligatory, when a person reaches puberty and becomes obligated by the general rulings, to examine his rashd. If it is established that he has reached puberty with rashd, then from puberty he is complete in all capacities, and he is freed from guardianship or tutelage, and his transactions and acknowledgments are effective, and his wealth is handed over to him. But if his rashd is not established upon his puberty, then he remains deficient in capacity of performance, and his wealth is withheld from him, and financial guardianship over him continues until his rashd is established. The basis of this is that the Quranic text previously cited conditions the handing over of orphans' wealth to them upon the appearance of this rashd, and is not satisfied with mere puberty. And there is no doubt that the impermissibility of handing over a person's wealth to him before the appearance of his financial rashd means the continuation of financial guardianship over him and the non-effectiveness of his dispositions before that, for there is no benefit in preventing his wealth from him if his contracts and acknowledgments concerning it are effective, for then his wealth would be wasted by his words even if not reaching his hand, so the Legislator's purpose of protecting him from his mismanagement would not be realized. And it is established in the science of Usul al-Fiqh that whatever is necessary for fulfilling an obligation is itself obligatory. So a legislative command for something is also a command for what it necessitates and without which it is not completed. For example, the command for wudu for prayer is a command to go to the place of water. And the imposition of legal obligations that require physical strength, such as acts of worship and jihad, is an imposition of sufficient eating and drinking to preserve the ability to perform these obligations. Likewise, the imposition of returning a usurped item is an imposition of bearing the cost of return if it requires expense, and so on.
Source pages p. 770

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm defines the key concepts of safah (foolishness) and rushd (prudence) and explains their role in legal capacity and guardianship. The author begins by defining safah as wasting wealth without wisdom, whether in forbidden activities or even in good deeds like building a mosque without genuine need—a striking example showing that fiqh's concern is wise management, not merely religious intent. Such a person is legally considered safih and subject to interdiction (hajr). He then clarifies that financial prudence requires experiential knowledge and self-control beyond the basic mental capacity for taklif. Thus a person can be fully responsible for religious duties and crimes but still lacking in financial maturity, requiring a guardian. The text stresses that rashd may coincide with puberty or be delayed, and it is the basis for complete civil capacity, not puberty alone. Legally, upon puberty a person's rashd must be examined; if present, he gains full capacity and his transactions are effective; if absent, financial guardianship continues until rashd is proven. The Quranic verse (4:6) is cited as the textual basis for requiring rashd before handing over property. The author then deduces that preventing access to wealth would be useless if his transactions over it were still valid, so guardians must block transactions as well. Finally, he invokes the usul principle that what is necessary for an obligation is itself obligatory, applying it to show that commands for prayer imply commands for what enables it (e.g., seeking water), and similarly for jihad and redressing wrongs. This principle underpins the obligation to ensure rashd before handing over property.

Key Terms
السفه (al-safah): تبذير المال وإتلافه في غير حكمة، سواء في خير أو شر، مما يستوجب الحجر
الرشد (al-rushd): بصيرة مالية تتوقف على التجربة وسيطرة النهى على الهوى، وهي شرط لكمال أهلية الأداء المالي
الحجر (al-hajr): المنع من التصرف في المال بسبب السفه أو الصغر أو الجنون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for handing over an orphan's property to him upon reaching puberty?
A) Mere puberty
B) Puberty and being a male
C) Puberty and appearance of rashd (financial prudence)
D) Puberty and having a guardian

Key Takeaway
Financial capacity requires rushd, a distinct condition from puberty, grounded in Quran (4:6) and the usul principle 'ma la yatimmu al-wajib'.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
advanced

The Legal Maxim 'What is Necessary for an Obligation is Obligatory' in Financial Guardianship
Applying the usul maxim to financial guardianship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim 'ma la yatimmu al-wajib' to explain why financial guardianship is obligatory until rashd is proven.

Core Idea
The obligation to protect wealth necessarily implies preventing its waste through guardianship.

This passage uses a fundamental usul principle to support the continuation of financial guardianship: whatever is necessary for fulfilling an obligation is itself obligatory. The author applies this maxim to show that since the Shari'a commands protecting the wealth of the safih (which is wajib), the necessary means to achieve that—namely, preventing him from transacting—is also wajib. Understanding this maxim is key to deriving many fiqh rulings across ibadat, mu'amalat, and jinayat.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim in Arabic and English
From the passage, identify the maxim: 'Ma la yatimmu al-wajib illa bihi fa huwa wajib.' Write it down and explain it in simple terms.
This maxim is a legal principle, not a mere logical statement. It creates a legal obligation for the means.
2
Identify the primary obligation in this context
The text implicitly refers to the obligation to protect the wealth of the safih from waste. This is derived from the Quranic command to hand over property only after rashd, which implies a duty to safeguard it beforehand.
The primary obligation is hifz al-mal (protection of wealth), which is a maqasid goal.
3
Apply the maxim to the means
The necessary means to protect wealth is to prevent the safih from transacting. Therefore, the prohibition of his transactions becomes obligatory. The passage explicitly states that allowing his contracts would defeat the purpose of withholding his wealth.
This is the direct application: the maxim turns the means into an obligation.
4
Analyze the furuat given in the text
The author provides three additional furuat: (1) command to pray implies command to go to water; (2) obligation of jihad and worship implies obligation to eat and drink sufficiently; (3) obligation to return usurped property implies obligation to bear the cost of return. Identify the common structure.
Each follows the same pattern: a known wajib is shown to require a specific means, and that means becomes wajib.
5
Reflect on the scope of the maxim
Consider the scope: it applies in all chapters of fiqh. Note that there are exceptions (istithna'at) where the means is not obligatory due to hardship or other overriding considerations. The passage does not mention exceptions, but the related maxim 'al-mashaqqa tajlib al-taysir' may limit it.
The maxim is a kulliya (universal) but may be restricted by other maxims.

Source Text
