ممنوعة عنه، وتستمر عليه الولاية من جهة المال(۱) حتى يثبت رشده . وأصل ذلك هو أن النص القرآني الذي سَلَفَ بيانه(٢) يعلق تسليم
أموال اليتامى إليهم بظهور هذا الرشد ، ولا يكتفي بمجرد البلوغ. ٢/٦٤ - ولا شك أنَّ عدم جواز تسليم مال الشخص إليه قبل ظهور رشده المالي معناه بقاء الولاية المالية عليه وعدم نفاذ تصرفاته قبل ذلك، إذ لا فائدة لمنع أمواله عنه إذا كانت عقوده وإقراراته عليها نافذة، إذ يضيع عندئذ أمواله بأقواله وإن لم تصل إليها يده فلا يتحقق غرض الشارع في حمايته من سوء تصرفه (ر: م/ ۹۸۲).
ومن المقرر في علم أصول الفقه أنَّ ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو واجب (۳). فالأمر التشريعي بشيء يعتبر أمراً أيضاً بما يستلزمه ذلك الشيء ولا يتم إلا به فالأمر بالوضوء للصلاة مثلاً هو أمر بالسعي إلى مكان الماء. وإيجاب الواجبات الشرعية التي تحتاج إلى قوة البدن، كالعبادات والجهاد، هو إيجاب للأكل والشرب الكافيين لحفظ القدرة على أداء هذه الواجبات. وكذا إيجاب رد المغصوب هو إيجاب لتحمل نفقة الرد إذا كان محتاجاً إلى نفقة، وهلم جراً...
(1) أما الولاية عليه من جهة النفس - وهي تتضمن سلطة التزويج الجبري والتأديب والتطبيب الجراحي، كما سيأتي في الفصل / ٦٦/ فإنها تنقطع عن الشخص بمجرد بلوغه عاقلاً وصيرورته مكلفاً. (۲) هو قوله تعالى في سورة النساء (٥/٤) : ( واتلوا البنى حتى إذا بلغوا النكاح فإن وانستم منهم رُشدًا فَادْفَعُوا إِلَيْهِمْ أَمْوَاله ). (۳) ر : أصول الفقه للخضري ص ٥٤ - ٥٦ ، والموافقات للشاطبي ٣٩٤/٢ طبعة
المطبعة الرحمانية
۸۲۱
Chapter Eight: The Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. Its opposite is safah (with two fatha), which is wastefulness of wealth and squandering it without wisdom, whether in evil matters, like gathering immoral people and spending on them, or in good matters, as if a person spends all his money building a mosque where there is no public need, then he is considered a safih in the view of jurists of the Sharia and deserves to be interdicted. That financial insight depends on a degree of experience and practical practice, and the control of reason over desire, beyond the basic intellectual capacity necessary for perceiving good and evil and benefit and harm to the extent sufficient for taklif. A person may have sufficient mental faculty to be addressed by the legal obligation and responsibility, yet not be prudent in managing wealth due to ignorance of that, or due to his desire overcoming his intellect and knowledge, such as the foolish spendthrift man, for he is legally obligated and responsible for duties of taklif and for crimes, but he is interdicted from disposing of his wealth due to his lack of rashd, and a guardian is appointed over him to act on his behalf just as is done for minors. This financial rashd may coincide with puberty, or may be delayed after it, depending on the person's nature and any prior training and experience he has in financial matters and people's affairs. It is based on this rashd, not merely puberty, that a person's complete civil capacity is tied, and what follows from this completeness of giving him freedom of disposition and handing over his wealth to him. And accordingly, it is legally obligatory, when a person reaches puberty and becomes obligated by the general rulings, to examine his rashd. If it is established that he has reached puberty with rashd, then from puberty he is complete in all capacities, and he is freed from guardianship or tutelage, and his transactions and acknowledgments are effective, and his wealth is handed over to him. But if his rashd is not established upon his puberty, then he remains deficient in capacity of performance, and his wealth is withheld from him, and financial guardianship over him continues until his rashd is established. The basis of this is that the Quranic text previously cited conditions the handing over of orphans' wealth to them upon the appearance of this rashd, and is not satisfied with mere puberty. And there is no doubt that the impermissibility of handing over a person's wealth to him before the appearance of his financial rashd means the continuation of financial guardianship over him and the non-effectiveness of his dispositions before that, for there is no benefit in preventing his wealth from him if his contracts and acknowledgments concerning it are effective, for then his wealth would be wasted by his words even if not reaching his hand, so the Legislator's purpose of protecting him from his mismanagement would not be realized. And it is established in the science of Usul al-Fiqh that whatever is necessary for fulfilling an obligation is itself obligatory. So a legislative command for something is also a command for what it necessitates and without which it is not completed. For example, the command for wudu for prayer is a command to go to the place of water. And the imposition of legal obligations that require physical strength, such as acts of worship and jihad, is an imposition of sufficient eating and drinking to preserve the ability to perform these obligations. Likewise, the imposition of returning a usurped item is an imposition of bearing the cost of return if it requires expense, and so on.
Source pages p. 770

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm defines the key concepts of safah (foolishness) and rushd (prudence) and explains their role in legal capacity and guardianship. The author begins by defining safah as wasting wealth without wisdom, whether in forbidden activities or even in good deeds like building a mosque without genuine need—a striking example showing that fiqh's concern is wise management, not merely religious intent. Such a person is legally considered safih and subject to interdiction (hajr). He then clarifies that financial prudence requires experiential knowledge and self-control beyond the basic mental capacity for taklif. Thus a person can be fully responsible for religious duties and crimes but still lacking in financial maturity, requiring a guardian. The text stresses that rashd may coincide with puberty or be delayed, and it is the basis for complete civil capacity, not puberty alone. Legally, upon puberty a person's rashd must be examined; if present, he gains full capacity and his transactions are effective; if absent, financial guardianship continues until rashd is proven. The Quranic verse (4:6) is cited as the textual basis for requiring rashd before handing over property. The author then deduces that preventing access to wealth would be useless if his transactions over it were still valid, so guardians must block transactions as well. Finally, he invokes the usul principle that what is necessary for an obligation is itself obligatory, applying it to show that commands for prayer imply commands for what enables it (e.g., seeking water), and similarly for jihad and redressing wrongs. This principle underpins the obligation to ensure rashd before handing over property.

Key Terms
ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو واجب (ma la yatimmu al-wajib illa bihi fa huwa wajib): مبدأ أصولي: إذا توقف فعل واجب على شيء آخر، فإن ذلك الشيء يصبح واجباً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on bearing the cost of returning usurped property?
A) It is recommended but not required.
B) It is obligatory if returning requires expense.
C) It is only obligatory if the usurper agreed to pay.
D) It is not the responsibility of the usurper.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'ma la yatimmu al-wajib illa bihi' is a powerful tool for deriving obligations necessary to fulfill explicit duties, and it underpins the Hanafi ruling that financial guardianship must block the safih's transactions.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Fifth Stage of Legal Capacity: The Concept of Rushd (Maturity)
Juristic exposition on rushd and its implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define rushd in Hanafi law, distinguish it from bulugh, and apply the ruling that rushd is required for full financial capacity.

Core Idea
Rushd is practical financial wisdom separate from physical maturity; without it, even an adult is interdicted.

This passage from al-Zarqa's juristic introduction explores the fifth stage of legal capacity – the stage of rushd. It clarifies a crucial distinction: reaching puberty (bulugh) grants legal responsibility (taklif) for worship, but full capacity to manage property requires an additional quality – practical experience in preserving wealth. You will learn why Islamic law deliberately avoids fixing a universal age for rushd, and why the view of the two disciples (Sahibayn) prevails in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish taklif from capacity for disposition
Identify the two types of legal address: one based on physical maturity (taklif) for acts of worship, and another based on financial prudence (rushd) for property transactions.
Taklif triggers at bulugh; rushd is needed for complete disposal of wealth.
2
Define rushd as a legal term
Break down the definition: rushd is not intelligence alone but practical experience in managing and preserving money. Identify its conditions and effects.
Use the legal definition dissector to see the components.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on fixing an age
Compare Abu Hanifa's view (maximum age 25 unless rushd appears earlier) with the Sahibayn's view (no fixed age, rushd must be verified). Identify the Quranic proof for the latter.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Assess the tarjih for fatwa
Determine which view is mufta bihi. Note that later Hanafi authorities prefer the Sahibayn's view because it aligns with the Quranic text and is more flexible.
The fatwa hierarchy will show the operative ruling.

(1) كما في تحديد سن البلوغ، إذ يعتبر من يتم الخامسة عشرة بالغاً حكماً دون حاجة إلى إثبات بلوغه فعلاً (ر: ف ٢/٦٣). ۸۲۳
Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship: Scholars have stated: From this fundamental principle it becomes clear that the legal prohibition from handing over property to its owner before ascertaining his maturity (rushd) is considered a prohibition from disposition; otherwise, there would be no benefit in preventing him from the property, but rather it would be futile. And it is established that there is no futility in legislation (Al-Muwafaqat by al-Shatibi 158/1, 3/64). This being so, one must be alert in this context that one may see juristic texts stating that when a person reaches puberty with sanity, guardianship over him ceases, his capacity is complete, and his disposition and acknowledgment are valid (1). But these juristic texts and the like (1) are interpreted as applicable when the person reaches puberty as a mature person (rashid). Their unconditioned expression in some places is only reliance on the detailed and qualified ruling established in other places: - 984-981 of the Majalla and 989. (1) (2) For example, what we previously quoted in the footnote under paragraph 7/62 from the book Jami' Ahkam al-Sighar by al-Asrushni, that the discerning minor's will is not valid unless he ratifies it after reaching puberty; the intended meaning is: 'after reaching puberty as a mature person (rashid).' (2) The verified scholar Shaykh Ahmad Ibrahim Bey said in his treatise 'Al-Ahliyyah' p. 17-18, verbatim: 'The jurists have agreed to make the basis of legal responsibility (taklif) puberty, whether by age or signs; with it, guardianship over both person and property ends, and the legal address becomes fully directed. But some states in the Islamic world, like the Egyptian state, observed that the interest lies in not hastening to give the minor his property merely upon his legal puberty, but rather waiting until he reaches his full strength (ashudd); likewise, they raised the age of discernment according to what they perceived as requirements of interests. I say: This is based on not differentiating between legal responsibility (taklif) and capacity for disposition, due to the apparent implication of some unqualified juristic texts from some places as we alerted, and this is contrary to the reality that becomes clear from deep consideration of jurists' statements. And the claim of agreement among jurists on that is not conceded; the same professor himself reported in the footnote the view of Abu Hanifa on waiting for the age of maturity up to twenty-five for handing over property, and the view of the majority of jurists along with the two companions of Abu Hanifa on waiting for maturity regardless of the age of the non-mature person, as we will soon see. This is what the Quranic text indicates, which has been explained earlier and on which practice is based. So the laws adopted by those Islamic states are based on these explicit juristic ijtihads. 822. Section (64): The Fifth Stage of Legal Capacity: The Stage of Maturity (Rushd). 4/64 - Fixing an Age for Maturity: Its Meaning and Benefit. The meaning of fixing an age for maturity is to set a limit in a person's age at which the natural interdiction imposed by law as a ruling on growing minors ends. This fixing has two benefits: 1 - That at that point the person will not be accepted to prove his maturity before this limit even if he is actually pubescent, or legally by age. 2 - That the person who reaches the fixed age of maturity, and is of sound mind (i.e., not insane or mentally deficient), is then considered fully capable of disposition legally, so the legal interdiction is removed from him and he is freed from the guardianship that was over him without needing to prove his maturity. This is the concept of fixing an age for maturity and the benefit of this fix in rulings. 5/64 - Does maturity have a fixed age in Islamic law? Islamic law - which is a law that came to have its principles eternal and applicable in every time and place - did not fix an age for maturity, because the time of maturity varies according to a person's natural disposition, and also varies according to differences in environment, general social and economic conditions, and according to knowledge, education, and general morals. What dispels the confusion is what we have proceeded on regarding the establishment of the separation between legal responsibility (taklif) and civil capacity for performance, i.e., capacity for financial disposition. Legal responsibility is established at puberty because it relies on a measure of physical ability and natural comprehension sufficient to understand the legal address and distinguish between good and evil. As for capacity for financial disposition, it relies on practical experience in managing and preserving property beyond those natural faculties. Hence it depends on maturity (rushd) in its juristic meaning that we have explained. And thereby the issue becomes clear, the texts become consistent, and every objection is repelled. (1) As in fixing the age of puberty: the one who completes fifteen years is considered legally pubescent without needing to prove actual puberty (see f 2/63). 823.
Source pages p. 772

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the theory of legal capacity (al-ahliyyah) and guardianship (al-wilayah), specifically focusing on the fifth stage: the stage of maturity (rushd). The author begins by establishing a fundamental principle: the legal prohibition from handing over property to a minor before ascertaining his rushd is a prohibition from disposition itself; otherwise, if the minor could still dispose, the prohibition would be futile, and there is no futility in legislation (as per al-Shatibi's Muwafaqat). He then clarifies a common misconception: although many juristic texts state that guardianship ends and capacity becomes complete upon reaching puberty with sanity, these texts are understood to assume that the person is also a rashid (mature). The unconditional statements are qualified by other detailed rulings. The author provides examples: a discerning minor's will is invalid unless ratified after puberty, but the intended meaning is after reaching puberty as a rashid. He then critiques the view of some modern states that set a fixed age for handing over property without regard to actual maturity, arguing that this stems from confusing legal responsibility (taklif) with capacity for disposition. He notes that Abu Hanifa held that a person should be interdicted until age 25 unless rushd appears earlier, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) and the majority hold that rushd must be verified regardless of age, in accordance with the Quran. The author then defines the concept of fixing an age for rushd: it would mean setting a maximum age at which natural interdiction ends, with two benefits: (1) no proof of earlier rushd is accepted, and (2) meeting that age with sound mind automatically removes interdiction. However, he absolutely states that Islamic law does not fix such an age because rushd varies with individual and environmental factors. He resolves any confusion by reiterating the separation between taklif (based on physical and mental capability to understand the law) and capacity for financial disposition (based on practical experience in managing property). Thus, rushd is about financial competence, not just physical maturity.

Key Terms
الرشد (al-rushd): الخبرة العملية في إدارة المال وحفظه، وهي أهلية التصرف المالي بعد البلوغ
التكليف (al-taklif): الخطاب الشرعي المتوجه إلى المكلف بعد البلوغ والعقل، فيثبت به وجوب العبادات وغيرها
أهلية الأداء المدنية (ahliyyat al-ada' al-madaniyya): أهلية التصرف المالي التي تعتمد على الخبرة العملية في إدارة المال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary difference between legal responsibility (taklif) and capacity for financial disposition (ahliyyat al-tasarruf)?
A) Taklif requires physical strength, while disposition requires mental acuity.
B) Taklif is based on puberty and natural comprehension, while disposition requires practical experience in managing property.
C) There is no difference; both are established at puberty.
D) Taklif applies only to acts of worship, while disposition applies only to financial transactions.

Key Takeaway
Rushd is a legally distinct condition from bulugh; the mufta bihi position is that rushd must be verified in each individual, with no fixed age limit.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
The Stage of Rushd: Determining Full Legal Capacity
Defines rushd, its age determination, and Hanafi ikhtilaf on guardianship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi position on rushd to determine when financial guardianship ends and evaluate the tarjih between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Rushd is not tied to a fixed age; Sharia left its determination to authorities, and Hanafis differ on when guardianship ceases.

This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aamm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa explains the fifth stage of legal capacity: rushd (sound judgment). Understanding rushd is crucial for determining when a minor becomes fully financially independent. The passage defines the concept, discusses whether Sharia sets a specific age, and presents the famous ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Sahibayn). As a mufti, you need to know which position is relied upon and why.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of rushd
Define rushd as sound judgment in financial matters. Note that it is distinct from puberty (bulugh). Puberty is a prerequisite for rushd, but rushd may lag behind.
Rushd is about the ability to manage property wisely, not physical maturity.
2
Identify the two benefits of setting a fixed age for rushd
First, before that age, no claim of rushd is accepted. Second, upon reaching that age, the person is legally considered fully capable without proof.
These benefits assume a fixed age exists, but Sharia did not fix one.
3
Analyze the Quranic basis and the lack of fixed age
The Quran (4:6) ties the release of property to perceiving rushd after puberty, but does not specify an age. Sharia left age determination to authorities (wulat al-amr) based on public interest.
The verse is the primary dalil for both positions, but interpreted differently.
4
Trace the Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Hanifa held that financial guardianship ends at puberty; property may be withheld until age 25 as a maximum. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) held that guardianship continues until rushd is actually observed, with no fixed age limit, and re-interdiction is possible.
The key difference: Abu Hanifa emphasizes dignity and freedom; Sahibayn emphasize protection of wealth.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its rationale
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school for mu'amalat is the view of the Sahibayn. Later scholars like al-Haskafi and Ibn Abidin adopted it due to its harmony with the apparent meaning of the Quran and practical necessity to prevent waste of wealth.
This tarjih is based on public interest and the principle of preventing harm (dar' al-mafasid).

الفصل (٦٤) الطور الخامس للأهلية طور الرشد
٤/٦٤ - تحديد سن الرشد : معناه وثمرته معنى تحديد سن الرشد أن يُنصب حد في عمر الشخص ينتهي فيه الحجر الطبيعي الذي يفرضه الشرع حكماً على الصغار الناشئين.
ولهذا التحديد ثمرتان :
۱ - أنه لا يُقبل عندئذ من الشخص أن يثبت رشده قبل هذا الحد ولو كان بالغاً فعلاً، أو حكماً بالسن.
۲ - ان الشخص الذي يبلغ سن الرشد المحددة، وهو سليم العقل (أي غير مجنون ولا معتوه) يُعتبر عندئذٍ كامل أهلية التصرف حكماً، فيرفع عنه الحجر الشرعي ويتحرَّر من الولاية التي كانت عليه دون حاجة إلى إثبات رشده هذا مفهوم تحديد سن للرشد وثمرة هذا التحديد في الأحكام.
٥/٦٤ - هل للرشد سن محددة في الشرع الإسلامي :
الشريعة الإسلامية - وهي شريعة إنما جاءت لتكون مبادئها خالدة قابلة للتطبيق في كل زمان ومكان - لم تأتِ بتحديد سن للرشد، لأن زمن الرشد يختلف تبعاً لفطرة الشخص، كما يختلف باختلاف البيئات والأوضاع الاجتماعية والاقتصادية العامة، وبحسب العلم والتربية والأخلاق العامة.
على أن نصوص الشريعة ودلائل أحكامها تدل على أنه : لا يعتبر رشد قبل البلوغ فالبلوغ شريطة مبدئية كنقطة ارتكاز للانتقال من طور القصور إلى طور الرشد وقد يصاحب الرشد البلوغ وقد يتخلف عنه. فتتبع دلائل وجود الرشد اعتباراً من البلوغ ولذا جاء نص القرآن في هذا المقام بمبدأ عام ثابت وهو ربط كمال أهلية الشخص بإيناس رشده بعد البلوغ كما تقدم في قوله تعالى : ﴿وَابْتَلُوا الْيَتَامَى حَتَّى إِذَا بَلَغُوا النِّكَاحَ فَإِنْ آنَسْتُم مِّنْهُمْ رُشْدًا فَادْفَعُوا إِلَيْهِمْ أَمْوَالَهُمْ﴾ [سورة النساء ٦/٤].
أما تحديد سن لهذا الرشد فتركته الشريعة لولاة الأمر بحسب مقتضيات الزمن والسياسة الشرعية في المصالح المرسلة.
ومن ثم اختلفت الاجتهادات في مدى انتظار الرشد: فذهب أبو حنيفة إلى انتهاء الولاية المالية على الشخص وإطلاق التصرُّف له بمجرد بلوغه ولو سفيهاً مثلافاً. لكن أمواله إذا بلغ سفيهاً يؤخر تسليمها إليه على سبيل الاحتياط والتأديب عملاً بظاهر النص القرآني في عدم تسليمه ماله حتى ير شد؛ ولكنه لا ينتظر أكثر من تمام الخامسة والعشرين من عمره. فإذا أتمها يسلم إليه ماله ولو ظلّ سفيها. ولا يجوز في نظره الحجر على الكبير إلا بسبب العته أو الجنون، لأنَّ في سلب أهلية الإنسان الحر العاقل ضرراً معنوياً بكرامته يفوق ضرره المالي الذي يراد اتقاؤه بالحجر عليه.
ولكن جمهور الفقهاء وصاحبي أبي حنيفة قد ذهبوا إلى خلاف ذلك، فقرروا وجوب استمرار الولاية المالية على الشخص إذا بلغ غير رشيد حتى يُؤنس منه الرشد دون تحديد سن معينة للانتظار، وفقاً لظاهر النص القرآني السالف البيان بحجة أن منع ماله عنه يستلزم حجره عن التصرف القولي فيه كما تقدم، وإلا لم يكن لحبس ماله عنه فائدة.
وقرروا أيضاً وجوب إعادة الحجر عليه بحكم قضائي إذا ظهر منه سَفَه وتبذير بعد الرشد، معتبرين أن ضرر السفه عام لا خاص.
٨٢٤
Chapter (64): The Fifth Stage of Legal Capacity: The Stage of Rushd. 4/64 - Determining the Age of Rushd: Its Meaning and Benefit. The meaning of determining the age of rushd is to set a limit in the person's age at which the natural interdiction that the Sharia imposes as a ruling on growing minors ends. This determination has two benefits: 1) That the person will not be accepted to prove his rushd before this limit, even if he is actually before it, or legally by age. 2) That the person who reaches the specified age of rushd, and is of sound mind (i.e., not insane or feeble-minded), is then considered to have full capacity for transactions legally, so the legal interdiction is lifted from him and he is freed from the guardianship that was over him without needing to prove his rushd. This is the concept of determining an age for rushd and the benefit of this determination in rulings. 5/64 - Does Rushd Have a Specific Age in Islamic Law? Islamic law - which is a law that came so that its principles are eternal and applicable in every time and place - did not come with a determination of an age for rushd, because the time of rushd varies according to the person's innate nature, as it varies according to differences in environments and general social and economic conditions, and according to knowledge, education, and general morals. However, the texts of Sharia and the indications of its rulings indicate that rushd is not considered before puberty, for puberty is a preliminary condition as a pivot point for the transition from the stage of incapacity to the stage of rushd, and rushd may accompany puberty or may lag behind it. So one follows the signs of the existence of rushd from the time of puberty onwards. Therefore, the text of the Quran came in this context with a fixed general principle, which is linking the perfection of a person's capacity to perceiving rushd from them after puberty, as previously stated in His saying, the Most High: 'And test the orphans until they reach the age of marriage; then if you perceive in them sound judgment, give them their property' [Surah An-Nisa 4:6]. As for determining an age for this rushd, the Sharia left it to those in authority according to the requirements of time and Sharia policy in public interests. Hence, ijtihads differed regarding the extent of waiting for rushd: Abu Hanifa held that financial guardianship over the person ends and he is permitted to transact upon simply reaching puberty, even if he is foolish, for example. But if he reaches puberty as a fool, his property is withheld from him as a precaution and discipline, acting upon the apparent meaning of the Quranic text regarding not handing over his property until he becomes rushd; however, he does not wait for more than completing the age of twenty-five. When he completes it, his property is handed over to him even if he remains foolish. In his view, it is not permissible to place interdiction on an adult except due to feeblemindedness or insanity, because depriving a free, sane person of his capacity causes a moral harm to his dignity that outweighs the financial harm that is sought to be avoided by placing interdiction on him. But the majority of jurists and the two companions of Abu Hanifa held the opposite opinion, so they affirmed the necessity of continuing financial guardianship over the person if he reaches puberty without being rushd until rushd is perceived from him, without determining a specific age for waiting, in accordance with the apparent meaning of the preceding Quranic text, on the grounds that preventing his property from him necessitates his interdiction from verbal transactions concerning it as mentioned earlier, otherwise there would be no benefit in withholding his property from him. And they also affirmed the necessity of re-imposing interdiction on him by judicial ruling if foolishness and wastefulness appear from him after rushd, considering that the harm of foolishness is general not specific.
Source pages p. 773

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on legal capacity (ahliyyah) and discusses the fifth stage: the stage of rushd (sound judgment). The author begins by defining 'determining an age for rushd' as setting a fixed age after which the natural guardianship imposed on minors ends. He lists two practical benefits: (1) before that age, no claim of rushd is accepted even if the person has actually reached puberty; (2) upon reaching that age, the person is legally considered fully capable without needing to prove rushd. The author then addresses whether Sharia specifies a fixed age for rushd. He explains that Sharia, being universal and timeless, did not fix such an age because rushd depends on individual disposition and varies with environment, education, and social conditions. The Quranic verse (4:6) establishes the general principle of testing orphans and handing over property only when rushd is perceived, but leaves the specific age determination to the authorities (wulat al-amr) based on public interest (maslaha mursala). The author then presents the ikhtilaf: Imam Abu Hanifa held that financial guardianship ends at puberty itself; if the person is foolish, property is withheld until age 25 as a maximum, after which it must be handed over regardless of foolishness. He forbade interdiction on a sane adult except for insanity or feeblemindedness, prioritizing personal dignity over financial harm. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and the majority disagreed: they required continued guardianship until rushd is actually observed, without an age limit, and also allowed re-interdiction after rushd if foolishness appears. The relied-upon position (al-mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school for mu'amalat (financial transactions) is generally the view of the Sahibayn, especially when custom supports it, as it aligns more with the apparent meaning of the Quran. The authority weight: Abu Hanifa's view is from Zahir al-Riwayah, but the fatwa is given on the Sahibayn's view due to practical needs and to prevent waste of wealth.

Key Terms
الرشد (al-rushd): Sound judgment and ability to manage one's financial affairs properly; the stage when a person is no longer considered a minor in terms of financial capacity
الحجر (al-hajr): Legal restriction on a person's capacity to act financially; interdiction
الولاية المالية (al-wilaya al-maliyya): Financial guardianship exercised by a guardian over a minor or incompetent person's property

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding financial guardianship after a person reaches puberty but is not yet rushd?
A) Guardianship ends at puberty regardless of rushd.
B) Guardianship continues until rushd is observed, without a specific age limit.
C) Guardianship ends at age 25 maximum.
D) Guardianship ends only if the person is insane.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi for rushd requires actual observation of sound judgment; guardianship can continue beyond puberty and be reinstated if needed.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Puberty, Discernment, and Property Handover: The Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Explains the relationship between puberty and discernment, and the Hanafi ikhtilaf on delaying property delivery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi ikhtilaf on delaying property delivery and apply the preferred position (Sahibayn/Majalla) to a modern case.

Core Idea
Puberty alone does not grant full property capacity; discernment (rushd) determines the handover, but the Hanafi school differs on how long to wait.

The Quran establishes that orphans' property should be handed over when they reach marriageable age and show discernment. But what happens if discernment does not appear at puberty? This lesson unpacks the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and his two companions, and shows you how the preferred view is applied in fatwa today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key concepts
Distinguish between bulugh (puberty), rushd (discernment), and safah (foolishness). Understand that rushd is the legal condition for full capacity, not just age.
These terms often get confused in English translations.
2
Analyze the primary textual evidence
Read Quran 4:6 carefully. Note that it ties property handover to 'perceiving discernment' after puberty, but does not set a maximum waiting period.
Both sides use this same verse — the difference is in its interpretation and the weight given to other principles.
3
Distinguish Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that guardianship ends at puberty, but property is withheld until discernment or age 25. After 25, it is handed over unconditionally. He forbids interdiction except for insanity or imbecility, citing the moral harm to dignity.
His view gives priority to the adult's autonomy over financial risk.
4
Distinguish the Sahibayn/majority position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that guardianship continues indefinitely until discernment appears, and that judicial interdiction is required for foolishness after maturity. They argue that the harm of squandering affects society as a whole.
This position is supported by the later Hanafi tradition and the Majalla.
5
Determine the tarjih and its practical implications
The preferred view in the school is that of the Sahibayn. The mufta bihi today is that a foolish adult remains under guardianship until discernment is proven, and interdiction requires a court order.
Understanding this tarjih is essential for issuing correct fatwas.

الباب الثامن : نظرية الأهلية والولاية
على أن نصوص الشريعة ودلائل أحكامها تدل على أنه : لا يعتبر رشد قبل البلوغ فالبلوغ شريطة مبدئية كنقطة ارتكاز للانتقال من طور القصور إلى طور الرشد وقد يصاحب الرشد البلوغ وقد يتخلف عنه. فتتبع دلائل وجود الرشد اعتباراً من البلوغ ولذا جاء نص القرآن في هذا المقام بمبدأ عام ثابت وهو ربط كمال أهلية الشخص بإيناس رشده بعد البلوغ كما تقدم في قوله تعالى : ﴿وَابْتَلُوا الْيَتَامَىٰ حَتَّىٰ إِذَا بَلَغُوا النِّكَاحَ فَإِنْ آنَسْتُم مِّنْهُمْ رُشْدًا فَادْفَعُوا إِلَيْهِمْ أَمْوَالَهُمْ﴾ [سورة النساء ٦/٤] .
أما تحديد سن لهذا الرشد فتركته الشريعة لولاة الأمر بحسب مقتضيات الزمن والسياسة الشرعية في المصالح المرسلة .
ومن ثم اختلفت الاجتهادات في مدى انتظار الرشد: فذهب أبو حنيفة إلى انتهاء الولاية المالية على الشخص وإطلاق التصرف له بمجرد بلوغه ولو سفيهاً مثلاً. لكن أمواله إذا بلغ سفيهاً يؤخر تسليمها إليه على سبيل الاحتياط والتأديب عملاً بظاهر النص القرآني في عدم تسليمه ماله حتى يرشد؛ ولكنه لا ينتظر أكثر من تمام الخامسة والعشرين من عمره. فإذا أتمها يسلم إليه ماله ولو ظلّ سفيهاً. ولا يجوز في نظره الحجر على الكبير إلا بسبب العته أو الجنون، لأنَّ في سلب أهلية الإنسان الحر العاقل ضرراً معنوياً بكرامته يفوق ضرره المالي الذي يراد اتقاؤه بالحجر عليه.
ولكن جمهور الفقهاء وصاحبي أبي حنيفة قد ذهبوا إلى خلاف ذلك، فقرروا وجوب استمرار الولاية المالية على الشخص إذا بلغ غير رشيد حتى يُؤنس منه الرشد دون تحديد سن معينة للانتظار، وفقاً لظاهر النص القرآني السالف البيان بحجة أن منع ماله عنه يستلزم حجره عن التصرف القولي فيه كما تقدم، وإلا لم يكن لحبس ماله عنه فائدة . وقرروا أيضاً وجوب إعادة الحجر عليه بحكم قضائي إذا ظهر منه سفه وتبذير بعد الرشد، معتبرين أن ضرر السفه عام لا خاص.
قال أبو بكر الجصاص:
الفصل (٦٤) الطور الخامس للأهلية طور الرشد
إن ضرر السفيه يسري إلى الكافة فإنَّه إذا أفنى ماله بالتبذير صار وبالاً وعيالاً على الناس وبيت المال». وهذا هو الراجح في المذهب الحنفي نفسه وعليه العمل والمجلة. غير أنَّ أبا يوسف يشترط لحجر السفيه أن يقضي به القاضي ولو كان سفهه أصلياً منذ البلوغ (ر: المجلة / ٩٥٨ و ٩٨١ - ٩٨٤/، وكتاب الحجر في رد المحتار ٩٤/٥ - ٩٥ وفي غيره من الكتب الفقهية، وكشف الأسرار شرح أصول البزدوي ٣٧١/٤ - ٣٧٤) .
Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. The texts of Sharia and its proofs indicate that discernment is not considered before puberty; puberty is a preliminary condition as a point of departure for transition from the stage of deficiency to the stage of maturity. Discernment may accompany puberty or may lag behind. So the existence of discernment is traced from puberty onwards. Hence the Qur'anic text in this context established a constant general principle: linking a person's complete legal capacity to perceiving his discernment after puberty, as in His statement: 'And test the orphans until they reach the age of marriage; then if you perceive in them sound judgment, hand over to them their property' [Surah an-Nisa 4:6]. As for determining an age for this discernment, Sharia left it to the rulers according to the necessities of time and Sharia policy based on public interests. Consequently, ijtihads differed regarding the extent of waiting for discernment. Abu Hanifa held that financial guardianship over a person ends and he is given full freedom of transaction upon reaching puberty, even if he is foolish, for example. But if he reaches puberty while foolish, his property is withheld from him as a precaution and discipline, acting upon the apparent meaning of the Qur'anic text in not handing over his property until he becomes discerning; however, he does not wait beyond completion of twenty-five years of age. When he completes it, his property is given to him even if he remains foolish. In his view, interdiction upon an adult is not permissible except due to imbecility or insanity, because stripping a free, sane person of his legal capacity causes moral harm to his dignity that outweighs the financial harm sought to be avoided by interdiction. But the majority of jurists and the two companions of Abu Hanifa held the opposite view. They ruled that financial guardianship over a person must continue if he reaches puberty without being discerning until discernment is perceived from him, without specifying a particular waiting age, in accordance with the apparent meaning of the aforementioned Qur'anic text, arguing that preventing his property from him necessitates his interdiction from verbal disposal as discussed earlier, otherwise withholding his property would have no benefit. They also ruled that interdiction must be re-imposed by judicial order if foolishness and squandering appear after maturity, considering that the harm of foolishness is general, not specific. Abu Bakr al-Jassas said: Section (64): The Fifth Stage of Capacity: The Stage of Discernment. 'The harm of the foolish person extends to everyone: if he wastes his property by squandering, he becomes a burden and dependent on people and the treasury.' This is the preponderant position in the Hanafi school itself, and upon it is the practice and the Majalla. However, Abu Yusuf stipulates for interdiction of the foolish person that the judge must rule it, even if his foolishness was original since puberty (see: Majalla articles 958, 981-984; the chapter on interdiction in Radd al-Muhtar 5/94-95 and other fiqh books; Kashf al-Asrar Sharh Usul al-Bazdawi 4/371-374).
Source pages p. 774

Commentary
This passage is a theoretical exposition of legal capacity (al-ahliyya) and guardianship (al-wilaya) in Hanafi fiqh. The author begins by establishing the general principle: puberty (bulugh) is the necessary threshold for legal capacity, but full capacity—including control over property—requires discernment (rushd). The Quranic verse (4:6) is cited as the primary textual evidence. The author then explains that the determination of an exact age for discernment is left to the rulers (wulat al-amr) based on public interest. This leads into a detailed presentation of the ikhtilaf (scholarly disagreement) on the issue. Abu Hanifa's position is that financial guardianship ends at puberty; however, if the person is foolish (safih), his property is withheld until discernment or age 25, whichever comes first. After 25, property is handed over even if foolishness persists. Abu Hanifa forbids interdiction (hajr) on a free adult except for mental incapacity. The majority, including the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), hold that guardianship continues indefinitely until discernment is perceived. They also require judicial interdiction if foolishness appears after maturity. The author then cites Abu Bakr al-Jassas to emphasize the public harm of foolishness. Finally, it is noted that the preferred view in the Hanafi school—and the basis for the Majalla—is that of the majority, with Abu Yusuf adding the condition of a judicial ruling for interdiction.

Key Terms
الرشد (al-rushd): Sound judgment and ability to manage one's financial affairs responsibly; the quality that triggers full capacity to dispose of property.
السفه (al-safah): Foolishness or squandering; a legal impediment that justifies withholding property and interdiction.
الحجر (al-hajr): Legal interdiction restricting a person's right to dispose of property due to incapacity or foolishness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens to a person who reaches puberty but is foolish (safih) and then turns 25 years old?
A) Financial guardianship continues until discernment is perceived.
B) His property is handed over to him even if he remains foolish.
C) He is automatically placed under judicial interdiction.
D) He must petition the court to regain control of his property.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's preferred position is that guardianship over a foolish adult continues indefinitely until discernment is perceived, with court-ordered interdiction as a safeguard.

Determining the Age of Majority and Its Rationale
Discussion of the fifth stage of legal capacity and the role of maslaha in setting the age of majority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rationale for not fixing a specific age of majority and apply the principle of maslaha to justify state-defined ages.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, rushd is a matter of proof, not a fixed age; rulers may set an age for public interest without violating Sharia.

This passage explores the concept of rushd (maturity) as a stage of legal capacity separate from mere puberty. It delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute about the interdiction of a safih and then traces how historical and modern societies have adjusted the age of majority based on public interest. Understanding this discussion is key to appreciating the flexibility of Hanafi legal theory in accommodating changing circumstances while remaining rooted in its principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fifth stage of capacity
Locate the part where Abu Bakr al-Jassas defines the stage of rushd as distinct from puberty. Understand that rushd refers to financial competence and sound judgment.
This is the foundational concept: capacity is not automatic at puberty; one must prove rushd.
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on hajr of the safih
Compare the preponderant view (no judge needed) with Abu Yusuf's view (judge's ruling required). Note the practical difference: immediate interdiction vs. waiting for a court order.
The dispute is recorded in the Majalla and later hanafi works; the preponderant view is mufta bihi.
3
Trace the historical shift in age of majority
Read about Roman law and modern codes to see how social complexity and moral decay led to raising the age. Observe that this is a temporal adjustment, not a fixed Sharia rule.
The author uses this historical evidence to show that maslaha drives such changes.
4
Evaluate the Syrian legal history
Examine how the Ottoman imperial will set age 20, then the Majalla restored the original rule allowing proof of rushd after puberty. Note that the Majalla followed Hanafi ijtihad but left room for sultanic orders.
This illustrates the harmony between Sharia and state policy (siyasa shar'iyya).
5
Synthesize the principle of maslaha
Conclude that Sharia does not prescribe a specific age for majority; rather, it delegates to rulers the authority to set ages based on public interest. This is fully consistent with Hanafi fiqh and its maqasid.
The key takeaway: flexibility without contradiction to the core ruling.

Source Text

قال أبو بكر الجصاص:
الفصل (٦٤) الطور الخامس للأهلية طور الرشد
إن ضرر السفيه يسري إلى الكافة فإنَّه إذا أفنى ماله بالتبذير صار وبالاً وعيالاً على الناس وبيت المال».
وهذا هو الراجح في المذهب الحنفي نفسه وعليه العمل والمجلة. غير أنَّ أبا يوسف يشترط لحجر السفيه أن يقضي به القاضي ولو كان سفهه أصلياً منذ البلوغ (ر: المجلة / ٩٥٨ و ٩٨١ - ٩٨٤/، وكتاب الحجر في رد المحتار ٩٤/٥ - ٩٥ وفي غيره من الكتب الفقهية، وكشف الأسرار شرح أصول البزدوي ٣٧١/٤ - ٣٧٤) .
٦/٦٤ - تحديد سن الرشد في القوانين :
كان في التشريع الروماني سن محددة للرشد الذي يخرج به الإنسان من نطاق الحجر الطبيعي القانوني، ويتمتع فيه بأهلية التصرف كاملة. وكانت لديهم هذه السن في بداية الأمر أربع عشرة سنة في الذكور، واثنتي عشرة في الإناث أي سن البلوغ الفعلي تقريباً)، لأن الأخلاق كانت ساذجة وروابط الأسرة ومراقبتها قوية الإحاطة بالناشئين. فلا حاجة للإبطاء منحهم الأهلية الكاملة.
فلما ازدحم المجتمع وتشعبت الأعمال، وطغت الأطماع، وضعفت روابط الأسرة، ظهرت للتعجيل بمنح الأهلية مضار اضطرتهم إلى تأخيره حتى سن الخامسة والعشرين.
وبعض الشرائع القانونية الحديثة حَدَّدت سن الرشد بتسع عشرة سنة،
·
وبعضها بإحدى وعشرين واختار القانون المصري سابقاً ثماني عشرة سنة . ثم رفعت إلى إحدى وعشرين (ر) شرح القانون المدني المصري القديم للأستاذ فتحي زغلول باشا ص/ ۲۸).
٧/٦٤ - ومن الواضح أنَّ رفع سن الرشد إلى حد معين بعد البلوغ
هو اليوم مما تقتضيه المصلحة. فقد تعقدت المعاملات وإجراءاتها،
وتشعبت كثيراً، وتدهورت الأخلاق العامة وكثر تفنن المحتالين في سلب أموال الناس فأصبح لا أبد من زيادة الاحتياط في حماية الناشئين، وصيانة ذممهم وأموالهم بتحديد سن الرشد في حد مرتفع. وهذا لا ينافي الشريعة الإسلامية الحكيمة بل يوافق مبادئها وفقهها في رعاية المصالح كل الموافقة . (ر: رسالة الأهلية للأستاذ الشيخ أحمد إبراهيم ص/ ۲۱).
سن الرشد القانونية في سورية
٨/٦٤ - (١) - قبل القانون المدني الجديد : في سنة ۱۲۸۸ هـ من العهد العثماني صدرت إرادة سلطانية سنية تمنع القضاة من سماع دعوى الرشد وإثباتها ممن لم يتم العشرين من عمره، نظراً للمصلحة الزمنية التي توجب رفع سن الرشد إلى حد تتوافر فيه الخبرة في الشؤون المالية (١)
ثم صدرت مجلة الأحكام العدلية بالإرادة السلطانية المؤرخة في ٢٣ من شعبان سنة ۱۲۹۳ هـ فأجازت المواد / ۹۸۱ - ۹۸۳/ منها للصغير إذا بلغ أن يثبت رشده منذ بلوغه وحينئذ يسلمه الوصي أمواله .
وقد جرت المجلة في ذلك على أصل الحكم الشرعي، لأن اللجنة
التي كلفت وضعها إنما مَشَت على تقنين الأحكام الشرعية الأصلية بناء على الاجتهاد الحنفي، تاركة أمر المصالح للأوامر السلطانية.
٩/٦ - وبذلك نسخت المجلة حكم الإرادة السلطانية السابقة في تحديد سن الرشد بعشرين عاماً ورجعت به إلى حد البلوغ الطبيعي، ولكنها لم تجعل البلوغ قرينة شرعية (قانونية) كافية لاعتبار الشخص رشيداً إلى أن يثبت سفههه ويقضي عليه بالحجر، بل جعلت الشخص بالبلوغ قابلاً لأن يكون رشيداً. فعليه إثبات رشده ليصبح كامل الأهلية.
(۱) ترى هذه الإرادة السنية في شرح المادة // ۹۸۳/ من المجلة من شرح العلامة علي حيدر، وشرح الأستاذ سليم رستم الباز (الطبعة الثانية).
Abu Bakr al-Jassas said: Section (64) The fifth stage of capacity: the stage of rushd (maturity). Indeed, the harm of the prodigal (safih) extends to everyone, for if he wastes his wealth through extravagance, he becomes a burden and dependent upon the people and the public treasury. This is the preponderant position in the Hanafi school itself, and upon it is practice and the Majalla. However, Abu Yusuf stipulates for the interdiction (hajr) of the prodigal that the judge should rule for it, even if his prodigality is original (since puberty). (See: Majalla articles 958 and 981-984, and the chapter on hajr in Radd al-Muhtar 5/94-95 and other fiqh books, and Kashf al-Asrar Sharh Usul al-Bazdawi 4/371-374). 6/64 - Determination of the age of majority in laws: In Roman legislation, there was a specific age for majority by which a person exits the scope of natural legal interdiction and enjoys full capacity to act. Initially, this age was fourteen years for males and twelve for females (i.e., approximately the actual age of puberty), because morals were simple and family ties and supervision strongly encompassed youths. So there was no need to delay granting them full capacity. When society became crowded, businesses diversified, greed prevailed, and family ties weakened, harms appeared from granting capacity early, forcing them to delay it until the age of twenty-five. Some modern legal codes set the age of majority at nineteen years, and some at twenty-one, and the former Egyptian law chose eighteen years, then raised it to twenty-one. (See: Sharh al-Qanun al-Madani al-Misri al-Qadim by Prof. Fathi Zaghlul Pasha, p. 28). 7/64 - It is clear that raising the age of majority to a certain point after puberty is today required by maslaha. Transactions and their procedures have become complex and greatly diversified, public morals have deteriorated, and the inventiveness of swindlers in plundering people's wealth has increased, so it has become necessary to increase caution in protecting youths and safeguarding their responsibilities and wealth by setting the age of majority at a high limit. This does not contradict the wise Islamic Sharia, but rather fully agrees with its principles and fiqh in caring for interests. (See: Risalah al-Ahliyyah by Prof. Sheikh Ahmad Ibrahim, p. 21). Legal age of majority in Syria: 8/64 - (1) Before the new Civil Code: In the year 1288 AH during the Ottoman era, a noble imperial will was issued preventing judges from hearing a claim of majority and proving it for anyone who has not reached twenty years of age, due to the temporal maslaha that required raising the age of majority to a limit where financial experience is available. Then the Majalla al-Ahkam al-Adliyya was issued by the imperial will dated 23 Sha'ban 1293 AH, and its articles (981-983) allowed the minor if he has reached puberty to prove his rushd from the time of puberty, and then the guardian delivers his property to him. The Majalla in that followed the original legal ruling, because the committee tasked with its compilation followed the codification of original Sharia rulings based on Hanafi ijtihad, leaving matters of maslaha to the sultanic orders. 9/64 - Thus, the Majalla abrogated the earlier imperial will regarding setting the age of majority at twenty years and returned to the natural age of puberty, but it did not make puberty a sufficient legal presumption to consider a person mature until his prodigality is proven and interdiction is ruled against him; rather, it made the person upon puberty eligible to be mature. Thus, he must prove his rushd to become fully capable. (1) See this imperial will in the explanation of Article (983) of the Majalla from the commentary of Allama Ali Haidar and the commentary of Prof. Salim Rustum al-Baz (second edition).
Source pages p. 775

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophical discussion about the fifth stage of legal capacity—the stage of rushd (maturity). The author begins by quoting Abu Bakr al-Jassas, who states that the harm of the prodigal (safih) extends to everyone, as wasting wealth makes him a burden on society and the public treasury. This underscores the rationale for interdiction (hajr) of the safih. The author then clarifies that this is the preponderant position in the Hanafi school, as adopted by the Majalla. However, Abu Yusuf held a different view, requiring a judge's ruling for interdiction even if the prodigality existed from puberty. This dispute is an intra-madhhab difference: the relied-upon position (al-mukhtar) is that a safih is automatically interdicted without a court order, as per the early Hanafi masters, but Abu Yusuf's opinion is also recorded. The author then traces the historical development of the age of majority in Roman law, showing that it shifted from puberty to twenty-five due to changing circumstances. Modern laws adopted various ages. The key point is that raising the age of majority after puberty is not contrary to Islamic Sharia; rather, it aligns with the principle of maslaha (public interest). The author then presents the Syrian legal history: an Ottoman imperial will set the age at twenty, but later the Majalla restored the original Sharia ruling that puberty is the threshold for possible rushd, but the minor must prove actual rushd to receive his property. This demonstrates the flexibility of Hanafi fiqh in accommodating state regulations (siyasa shar'iyya) while preserving the foundational principles. The passage is a beneficial probe into how classical Hanafi rulings interact with modern codification and public policy.

Key Terms
سفيه (safih): A person who is legally interdicted due to wasteful spending and lack of financial prudence, regardless of mental capacity.
رشد (rushd): The state of having sound judgment and financial competence, enabling a person to manage his own affairs; the stage of legal capacity after puberty.
حجر (hajr): Legal interdiction preventing a person from disposing of his property due to a disqualifying condition like safah, insanity, or minority.
سن الرشد (sinn al-rushd): The legislated age at which a person is presumed to have attained full legal capacity to act; in Islamic law, it is not fixed but determined by actual rushd, though rulers may set a temporal age based on maslaha.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition did Abu Yusuf require for the interdiction (hajr) of a person who is prodigal (safih) from the age of puberty?
A) That the prodigal must reach a specific age like 25.
B) That a judge must rule for the interdiction.
C) That the prodigal must be declared insane.
D) That the guardian must consent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not fix a specific age for majority; it requires proof of rushd, and allows rulers to set ages for public interest, harmonizing Sharia with changing circumstances.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

8 min
The Inseparable Link Between Capacity and Property Delivery in Hanafi Law: Refuting the Decoupling Argument
Critical refinement of a modern misinterpretation of Hanafi legal capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the relationship between rushd and property delivery, and identify the logical flaw in the argument that capacity can be perfected while property is withheld.

Core Idea
Hanafi law requires both capacity and property delivery; you cannot have one without the other.

In this passage, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa corrects a modern misunderstanding about the Ottoman Majalla's provisions on the age of rushd. Some jurists and courts have argued that a minor who reaches puberty can be fully capacitated while his property is withheld until rushd is proven. Al-Zarqa demonstrates that this view contradicts the very purpose of legal capacity and the internal logic of Hanafi fiqh. We will unpack his reasoning and see why capacity and property delivery are necessarily linked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The issue is whether 'rushd' (sound judgment) is a condition for full legal capacity, or whether full capacity is granted at puberty with only property delivery delayed.
2
Understand the opposing argument
Dr. Mahmasani and the Syrian Court of Cassation relied on Article 982 of the Majalla, which says property is not handed over until rushd is verified, to claim that interdiction (hajr) is removed at puberty and capacity is complete.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle of talazum
Al-Zarqa invokes the necessary link (talazum) between 'hajr al-mal' (withholding property) and 'hajr al-tasarruf' (preventing verbal dispositions). If property is withheld, capacity to dispose must also be restricted, otherwise the minor can squander wealth through debts.
4
Analyze the logical proof
If a minor could make contracts while property is withheld, creditors could take the property, defeating the purpose of withholding. Therefore, preventing delivery necessarily implies preventing disposal.
5
Conclude the correct Hanafi position
Thus, rushd is a condition for full capacity; until rushd is proven, both property and the power to dispose are restricted. The Majalla's Articles 981 and 982 must be read together: Article 981 requires testing for rushd before considering the minor capacitated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٦٤) الطور الخامس للأهلية : طور الرشد . ثم صدر في ٤ من ربيع الأول سنة ١٣٢٤ هـ نظام إدارة أموال الأيتام الذي لا يزال بعض أحكامه نافذاً في سورية إلى اليوم) فنصت المادة / ٥٩ منه على أنه : بعد أن يكمل الأيتام سن العشرين تنظم المحكمة الشرعية حجة بذلك، وتبرز لمديري الأيتام، وبعد إجراء المعاملة اللازمة تسلم إليهم أموالهم التي في الصندوق» . . . وهذا كما ترى عودة قانونية من السلطان العثماني إلى تحديد سن الرشد بالحد الذي كانت قررته الإرادة السنية السلطانية سنة ١٢٨٨ هـ، وهو تمام العشرين عاماً. ذلك لأن التعبير القانوني بتسليم المال إلى الأيتام بعد إكمالهم عشرين عاماً، أو بعدم تسليمهم أموالهم قبل هذه السن يستلزم نفي إمكان رشدهم قبلها ويساوي التعبير بعدم» كمال أهليتهم قبل سن العشرين . فقد أسلفنا أن منع تسليم المال إلى صاحبه الناشيء قبل إيناس رشده يستلزم منع نفاذ تصرفاته القولية (ر : ف (٢/٦٤ . ولا يعقل في المنطق التشريعي خلاف ذلك؛ وإلا استطاع الشخص أن يتصرف بجميع أمواله ويبددها بكلمتين عن طريق التعاقد أو الإقرار، فلا يكون عدم تسليمها إليه من إتلافه لها، إذ يتسلمها غرماؤه الدائنون له بدلاً منه (1) . (۱) أثناء الطبعة الثالثة من هذا الكتاب اطلعت على ما كتبه الأستاذ المحامي الدكتور صبحي المحمصاني من بيروت عن الأهلية في الجزء الثاني من كتابه القيم الذي ظهر حديثاً باسم النظرية العامة للموجبات والعقود في الشريعة الإسلامية» ونقلته عنه المجلة القضائية البيروتية في العدد الثالث من السنة / ۲۸/ فإذا به يقرر انفكاك التلازم بين كمال أهلية التصرف وتسليم المال : فإنه بعد أن حكى اختلاف الاجتهادات في ارتفاع الحجر عن الصغير بمجرد البلوغ أو بظهور الرشد بعد البلوغ، نقل ما جاء في المادة / ۹۸۲/ من المجلة ونصها : إذا بلغ الصبي غير رشيد فلا تدفع إليه أمواله مالم يتحقق رشده، ويمنع التصرف . ثم قال مستنتجاً: فإذن يرتفع الحَجْر عن الصغير بالبلوغ، إلا أن يحجر عليه القاضي إذا استبان عدم رشده، ولكن أمواله لا تدفع إليه إلا بعد ثبوت الرشد. فالأهلية شيء وتسليم المال شيء آخر . (ر) الكتاب المذكور ص / ١١٣ - ١١٤). ج وقد سبقته إلى هذا الفهم محكمة التمييز السورية بقرارها المؤرخ في ١٤ من شهر شباط / ۱۹۳۳ م فنقضت به حكماً للمحكمة الشرعية يقضي برد دعوى إثبات الرشد لعدم إكمال المدعي سن العشرين، وعللت النقض بمثل هذا التعليل !! ومما أسلفنا بيانه يظهر الخطأ جليّاً في هذا الفهم، لأنه مبني على انفكاك التلازم بين حجر المال وحجر التصرف فيه، وكذا بين كمال الأهلية والرشد؛ بحيث يعتبر الشخص كامل الأهلية بمجرد البلوغ ولكن يحبس عنه ماله حتى يثبت رشده، على أساس أن تسليم المال تابع لثبوت رشده، وأن الرشد غير كمال الأهلية! وشبهة الأستاذ المحمصاني في هذا ناشئة من نص المادة / ۹۸۱/ من المجلة، ونصها كما نقله في مطلع بحثه هو كما يلي: ولا ينبغي أن يستعجل في إعطاء الصبي ماله عند بلوغه، بل يجرب بالتأني، فإذا تُحقق كونه رشيداً تدفع حينئذ إليه أمواله . ولا يخفى أن معنى هذه المادة هو أنه لا يعتبر الصبي رشيداً كامل الأهلية بمجرد بلوغه، بل تجب تجربته واختباره وليس معناها أنه يمنع عنه ماله رغم اعتباره بمجرد البلوغ رشيداً كامل الأهلية. (۱) يعبر القانون المدني هنا وفي جميع المواطن الأخرى عن السنة الشمسية بلفظ سنة ميلادية وهو تعبير غير صحيح، لأن لفظ (ميلادية وهجرية) إنما يدل لغة على مبدأ الحساب السنوي للتاريخ لا على نوع السنة من كونها شمسية أو قمرية، وإن كان الواقع أن التاريخ الميلادي المسيحي محسوب بالسنة الشمسية، والتاريخ الهجري الإسلامي بالسنة القمرية لأن هذا الواقع مصادفة لا لغة.
Chapter (64) The Fifth Stage of Legal Capacity: The Stage of Rushd. Then on the 4th of Rabi' al-Awwal 1324 AH, the Regulation for the Administration of Orphans' Funds was issued, some of whose provisions are still in force in Syria today. Article 59 of it stated: 'After the orphans complete the age of twenty, the Sharia court prepares a document of attestation, and presents it to the orphan administrators, and after necessary procedures, their funds in the fund are handed over to them.' This, as you see, is a legal return by the Ottoman Sultan to setting the age of rushd at the limit decided by the sovereign imperial will in 1288 AH, which is the completion of twenty years. That is because the legal expression of handing over property to orphans after they complete twenty years, or of not handing over their property before this age, entails the negation of the possibility of their rushd before it and equals the expression of 'not' perfecting their legal capacity before the age of twenty. We have previously stated that preventing the handing over of property to its young owner before ascertaining his rushd entails preventing the enforcement of his verbal dispositions. Nor is the contrary conceivable in legislative logic; otherwise, a person could dispose of all his property and squander it in two words through contract or acknowledgment, and then non-delivery to him would not protect his wealth from waste, as his creditors would receive it instead of him. (1) (1) During the third edition of this book, I came across what was written by lawyer Dr. Subhi Mahmasani from Beirut about legal capacity in the second part of his valuable book recently published under the name 'The General Theory of Obligations and Contracts in Islamic Law,' and it was quoted by the Beirut Judicial Journal in issue 3 of year 28. He there decided the decoupling of the interconnection between the perfection of the capacity to dispose and the delivery of property: after recounting the difference of ijtihads regarding the removal of interdiction from the minor upon mere puberty or upon the appearance of rushd after puberty, he quoted what came in Article 982 of the Majalla, its text: 'If the minor reaches puberty but is not rashid, his property is not handed over to him until his rushd is verified, and he is prevented from disposing.' Then he concluded: 'Thus, interdiction is removed from the minor upon puberty, unless the judge places him under interdiction if he discerns his lack of rushd, but his property is not handed over to him except after establishing rushd. So legal capacity is one thing, and handing over property is another.' (Reference: the aforementioned book, pp. 113-114). And the Syrian Court of Cassation preceded him in this understanding with its decision dated 14 February 1933, by which it reversed a judgment of the Sharia court that dismissed a lawsuit for establishing rushd due to the plaintiff not having completed the age of twenty, and it justified the reversal with such reasoning!! From what we have explained, the error in this understanding is evident, because it is based on the decoupling between the interdiction on property and the interdiction on disposal thereof, and also between perfection of legal capacity and rushd; such that a person is considered fully capacitated upon mere puberty but his property is withheld from him until his rushd is established, on the basis that the handing over of property is dependent on establishing his rushd, and rushd is not perfection of legal capacity! Dr. Mahmasani's misconception in this arises from the text of Article 981 of the Majalla, its text as he quoted at the beginning of his research is as follows: 'One should not hasten to give the youth his property upon his reaching puberty, but should test him gradually. When he is verified to be rashid, then his property is handed over to him.' It is not hidden that the meaning of this article is that the youth is not considered rashid and fully capacitated merely upon reaching puberty, but rather must be tried and tested; it does not mean that his property is withheld from him despite his being considered, upon mere puberty, rashid and fully capacitated. (1) The Civil Code here and in all other places expresses the solar year with the word 'Miladi' (Christian) year, which is an incorrect expression, because the words 'Miladi' and 'Hijri' linguistically indicate only the starting point of the annual calendar calculation, not the type of year as solar or lunar, even though in reality the Christian calendar is calculated by the solar year and the Islamic Hijri calendar by the lunar year, because this reality is coincidental, not linguistic.
Source pages p. 777

Commentary
This passage from 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Am' by Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa is a legal philosophy discussion about the fifth stage of legal capacity: the stage of rushd (maturity/sound judgment). The author begins by citing an Ottoman regulation from 1324 AH concerning the administration of orphans' funds, which set the age for handing over property to orphans at twenty years. He argues that this regulation implicitly establishes that rushd is not automatically attained at puberty; rather, a specific age (twenty) is presumed, and before that age rushd is not legally possible. Al-Zarqa then reiterates his earlier argument that preventing the handing over of property until rushd is verified necessarily entails preventing the minor from making verbal dispositions (contracts, acknowledgments), because if the minor could still dispose of property verbally even though the property is not physically handed over, he could still squander it through debts, as creditors would take it. Thus, the preventive measure of withholding property without also disabling capacity to dispose would be futile. The passage then enters into a polemical engagement with Dr. Subhi Mahmasani, a modern Lebanese scholar, who argued in his book that there is a decoupling between perfection of capacity to dispose and delivery of property. Mahmasani cited Article 982 of the Ottoman Majalla (Civil Code) to support his view that interdiction is removed at puberty but property is not handed over until rushd is proven. The Syrian Court of Cassation had similarly ruled in 1933 that a minor who had reached puberty could be considered fully capacitated yet his property withheld. Al-Zarqa refutes this interpretation vigorously, arguing that it is based on a false separation between two types of interdiction: interdiction on property (hajr al-mal) and interdiction on disposal (hajr al-tasarruf). In Hanafi fiqh, these are linked: if a person is considered fully capacitated (kamil al-ahliyya), he cannot be prevented from disposing of his property. The Majalla's Article 981, which Mahmasani also quoted, actually says that a minor should not be considered rashid at mere puberty but must be tested. This means that rushd is a condition for full capacity, not that full capacity exists from puberty and property is withheld. The author corrects this misunderstanding, affirming the traditional Hanafi position that rushd is a prerequisite for full legal capacity and delivery of property. This passage serves as a critical refinement of a modern reinterpretation of classical Hanafi rules on legal capacity.

Key Terms
الرشد (al-rushd): النضج العقلي والمالي الذي يؤهل الشخص للتصرف في ماله بحكمة
الأهلية (al-ahliyya): الصلاحية القانونية لوجوب الحقوق ونفاذ التصرفات
الحجر (al-hajr): المنع من التصرف في المال بسبب الصغر أو السفه أو الفلس
التلازم (al-talazum): الارتباط الضروري بين أمرين بحيث لا ينفك أحدهما عن الآخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Zarqa, what is the logical flaw in Dr. Mahmasani's interpretation of the Majalla?
A) He misread Article 982 to imply that capacity and property delivery are separate.
B) He ignored the requirement that a judge must always declare rushd before property delivery.
C) He assumed that Article 981 allows property delivery to be delayed even after rushd is proven.
D) He claimed that the Ottoman regulation set the age of rushd at 18, not 20.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, rushd is an essential condition for full legal capacity; without it, both the delivery of property and the power to dispose are restricted, preventing the minor from squandering wealth through contracts or debts.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Separating Legal Capacity and Property Handover: A Hanafi Critique
Critique of separating legal capacity from property handover after puberty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the error in separating legal capacity (ahliyya) from prudence (rushd) and state the correct Hanafi position on property handover after puberty as per the Majalla.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, legal capacity and prudence are linked; property is handed over after verifying rushd, not withheld from a fully capable person.

This passage critiques a misinterpretation that separates legal capacity (ahliyya) from prudence (rushd), leading to the practice of withholding property from a person who is otherwise considered fully capable. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, refutes this view by analyzing Article 981 of the Majalla and demonstrating that the Hanafi school treats capacity and rushd as interdependent. You will learn to recognize and correct this common error in applying fiqh to modern legal contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mistaken Interpretation
Read the passage to understand the view attributed to Professor al-Mahmasani and the Syrian Court of Cassation. Note their claim that legal capacity and property handover are separate concepts.
Focus on the phrase 'الأهلية شيء وتسليم المال شيء آخر'.
2
Understand the Correct Hanafi Position
From the author's refutation, extract the Hanafi principle that capacity and rushd are linked. Note that upon puberty with sound mind, a person attains full capacity, but property is handed over only after testing (tajriba) to confirm rushd.
The author cites Majalla Article 981 to show that testing is for verifying rushd, not for withholding property from a capable person.
3
Analyze the Evidence (Majalla Article 981)
Examine the text of Majalla Article 981 as quoted. Identify the difference between the mistaken reading (separating capacity and property) and the correct reading (testing before handover without negating capacity).
Key phrase: 'لا ينبغي أن يستعجل في إعطاء الصبي ماله عند بلوغه' — the article advises not to be hasty, not to withhold indefinitely.
4
Evaluate Practical Implications for Fatwa
Consider how this understanding affects a mufti's response to modern cases where local laws set a fixed age for property handover. Determine the correct Hanafi approach: verify rushd through testing, but do not treat capacity as separate.
The Syrian Code's age-18 rule is critiqued as conflicting with the fiqh principle of testing upon puberty.

Chapter Eight: The Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. 64/10 - (B): After the new Civil Code: The Syrian Civil Code issued in 1949 CE, while we are reprinting this book, Article 46 set the age of majority (rushd) at eighteen complete solar years (1), and stated that every person who completes that age while possessing his mental faculties and not judicially interdicted becomes fully capable of exercising his civil rights. = If a minor reaches puberty but is not prudent (rashid), his wealth is not handed over to him until his prudence is verified, and he is prevented from disposing. Then he concluded: So interdiction is lifted from the minor upon reaching puberty, unless the judge places him under interdiction if he perceives his imprudence, but his wealth is not handed over to him until prudence is established. So legal capacity is one thing and handing over wealth is another. (Reference: the mentioned book, pp. 113-114). The Syrian Court of Cassation preceded this understanding in its decision dated 14 February 1933 CE, thereby overturning a ruling of the religious court that dismissed a lawsuit to establish prudence because the claimant had not completed twenty years of age, and justified the cassation with similar reasoning!! From what we have stated, the error in this understanding is clear, because it is based on disconnecting the correlation between interdiction of wealth and interdiction of disposition, and also between full legal capacity and prudence; such that a person is considered fully capable merely upon puberty but his wealth is withheld until his prudence is proven, on the basis that handing over wealth is contingent on establishing his prudence, and that prudence is not full legal capacity! Professor Al-Mahmasani's doubt arises from the text of Article 981 of the Majalla, the text of which, as he quoted at the beginning of his research, is as follows: One should not be hasty in giving the minor his wealth upon his puberty, but should test him gently; when he is confirmed to be prudent, his wealth is then handed over to him. It is not hidden that the meaning of this article is that the minor is not considered prudent and fully capable merely upon puberty, but must be tried and tested, and it does not mean that his wealth is withheld from him despite him being considered prudent and fully capable merely upon puberty. (1) The Civil Code here and in all other places uses the phrase 'solar year' as 'Milaadi (Christian) year,' which is an incorrect expression, because the terms 'Milaadi' and 'Hijri' linguistically indicate the beginning of the annual calendar calculation, not the type of year as solar or lunar, although in reality the Christian calendar is calculated by solar years and the Islamic Hijri calendar by lunar years because this reality is coincidental, not linguistic. 'his civil rights.' Our law has thus contradicted its source, which is the new Egyptian Code that maintained the determination of the age of majority at twenty-one years as in the old Egyptian Code. The rationale for lowering the age of majority in our Syrian law to eighteen is that, according to our Public Employees Law, this age qualifies a person to assume public office in the state; it is inconceivable that a person undertakes a public state job while still a minor under interdiction from disposing of his private rights and wealth. Finally, the new Personal Status Law was issued in Syria in 1953 CE, and Article 163 confirmed the determination of the age of majority at eighteen complete solar years, in accordance with the Civil Code.
Source pages p. 778

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the Syrian Civil Code's stance on legal capacity and the handover of property. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by quoting the Syrian Civil Code (1949) which sets the age of majority (rushd) at eighteen solar years. He then presents the mistaken interpretation held by Professor al-Mahmasani and the Syrian Court of Cassation, who claim that legal capacity (al-ahliyya) and the handover of property are separate: a person becomes fully capable at puberty but his property can be withheld until his prudence (rushd) is proven. Al-Zarqa refutes this by explaining that according to the Hanafi school, the interdiction of property (hajr al-mal) and interdiction of disposition (hajr al-tasarruf) are inherently linked. He cites Article 981 of the Majalla to show that it does not separate the two; rather, it requires testing the minor to determine prudence, not to hold back his property despite recognizing his full capacity. Al-Zarqa criticizes the Syrian code for diverging from the Egyptian code and for basing its reduction of the majority age on the rationale of public employment eligibility, which he implies is a weak justification from a fiqh perspective. The footnote corrects the misnomer 'Milaadi year' for 'solar year.' The author concludes by noting that the Syrian Personal Status Law of 1953 follows the Civil Code in setting majority at eighteen.

Key Terms
الأهلية (al-ahliyya): Legal capacity to acquire rights and obligations (ahliyyat al-wujub) and to exercise them (ahliyyat al-ada'). Here it refers to full capacity to act (kamil al-ahliyya).
الرشد (al-rushd): Prudence or sound judgment in financial matters, which qualifies a person to handle his own property. It is not identical with full legal capacity; it is a separate condition for property handover in the mistaken view, but the author argues they are inseparable.
الحجر (al-hajr): Judicial interdiction preventing a person from disposing of his wealth due to incapacity or imprudence. Here, the author distinguishes between 'hajr al-mal' (interdiction of wealth) and 'hajr al-tasarruf' (interdiction of disposition), but argues they are inseparable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the author's main criticism of the Syrian Court of Cassation's decision?
A) It set the age of majority at eighteen solar years.
B) It separated legal capacity from property handover, violating the Hanafi principle of inseparability.
C) It ignored the need for judicial interdiction for the insane.
D) It contradicted the Egyptian code by lowering the age of majority.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, legal capacity (ahliyya) and prudence (rushd) are interdependent; property is handed over after confirming rushd through testing, not withheld from a fully capable person.

Classification of Impediments to Legal Capacity
Classification of impediments and their types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify impediments (ʿawāriḍ) into celestial and acquired and identify the six celestial and three acquired impediments according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Impediments to legal capacity are either beyond human choice (celestial) or result from it (acquired).

In this lesson, we examine how the Hanafi school categorizes the conditions that diminish or remove a person's legal capacity. The classification into celestial (samāwiyyah) and acquired (muktasabah) provides the first layer for determining the effect on capacity of performance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of impediments
From the text, distinguish between celestial impediments (no human choice in their occurrence) and acquired impediments (human choice involved).
2
List the celestial impediments
Name the six celestial impediments: insanity, feeble-mindedness, unconsciousness, sleep, death-sickness, and slavery.
Slavery is considered celestial because the person does not choose to be enslaved originally.
3
List the acquired impediments
Name the acquired impediments: intoxication and foolishness, and note the addition of overwhelming debt by later jurists.
Debt was not originally considered an impediment but later fatwa made it so.
4
Understand the legal effect of classification
Note that this classification does not, by itself, produce a legal difference (thamarah); it is merely organizational.
The real effect comes from the specific rulings attached to each impediment.
