الباب الثامن : نظرية الأهلية والولاية
والفرق بين
العته والجنون أنَّ العَتَه ضعف في العقل ينشأ عنه ضعف في الوعي والإدراك أما الجنون فهو اختلال في العقل ينشأ عنه اضطراب
أو هيجان.
وقد اختلفوا في العته والجنون هل هما درجتان متفاوتتان من جنس واحد، أو هما حقيقتان مختلفتان؟ وعلى كل يتميّز المعتوه عندهم بالهدوء في أوضاعه بالنسبة إلى المجنون (۱) . (ر) أصول فخر الإسلام البزدوي، وشرحه الكشف ٢٧٤/٤ - ٢٧٥ ، ورد المحتار في كتاب الحجر، ورسالة الأهلية للأستاذ الشيخ أحمد إبراهيم ص ٢٣ - ٢٧).
٢/٦٥ - في أي الأهليتين تؤثر هذه العوارض؟
قدمنا فيما سلف من مباحث الأهلية أنَّ أهلية الوجوب مناطها الصفة الإنسانية دون العقل. فلكل شخص منذ ولادته أهلية وجوب كاملة يصلح بمقتضاها لثبوت الحقوق له وعليه وللجنين في رحم أمه أهلية وجوب ناقصة يصلح بمقتضاها لثبوت بعض من الحقوق له لا عليه (ر: ف ٣/٦٠ - ٤/٦٠). أما أهلية الأداء فمناطها العقل. فلذا لا تبدأ في الشخص حتى يصبح له تمييز.
يتضح من ذلك أنَّ هذه العوارض ينحصر تأثيرها في أهلية الأداء دون أهلية الوجوب، لأنَّ هذه العوارض مهما كان لها في حالة الشخص وفي ملكاته العقلية من تأثير لا تسلب عنه الصفة الإنسانية، ولا تجعله أدنى حالة من الطفل الوليد .
(۱) المبدأ العام في المجنون أنه يعتبر كالصغير غير المميز في جميع أحكامه . أما المعتوه فقد اعتبره الاجتهاد الحنفي في حكم الصغير المميز، فتخضع تصرفاته للتفصيل بحسب التقسيم الثلاثي المتقدم في طور التمييز ( ر ) المجلة / ۹۷۹ - ۹۸۰) .
لكن الأستاذ الشيخ أحمد إبراهيم رحمه الله قد حقق في رسالة الأهلية وقرر أن المعتوه على درجتين مميز، وغير مميز، وأن هذا الثاني كالمجنون في الأحكام (ر: رسالة الأهلية (ص/ (٢٦) وعلى هذا جاءت المادة / ٤٧/ من القانون المدني الجديد .
Chapter Sixty-Five: Impediments to Legal Capacity. 1/65 - Indeed, what has preceded regarding the stages of legal capacity, its rules, and its rulings all according to the phases of human development is based on the natural state in which a person is sound in development. However, physical or mental impediments may occur to persons, such as death-sickness and insanity, which have a total effect on their capacity, or a partial effect that diminishes it to varying degrees according to the type and nature of the impediment. These impediments are divided by scholars of Usul al-Fiqh into two types: celestial and acquired. Celestial impediments are those in whose creation the person has no choice. Acquired impediments are those in whose acquisition the person has choice (1). After that, celestial impediments are six: insanity, feeble-mindedness, unconsciousness, sleep, death-sickness, and slavery. Acquired impediments include: intoxication and foolishness. And we add to these acquired impediments another impediment: being in debt that consumes one's wealth or bankruptcy. (1) This division bears no fruit in rulings; rather it is apparently for mere arrangement. (2) Our jurists used not to mention this debt among the impediments to legal capacity, because debt, even if it consumes one's wealth, was not considered in the principles of the schools and juristic theories as a preventer of the debtor's disposal of his wealth. Then later jurists gave fatwa that it is a preventer, as previously indicated in paragraphs 7/15 and 6/38, and we will clarify it here shortly. Hence it must now be counted among impediments to legal capacity.

Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. And the difference between feeble-mindedness and insanity: feeble-mindedness is a weakness in the intellect resulting in weakness in awareness and perception, while insanity is a disturbance in the intellect resulting in disorder or agitation. And they differed regarding feeble-mindedness and insanity: are they two varying degrees of one kind, or two distinct realities? In any case, the feeble-minded person is distinguished by calmness in his states compared to the insane person (1). (See: Usul Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi and its Sharh al-Kashf 4/274-275, and Radd al-Muhtar in the Book of Hijr, and Risalah al-Ahliyyah by Shaykh Ahmad Ibrahim pp. 23-27). 2/65 - Which of the two capacities do these impediments affect? We previously presented in the discussions of legal capacity that the basis of capacity of obligation is human nature, not intellect. So every person from his birth has a complete capacity of obligation by virtue of which rights are established for and against him. The fetus in its mother's womb has an incomplete capacity of obligation by virtue of which some rights are established for him, not against him (see: 3/60-4/60). As for capacity of performance, its basis is intellect. Hence it does not begin in a person until he has discernment. From that it becomes clear that the effect of these impediments is confined to capacity of performance, not capacity of obligation, because these impediments, whatever effect they have on the person's state and his mental faculties, do not strip him of human nature, nor make him lower than a newborn infant. (1) The general principle regarding the insane person is that he is considered like a non-discerning minor in all his rulings. As for the feeble-minded person, Hanafi ijtihad considered him in the ruling of a discerning minor, so his transactions are subject to detail according to the threefold division in the stage of discernment (see: Majallah 979-980). However, Shaykh Ahmad Ibrahim (may Allah have mercy on him) verified in his treatise on legal capacity and concluded that the feeble-minded person has two degrees: discerning and non-discerning, and the latter is like the insane in rulings (see: Risalah al-Ahliyyah p. 26). And upon this came Article 47 of the new Civil Code.
Source pages p. 782

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of impediments (ʿawāriḍ) to legal capacity (ahliyyah). The author first explains that the previous discussion of capacity assumed a natural, sound state. Then he introduces impediments that can diminish or remove capacity, dividing them into celestial (samāwiyyah), beyond human control, and acquired (muktasabah), resulting from human action. He lists six celestial impediments: insanity (junūn), feeble-mindedness (ʿatah), unconsciousness (ighmā'), sleep (nawm), death-sickness (marad al-mawt), and slavery (riqq). The acquired impediments are intoxication (sukr) and foolishness (safah). The author adds a third acquired impediment: overwhelming debt or bankruptcy, noting that later jurists (mutā'akhkhirūn) considered it a preventer (māniʿ) of free disposal of wealth, even though earlier principles did not. Then he distinguishes between ʿatah (weakness of intellect causing weak awareness) and junūn (disturbance causing agitation). Scholars disagreed whether these are degrees of one type or different realities; the Hanafi view treats the insane like a non-discerning minor and the feeble-minded like a discerning minor with detailed rules. Finally, the author clarifies that all these impediments affect only the capacity of performance (ahliyyat al-adā'), not capacity of obligation (ahliyyat al-wujūb), because the latter is based on human nature (ṣifah insāniyyah) which is not removed by these conditions. Thus a person retains obligation capacity even when insane, unconscious, etc.

Key Terms
عارض الأهلية (ʿāriḍ al-ahliyyah): عارض جسمي أو عقلي يطرأ على الشخص فيؤثر في أهليته تأثيراً كلياً أو جزئياً.
سماوي (samāwī): عارض ليس للشخص اختيار في إيجاده.
مكتسب (muktasab): عارض للشخص في تحصيله اختيار.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Insanity (junūn)
B) Sleep (nawm)
C) Intoxication (sukr)

Key Takeaway
Impediments to legal capacity are divided into celestial (no choice) and acquired (choice), and this classification is merely organizational; the real effects are studied per impediment.

Effect of Impediments on Capacity of Performance and Hanafi Treatment of Insanity and Feeble-Mindedness
Effect of impediments on capacity and detailed rulings on junūn and ʿatah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why impediments affect only capacity of performance, and describe the Hanafi ruling for the insane (junūn) and the feeble-minded (ʿatīh) as akin to a non-discerning minor and a discerning minor, respectively.

Core Idea
Impediments strip capacity of performance but leave capacity of obligation intact; insane like a child, feeble-minded like a child with discernment.

In this continuation, we delve into the core principle of Hanafi capacity theory: impediments impact only the ability to perform valid legal acts (ahliyyat al-adā'), not the passive capacity to bear rights and obligations (ahliyyat al-wujūb). We also examine the detailed Hanafi classification of the insane and the feeble-minded, a ruling that directly affects the validity of their contracts and transactions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two types of capacity
Remember that ahliyyat al-wujūb is based on human nature and is complete for every living person (except fetus has incomplete), while ahliyyat al-adā' is based on intellect and discernment.
2
Identify the principle of effect
From the text, note the statement: 'the effect of these impediments is confined to capacity of performance, not capacity of obligation.' Understand why: impediments cannot remove human nature.
This is a fundamental qawida in Hanafi jurisprudence.
3
Analyze the Hanafi treatment of the insane
The insane person is considered like a non-discerning minor: his actions are totally invalid (batil) if financial, and his worship is not required.
General principle: al-majnūn ka al-ṣaghīr ghayr al-mumayyiz.
4
Analyze the Hanafi treatment of the feeble-minded
The feeble-minded (maʿtūh) is considered like a discerning minor: his transactions are subject to the threefold division (valid with guardian's permission, etc.).
However, there is a later revision that ʿatah has two degrees: one is like insane (non-discerning), the other like discerning minor.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a person with ʿatah, determine his capacity based on whether he has any discernment.
Use the modern Hanafi codes as guidance.

الفصل الخامس والستون
عوارض الأهلية
١/٦٥ - إنَّ ما تقدم من مراحل الأهلية وقواعدها وأحكامها جميع بحسب أطوار نشأة الإنسان إنما هو بناء على الحالة الطبيعية التي يكون فيها الشخص سليم النشأة.
غير أنه قد يطرأ على الأشخاص عوارض جسمية أو عقلية، كمرض الموت، والجنون يعتبر لها في أهليتهم تأثير كلي، أو تأثير جزئي ينقص منها نقصاً تتفاوت درجاته بحسب نوع العارض وطبيعته .
هذه العوارض يقسمها علماء أصول الفقه إلى قسمين : سماوية، ومكتسبة. فالعوارض السماوية هي ما ليس للشخص اختيار في إيجادها. والمكتسبة هي ما يكون له في تحصيلها اختيار (۱) .

العته والجنون أنَّ العَتَه ضعف في العقل ينشأ عنه ضعف في الوعي والإدراك أما الجنون فهو اختلال في العقل ينشأ عنه اضطراب
أو هيجان.
وقد اختلفوا في العته والجنون هل هما درجتان متفاوتتان من جنس واحد، أو هما حقيقتان مختلفتان؟ وعلى كل يتميّز المعتوه عندهم بالهدوء في أوضاعه بالنسبة إلى المجنون (۱) . (ر) أصول فخر الإسلام البزدوي، وشرحه الكشف ٢٧٤/٤ - ٢٧٥ ، ورد المحتار في كتاب الحجر، ورسالة الأهلية للأستاذ الشيخ أحمد إبراهيم ص ٢٣ - ٢٧).
٢/٦٥ - في أي الأهليتين تؤثر هذه العوارض؟
قدمنا فيما سلف من مباحث الأهلية أنَّ أهلية الوجوب مناطها الصفة الإنسانية دون العقل. فلكل شخص منذ ولادته أهلية وجوب كاملة يصلح بمقتضاها لثبوت الحقوق له وعليه وللجنين في رحم أمه أهلية وجوب ناقصة يصلح بمقتضاها لثبوت بعض من الحقوق له لا عليه (ر: ف ٣/٦٠ - ٤/٦٠). أما أهلية الأداء فمناطها العقل. فلذا لا تبدأ في الشخص حتى يصبح له تمييز.
يتضح من ذلك أنَّ هذه العوارض ينحصر تأثيرها في أهلية الأداء دون أهلية الوجوب، لأنَّ هذه العوارض مهما كان لها في حالة الشخص وفي ملكاته العقلية من تأثير لا تسلب عنه الصفة الإنسانية، ولا تجعله أدنى حالة من الطفل الوليد .
(۱) المبدأ العام في المجنون أنه يعتبر كالصغير غير المميز في جميع أحكامه . أما المعتوه فقد اعتبره الاجتهاد الحنفي في حكم الصغير المميز، فتخضع تصرفاته للتفصيل بحسب التقسيم الثلاثي المتقدم في طور التمييز ( ر ) المجلة / ۹۷۹ - ۹۸۰) .
لكن الأستاذ الشيخ أحمد إبراهيم رحمه الله قد حقق في رسالة الأهلية وقرر أن المعتوه على درجتين مميز، وغير مميز، وأن هذا الثاني كالمجنون في الأحكام (ر: رسالة الأهلية (ص/ (٢٦) وعلى هذا جاءت المادة / ٤٧/ من القانون المدني الجديد .
Chapter Sixty-Five: Impediments to Legal Capacity. 1/65 - Indeed, what has preceded regarding the stages of legal capacity, its rules, and its rulings all according to the phases of human development is based on the natural state in which a person is sound in development. However, physical or mental impediments may occur to persons, such as death-sickness and insanity, which have a total effect on their capacity, or a partial effect that diminishes it to varying degrees according to the type and nature of the impediment. These impediments are divided by scholars of Usul al-Fiqh into two types: celestial and acquired. Celestial impediments are those in whose creation the person has no choice. Acquired impediments are those in whose acquisition the person has choice (1). After that, celestial impediments are six: insanity, feeble-mindedness, unconsciousness, sleep, death-sickness, and slavery. Acquired impediments include: intoxication and foolishness. And we add to these acquired impediments another impediment: being in debt that consumes one's wealth or bankruptcy. (1) This division bears no fruit in rulings; rather it is apparently for mere arrangement. (2) Our jurists used not to mention this debt among the impediments to legal capacity, because debt, even if it consumes one's wealth, was not considered in the principles of the schools and juristic theories as a preventer of the debtor's disposal of his wealth. Then later jurists gave fatwa that it is a preventer, as previously indicated in paragraphs 7/15 and 6/38, and we will clarify it here shortly. Hence it must now be counted among impediments to legal capacity.

Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. And the difference between feeble-mindedness and insanity: feeble-mindedness is a weakness in the intellect resulting in weakness in awareness and perception, while insanity is a disturbance in the intellect resulting in disorder or agitation. And they differed regarding feeble-mindedness and insanity: are they two varying degrees of one kind, or two distinct realities? In any case, the feeble-minded person is distinguished by calmness in his states compared to the insane person (1). (See: Usul Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi and its Sharh al-Kashf 4/274-275, and Radd al-Muhtar in the Book of Hijr, and Risalah al-Ahliyyah by Shaykh Ahmad Ibrahim pp. 23-27). 2/65 - Which of the two capacities do these impediments affect? We previously presented in the discussions of legal capacity that the basis of capacity of obligation is human nature, not intellect. So every person from his birth has a complete capacity of obligation by virtue of which rights are established for and against him. The fetus in its mother's womb has an incomplete capacity of obligation by virtue of which some rights are established for him, not against him (see: 3/60-4/60). As for capacity of performance, its basis is intellect. Hence it does not begin in a person until he has discernment. From that it becomes clear that the effect of these impediments is confined to capacity of performance, not capacity of obligation, because these impediments, whatever effect they have on the person's state and his mental faculties, do not strip him of human nature, nor make him lower than a newborn infant. (1) The general principle regarding the insane person is that he is considered like a non-discerning minor in all his rulings. As for the feeble-minded person, Hanafi ijtihad considered him in the ruling of a discerning minor, so his transactions are subject to detail according to the threefold division in the stage of discernment (see: Majallah 979-980). However, Shaykh Ahmad Ibrahim (may Allah have mercy on him) verified in his treatise on legal capacity and concluded that the feeble-minded person has two degrees: discerning and non-discerning, and the latter is like the insane in rulings (see: Risalah al-Ahliyyah p. 26). And upon this came Article 47 of the new Civil Code.
Source pages p. 782

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of impediments (ʿawāriḍ) to legal capacity (ahliyyah). The author first explains that the previous discussion of capacity assumed a natural, sound state. Then he introduces impediments that can diminish or remove capacity, dividing them into celestial (samāwiyyah), beyond human control, and acquired (muktasabah), resulting from human action. He lists six celestial impediments: insanity (junūn), feeble-mindedness (ʿatah), unconsciousness (ighmā'), sleep (nawm), death-sickness (marad al-mawt), and slavery (riqq). The acquired impediments are intoxication (sukr) and foolishness (safah). The author adds a third acquired impediment: overwhelming debt or bankruptcy, noting that later jurists (mutā'akhkhirūn) considered it a preventer (māniʿ) of free disposal of wealth, even though earlier principles did not. Then he distinguishes between ʿatah (weakness of intellect causing weak awareness) and junūn (disturbance causing agitation). Scholars disagreed whether these are degrees of one type or different realities; the Hanafi view treats the insane like a non-discerning minor and the feeble-minded like a discerning minor with detailed rules. Finally, the author clarifies that all these impediments affect only the capacity of performance (ahliyyat al-adā'), not capacity of obligation (ahliyyat al-wujūb), because the latter is based on human nature (ṣifah insāniyyah) which is not removed by these conditions. Thus a person retains obligation capacity even when insane, unconscious, etc.

Key Terms
أهلية الوجوب (ahliyyat al-wujūb): صلاحية الشخص لثبوت الحقوق له وعليه، مناطها الصفة الإنسانية، ولا تتأثر بالعوارض.
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-adā'): صلاحية الشخص لصحة تصرفاته ونفاذها، مناطها العقل والتمييز، وتتأثر بالعوارض.
الجنون (al-junūn): اختلال في العقل ينشأ عنه اضطراب أو هيجان، يزيل أهلية الأداء بالكلية.
العته (al-ʿatah): ضعف في العقل ينشأ عنه ضعف في الوعي والإدراك مع هدوء بالنسبة للمجنون.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He is treated like a discerning minor; his contracts are suspended.
B) He is treated like an insane person; his contracts are void.

Key Takeaway
Impediments only affect capacity of performance, and the Hanafi school treats the insane as non-discerning minors and the feeble-minded as either non-discerning (like insane) or discerning (like discerning minors) depending on their mental state.

Legal Capacity and Its Impediments: The Dual Nature
Defining 'atah and junun, distinguishing ahliyyah types, and general principles of impediments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ahliyyat al-wujub and ahliyyat al-ada', explain their respective bases (humanity vs. intellect), and apply the principle that impediments like 'atah and junun only affect capacity for performance.

Core Idea
Legal capacity splits into obligation (humanity-based) and performance (intellect-based); impediments only affect performance.

Legal capacity is the foundation of all shari'i obligations and transactions. But it is not a single, monolithic concept. This lesson unpacks the two-tiered structure of capacity as presented by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa: capacity for obligation, rooted in mere humanity, and capacity for performance, rooted in intellect. We then explore how impediments like imbecility ('atah) and insanity (junun) impair only the latter, leaving the former untouched. Along the way, we define these impediments, examine a classical intra-Hanafi dispute on grading imbecility, and lay the groundwork for applying these principles in fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
1. Recognize the two capacities
Identify the two distinct types of ahliyyah defined in the passage: ahliyyat al-wujub (capacity for obligation) and ahliyyat al-ada' (capacity for performance). Note their different foundations: the first is based on the human attribute (as-sifah al-insaniyyah), the second on intellect (al-'aql).
Capacity for obligation is complete for every living person (except the fetus, who has incomplete capacity for rights only).
2
2. Define 'atah and junun
Extract the definitions from the text: 'atah is a weakness in the intellect resulting in reduced awareness, while junun is a derangement causing disturbance or agitation. Note the distinguishing feature: the imbecile (ma'tuh) is relatively calm compared to the insane (majnun).
There is a debate whether they are two degrees of one condition or completely different realities.
3
3. Understand the effect on capacities
Apply the key principle: these impediments affect only ahliyyat al-ada' (performance capacity). They do not strip a person of humanity, so ahliyyat al-wujub remains intact. An insane person is like a non-discerning minor; an imbecile, according to the majority Hanafi view, is like a discerning minor.
The passage emphasizes that even the most severe impediment cannot make a person lower than a newborn in terms of obligation capacity.
4
4. Distinguish removing vs. diminishing impediments
Note the classification: some impediments (like junun) remove capacity entirely; others (like marad al-mawt) diminish it, leaving partial capacity. The removing impediment returns the person to the stage of non-discerning childhood; the diminishing impediment returns them to the stage of discernment.
This principle guides the mufti in determining the validity of transactions by affected persons.
5
5. Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on imbecility
The passage notes that while the majority Hanafi view treats the imbecile as a discerning minor, Sheikh Ahmad Ibrahim distinguished two degrees: discerning and non-discerning, the latter being like the insane. This later view was adopted in modern civil codes (e.g., Article 47).
This illustrates the living tradition of Hanafi jurisprudence and its capacity to evolve within principles.

(ر) أصول فخر الإسلام البزدوي، وشرحه الكشف ٢٧٤/٤ - ٢٧٥ ، ورد المحتار في كتاب الحجر، ورسالة الأهلية للأستاذ الشيخ أحمد إبراهيم ص ٢٣ - ٢٧). ٢/٦٥ - في أي الأهليتين تؤثر هذه العوارض؟ قدمنا فيما سلف من مباحث الأهلية أنَّ أهلية الوجوب مناطها الصفة الإنسانية دون العقل. فلكل شخص منذ ولادته أهلية وجوب كاملة يصلح بمقتضاها لثبوت الحقوق له وعليه وللجنين في رحم أمه أهلية وجوب ناقصة يصلح بمقتضاها لثبوت بعض من الحقوق له لا عليه (ر: ف ٣/٦٠ - ٤/٦٠). أما أهلية الأداء فمناطها العقل. فلذا لا تبدأ في الشخص حتى يصبح له تمييز. يتضح من ذلك أنَّ هذه العوارض ينحصر تأثيرها في أهلية الأداء دون أهلية الوجوب، لأنَّ هذه العوارض مهما كان لها في حالة الشخص وفي ملكاته العقلية من تأثير لا تسلب عنه الصفة الإنسانية، ولا تجعله أدنى حالة من الطفل الوليد . Chapter Eight: Theory of Capacity and Guardianship. The difference between imbecility (al-'atah) and insanity (al-junun) is that imbecility is a weakness in the intellect resulting in weakness in awareness and perception, while insanity is a derangement in the intellect resulting in disturbance or agitation. They differed whether imbecility and insanity are two varying degrees of one kind or two different realities. In any case, the imbecile is distinguished by calmness in his conditions relative to the insane. (Reference: Usul Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi and its commentary al-Kashf 4/274-275, Radd al-Muhtar in Kitab al-Hajr, and Risalah al-Ahliyyah by Sheikh Ahmad Ibrahim pp. 23-27). 2/65 - Which of the two capacities do these impediments affect? We have previously presented in the discussions of capacity that capacity for obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) is based on human attribute without intellect. So every person from his birth has complete capacity for obligation by virtue of which rights are established for him and against him; and the fetus in its mother's womb has incomplete capacity for obligation by virtue of which some rights are established for him but not against him (cf. 3/60-4/60). As for capacity for performance (ahliyyat al-ada'), it is based on intellect. Hence it does not begin in a person until he attains discernment. It becomes clear from this that these impediments are limited in their effect to capacity for performance, not capacity for obligation, because these impediments, no matter their effect on the person's condition and mental faculties, do not strip him of human attribute, nor do they make him in a lower state than a newborn child. (1) The general principle regarding the insane is that he is considered like a non-discerning minor in all his rulings. As for the imbecile, Hanafi ijtihad considered him in the ruling of a discerning minor, so his dispositions are subject to detail according to the triple division previously mentioned in the stage of discernment (cf. Majalla articles 979-980). But Professor Sheikh Ahmad Ibrahim, may Allah have mercy on him, verified in Risalah al-Ahliyyah and decided that the imbecile has two degrees: discerning and non-discerning, and that the latter is like the insane in rulings (cf. Risalah al-Ahliyyah p. 26). And upon this came Article 47 of the new Civil Code. 834 Chapter (65): Impediments of Capacity: Rulings. And each impediment of capacity has a particular effect giving rise to exceptional rulings for the dispositions of persons who are affected by the impediment, which are excepted from the general shari'i rulings that apply to others. Some of these impediments remove capacity for performance entirely, like insanity, and some diminish it, leaving it deficient in certain limits, like death sickness (marad al-mawt). And the general principle regarding the effect of each of these two types of impediments of capacity is that the impediment that removes capacity returns the person to the equivalent of the previous stage of childhood; and the impediment that diminishes capacity returns the person to the equivalent of the stage of discernment, so the rulings of the discerning person apply to him. 3/65 - This is a definition, division, and general presentation of the impediments of capacity. As for the detailed effects of each of these impediments and their rulings, that is beyond the purpose of our discussion here, and it is elaborated in books of Usul al-Fiqh and its branches. Books of Usul mention them in the discussion of capacity and its impediments from the topics of al-mahkum 'alayhi (the person subject to law), who is the accountable person addressed by shari'i rulings; and books of Furu' mention them scattered in their various contexts in the fiqh chapters, such as in the book of purity, prayer, divorce, interdiction, and others. The scholar Zayn al-'Abidin Ibn Nujaym also undertook to explain many of them in the section on rulings from his book al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, which is its third art. However, we will single out two impediments for some clarification in what follows: death sickness from the divine impediments and indebtedness from the acquired impediments. (1) The person in the terminology of Islamic Usul al-Fiqh is described as al-mahkum 'alayhi because he is subject to the binding shari'i rulings that the Lawgiver requires of him. 835
Source pages p. 783

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa is a foundational text on the theory of legal capacity (ahliyyah) in Islamic jurisprudence. The author begins Chapter Eight by distinguishing between two impediments: imbecility ('atah) and insanity (junun). 'Atah is a weakness in the intellect leading to reduced awareness, while junun is a derangement leading to disturbance or agitation. There is scholarly disagreement whether these are degrees of the same condition or entirely different realities. The imbecile is generally calmer than the insane. The passage then addresses which type of capacity is affected by these impediments. Capacity for obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) is rooted in the mere fact of being human, independent of intellect; it is complete for every living person (except the fetus who has partial capacity for rights only). Capacity for performance (ahliyyat al-ada'), however, requires intellect and begins with discernment (tamyiz). Thus, impediments like insanity and imbecility only affect capacity for performance, not obligation capacity, because they do not negate humanity. The general principle is that an insane person is like a non-discerning minor, while according to the majority Hanafi view, an imbecile is like a discerning minor, subject to the three-part classification of dispositions (valid, void, suspended). However, Sheikh Ahmad Ibrahim distinguished two types of imbeciles: discerning and non-discerning, the latter like an insane person. This is adopted in modern civil codes. The chapter also explains that impediments either remove capacity entirely (like insanity) or diminish it (like death sickness). The removing impediment returns the person to the stage of childhood (no valid dispositions); the diminishing impediment returns the person to the stage of discernment (valid dispositions subject to guardian's consent). The author notes that detailed rulings of each impediment are covered in both Usul and Furu' books, and Ibn Nujaym's Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir covers many. The author will focus on two: death sickness (divine impediment) and indebtedness (acquired impediment).

Key Terms
أهلية الوجوب (ahliyyat al-wujub): Capacity to have rights and obligations based on human attribute, present from birth.
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-ada'): Capacity to perform legal acts based on intellect and discernment.
العته (al-'atah): A weakness in intellect causing reduced awareness, distinguished from insanity by calmness.
الجنون (al-junun): A derangement of intellect causing disturbance or agitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how is an imbecile (ma'tuh) generally treated in terms of legal capacity?
A) Like a non-discerning minor, with no valid dispositions.
B) Like a discerning minor, with dispositions subject to the three-part classification.
C) Like a sane adult, with full capacity.
D) Like a person under interdiction for prodigality.

Key Takeaway
Legal capacity is dual: obligation capacity (ahliyyat al-wujub) is inherent to humanity and unaffected by mental impediments; performance capacity (ahliyyat al-ada') depends on intellect and is removed or diminished by impediments like 'atah and junun.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Definition and Legal Theory of Deathbed Illness (Marad al-Mawt)
Defines marad al-mawt and explains its legal theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions defining marad al-mawt and apply its legal effects (partial interdiction) to a hypothetical fatwa scenario.

Core Idea
A deathbed illness partially interdicts the patient, protecting creditors and heirs.

In Hanafi fiqh, a person's final illness is not just a medical condition but a distinct legal category. This lesson unpacks the precise definition of marad al-mawt and the reasoning behind the special rulings that attach to it. Understanding this definition is critical for a mufti who must decide when a patient's voluntary dispositions are limited and when debts take precedence.

Guided Path
1
Isolate the three definition components
Identify the three conditions: (1) incapacitation from normal activities (outdoor for men, indoor for women), (2) connection to death (the illness is followed by death without full recovery), (3) the illness does not persist unchanged for a full year. If it remains static for a year or more, it is chronic, not marad al-mawt.
Note that bed confinement is not required.
2
Distinguish legal from linguistic usage
Recognize that in legal texts, 'patient' (marid) specifically means one in marad al-mawt, and 'healthy' (sahih) means anyone not in that state, including those with minor illness. This is a term-of-art shift: 'patient' is the general term used for the specific, and 'healthy' is the specific used for the general.
This distinction prevents confusion when reading fiqh manuals.
3
Derive sabab, shart, and mani from the definition
Using the legal_definition_dissector: sabab (cause) is the incapacitating illness; shart (condition) is that it is connected to death and not unchanged for a year; mani (preventer) is recovery or static persistence for a year or more. The hukm is partial interdiction.
These elements structure the legal category.
4
Analyze the theory behind marad al-mawt
The illness is a warning of death, which triggers two processes: (1) the patient's legal personality begins to dissolve, and (2) the rights of creditors and heirs become attached to the estate. Debts shift from liability-only to liability-plus-assets; the estate becomes mortgaged for debts.
This theory explains why dispositions are restricted.
5
Apply the interdiction rules
The patient is interdicted absolutely toward creditors (no limit on their claims) and interdicted limited to two-thirds of wealth toward heirs (so voluntary gifts exceeding one-third are invalid unless heirs approve). This balances the patient's freedom to will with the rights of creditors and heirs.
The one-third limit applies only to bequests and gifts in marad al-mawt.

الباب الثامن : نظرية الأهلية والولاية
٤/٦٥ - مرض الموت تعريفه ونظريته :
تعريفه :
مرض الموت هو المرض الذي يعجز الرجل عن ممارسة أعماله المعتادة خارج البيت ويعجز المرأة عن أعمالها المعتادة داخل البيت ويتصل به الموت دون أن يستمر سنة كاملة على حال واحدة من غير ازدياد .
فلا فرق بين أن يلزمه الفراش أو لا، ولا فرق أيضاً أن يموت بين الإنسان من نفس المرض أو يموت بسبب آخر في خلال المرض.. فلو لم يتصل به الموت بل شُفي منه ثم مات لا يكون المرض السابق مرض موت بل حكمه كحكم الصحة.
وكذا لو استمر سنة فأكثر على حال واحدة لا يتزايد فإنه عندئذ يعتبر حالة مزمنة حكمها كحكم الصحة. أما إذا كان يتزايد ولو تزايداً بطيئاً خفيفاً فإنه يُعتبر مرض موت من أوله ولو دام سنين كثيرة.
وإذا استمر سنة فأكثر دون تزايد ثم تزايد واتصل به الموت، اعتبر مرض موت من تاريخ اشتداده (المجلة/ ١٥٩٥).
ويلحظ في الاصطلاح الشرعي أنه حيثما أطلق الفقهاء لفظي المريض والصحيح فإنما مرادهم بالمريض من هو في مرض الموت، وبالصحيح من ليس في مرض الموت، سواءً أكان غير مريض أصلاً أو مريضاً بغير مرض الموت. فهم عند الإطلاق إنما يريدون المرض والصحة الشرعيين اللذين تختلف بينهما الأحكام، لا معناهما اللغوي.
وهذا من قبيل إطلاق الأعم على الأخص في «المريض وإطلاق الأخص على الأعم في «الصحيح».
نظرية مرض الموت:
إن النظرية الشرعية في مرض الموت ترتكز على اعتبار أن هذا المرض إنذار بحادثة الموت التي تنتهي بها الشخصية والأهلية، ويرتب الشرعُ عليها أحكاماً جديدة منها الإرث وحلول الديون التي على الميت إذ ينتقل تعلقها من ذمَّة المدين إلى أمواله لخراب ذمته بالموت، فتصبح تركته كالمرهونة شرعاً بالديون.
فمرض الموت مقدّمة إنذارية لنتيجتين حقوقيتين سلبية وإيجابية سيكون حادث الموت المنذر به سبباً لهما :
١ـ فهو مقدمة لزوال شخصية المريض وانسلاخ أهليته وملكيته.
٢ـ وهو أيضاً مقدمة لثبوت الحقوق العينية في أموال المريض لمن ستنتقل إليهم هذه الأموال بعد موته من دائنين أو ورثة. ففي مرض الموت بدء ذاك الزوال وهذا الثبوت معاً صيانة للحقوق التي أثبتها الشرع في التركة لهاتين الزمرتين (الدائنين والورثة).
وينشأ عن ذلك أنَّ الديون تصبح متعلقة بمال المريض وذمته جميعاً، بعد أن كانت متعلقة قبل المرض بذمته فقط، وذلك لعجزه عن السعي والاكتساب، فتضعف ذمته، فيضم إليها ماله توثيقاً.
والشرع قد أطلق للإنسان أن يتصرف بثلث ما سيتركه من مال تصرفاً مضافاً إلى ما بعد موته بالوصيَّة ونحوها فيما يشاء من وجوه الخير التي يبتغي بها ثواب الآخرة.
وقد ألحق الشرع حالة المرض الأخير من هذه الناحية بالموت. فالتصرف خلالها بالتبرع وما في حكمه، ولو مُنجزاً، يُعتبر كالتصرف المضاف إلى ما بعد الموت.
فمن هنا يعتبر المريض بمرض الموت محجوراً بالنسبة إلى الدائنين حَجْراً عاماً مطلقاً غير مقيد بحد محدود من ماله ومحجوراً بالنسبة إلى الورثة حجراً محدوداً بثلثي ماله.
وعلى هذا فالمريض بمرض الموت إما أن يكون مديناً أو غير مدين
Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship.
4/65 - Deathbed Illness: Its Definition and Theory.
Its Definition:
Deathbed illness is the illness that incapacitates a man from performing his usual activities outside the home and incapacitates a woman from her usual activities inside the home, and it is connected with death, without continuing for a complete year in a single state without increase.
Thus, there is no difference whether he is confined to bed or not, and there is also no difference whether the person dies from that same illness or dies from another cause during the illness. If it is not connected with death, but he recovers from it and then dies, the previous illness is not a deathbed illness; rather its ruling is like that of health.
Similarly, if it continues for a year or more in a single state without increasing, then it is considered a chronic condition, and its ruling is like that of health. As for if it is increasing, even if slowly and slightly, then it is considered a deathbed illness from its beginning, even if it lasts for many years.
And if it continues for a year or more without increase, then it increases and is connected with death, it is considered a deathbed illness from the date of its severity (Majalla/1595).
And it is noted in legal terminology that wherever the jurists use the terms 'patient' and 'healthy', they mean by 'patient' one who is in a deathbed illness, and by 'healthy' one who is not in a deathbed illness, whether he is not ill at all or ill with a non-deathbed illness. In absolute usage, they mean the legal illness and health between which rulings differ, not their linguistic meanings.
This is of the type of using the general for the specific in 'patient' and using the specific for the general in 'healthy'.
Theory of Deathbed Illness:
Indeed, the legal theory regarding deathbed illness is based on considering that this illness is a warning of the event of death, which terminates personality and legal capacity. The Lawgiver attaches to it new rulings, including inheritance and the acceleration of debts owed by the deceased, as their attachment transfers from the debtor's liability to his assets due to the destruction of his liability by death, so his estate becomes as if legally mortgaged for debts.
Thus, deathbed illness is a warning preface for two juristic outcomes, negative and positive, for which the warned death event will be the cause:
1. It is a preface for the elimination of the patient's personality and the stripping of his capacity and ownership.
2. It is also a preface for the establishment of real rights in the patient's assets for those to whom these assets will transfer after his death, whether creditors or heirs. So during deathbed illness, both that elimination and this establishment begin together, safeguarding the rights that the Lawgiver has established in the estate for these two groups (creditors and heirs).
This results in debts becoming attached to both the patient's assets and his liability, after they were attached before the illness only to his liability, due to his inability to strive and earn. Thus his liability weakens, so his assets are joined to it as security.
And the Lawgiver has permitted a person to dispose of one-third of what he will leave of wealth, in a disposition added to after his death, by will and the like, in whatever charitable ways he wishes, seeking the reward of the Hereafter.
And the Lawgiver has annexed the state of final illness in this regard to death. Thus, voluntary dispositions during it and things in their ruling, even if immediate, are considered like dispositions added to after death.
Hence, the patient with a deathbed illness is considered interdicted with respect to creditors by a general absolute interdiction not limited to a specific limit of his wealth, and interdicted with respect to heirs by an interdiction limited to two-thirds of his wealth.
Thus, the patient with a deathbed illness is either a debtor or not a debtor.
Source pages p. 785

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa discusses the definition and legal theory of marad al-mawt (deathbed illness). The author first defines marad al-mawt as an illness that prevents a man from his usual outdoor activities and a woman from her usual indoor activities, and that is connected to death without lasting a full year unchanged. The key conditions are: (1) incapacitation relative to normal activities, (2) connection to death (not recovery), and (3) not remaining static for a full year. If the illness persists without increase for a year or more, it is considered chronic and treated like health. But if it is progressive, even slowly, it is deemed deathbed illness from the start. The author then notes that in legal terminology, 'patient' (marid) specifically means one in marad al-mawt, and 'healthy' (sahih) means one not in marad al-mawt, regardless of actual health. The theory behind marad al-mawt is that it serves as a warning of death, which triggers legal effects: the patient's personality and capacity begin to end, and rights of creditors and heirs become attached to his estate. Therefore, debts become secured against both the person and his assets, and the patient is partially interdicted: absolutely for creditors (no limit) and limited to two-thirds for heirs (so voluntary dispositions like gifts are limited to one-third of the estate). This ensures that the rights of creditors and heirs are protected during the final illness.

Key Terms
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): An illness that incapacitates the person from normal activities, is connected to death, and does not persist unchanged for a full year.
مريض (marid): In legal usage, a person in a state of deathbed illness (marad al-mawt).
صحيح (sahih): In legal usage, a person not in a state of deathbed illness, whether completely healthy or suffering from a non-terminal illness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) If it shows any increase, even slight, and is connected to death.
C) If the patient is bedridden for the entire year.
D) If the patient dies exactly after one year.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of marad al-mawt hinges on incapacitation, connection to death, and non-static progression; it triggers partial interdiction to protect creditors and heirs.

Indebtedness and Legal Capacity: The Original Rule and the Later Restriction
A legal philosophy text explaining the evolution of a capacity rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the original Hanafi ruling on the effect of debt on capacity and identify the later modification based on sadd al-dhara'i'.

Core Idea
Debt attaches to the dhimma, not property; later jurists restricted dispositions to block fraud.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm explores a key legal philosophy question: does debt restrict a person's capacity to dispose of property? The original juristic view across schools says no – debts are on the dhimma, not on specific assets. However, later Hanafi jurists noticed this rule was abused by debtors to defraud creditors. They responded by blocking the loophole through the principle of sadd al-dhara'i', modifying the ruling for cases of overwhelming debt. This lesson unpacks that evolution.

Guided Path
1
State the original ruling
Identify the default position regarding a debtor's capacity to dispose of property according to the original juristic view.
Most schools, including early Hanafis, held that debt does not affect capacity.
2
Understand the rationale
Explain why debts are linked to the dhimma (liability) rather than specific property, and why capacity remains intact.
The dhimma is a receptacle for obligations; property is not automatically encumbered.
3
Identify the abuse
Describe the fraudulent practices that emerged over time, such as gifts, endowments, and sales at undervalue to evade creditors.
Debtors exploited the original rule to hide assets.
4
Describe the later modification
State the ruling of later Hanafi jurists: dispositions aimed at evading creditors are suspended pending their consent.
This modification applies even if the debtor is not formally interdicted by a judge.
5
Recognize the legal principle
Identify sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking pretexts) as the underlying principle for this modification.
The later jurists used this principle to close the loophole.

(۱) تقدم بيان معنى الذمة» فلينظر (ف ١٠/٥٩).
Chapter (65) Impediments to Legal Capacity. 5/65 - Indebtedness: Debts that encumber a person, whatever their amount, whether they cover his property or not, originally in the juristic view of most jurists of Sharia had no effect on the debtor's capacity to dispose of his property. This is because debts are attached only to the debtor's liability (dhimma) [unclear: 'У' (1)] not to his specific property, so his property remains free, not burdened with anyone's right. Thus his capacity to dispose of it remains complete and unimpaired, so the debtor's dispositions in all his property are effective. And a person, through his economic activity, may increase the wealth in his hands by good investment and management, so he pays off his debts and builds wealth, and turns from bankruptcy to riches. Further elaboration and reasoning for this juristic view will come in the third part of this juristic series under the discussion of the theory of 'dhimma'. See Fath al-Qadir Sharh al-Hidayah by Kamal ibn al-Humam in the book of Waqf 5/424, and al-Is'af, chapter on terms of waqf and its people, and its place, and Radd al-Muhtar 3/395. But over time, people took this ruling as a loophole for fraud and a pretext resorted to by debtors to smuggle their assets, taking advantage of the right to dispose. So the debtor, out of spite for the creditor, would endow his property to charitable causes or to his descendants and after them to a charitable cause, or give his property as gifts to those he trusts from his relatives or friends, or sell his property at a low price showing favoritism to the buyer, all in order to remove his property from his ownership to those he trusts to prevent the creditor from collecting his debt. When the later jurists noticed this, they turned to block this pretext and issued fatwas that a debtor with overwhelming debt, even if not interdicted by a judge, if he disposes in a way that leads to smuggling his assets from the creditors, such as giving gifts and endowments of all or some of his property, then his disposition is not effective but rather depends on the consent of the creditors. (1) The meaning of 'dhimma' has been explained previously; refer to (F 10/59).
Source pages p. 788

Commentary
This passage addresses the effect of debt on a person's legal capacity (أهلية التصرف) to conduct financial transactions—a key issue in Hanafi fatwa practice. The original juristic theory holds that debts do not impair capacity because they are attached to the debtor's liability (ذمة) rather than to his specific assets. Consequently, the debtor retains full freedom to manage his property, potentially even increasing it to pay off debts. This principle is consistent across most schools and is considered a foundational view in the Hanafi madhhab. However, the author notes a practical problem: over time, debtors exploited this freedom to defraud creditors by fraudulently transferring assets through gifts, endowments, or sales at undervalue. To prevent this abuse (سد الذريعة), later Hanafi jurists modified the rule: if a debtor is in overwhelming debt (دين مستغرق), any disposition aimed at evading creditors is suspended pending their approval. This is not a change in the original legal capacity but a restriction to protect rights. The discussion underscores the dynamic nature of fiqh, where rulings adapt to changing circumstances while preserving core principles.

Key Terms
أهلية التصرف (ahliyyat al-tasarruf): Legal capacity to perform valid financial dispositions, such as sales, gifts, and endowments.
ذمة (dhimma): The legal personality or liability of a person, to which debts and obligations attach, distinct from specific property.
تهريب الأموال (tahrib al-amwal): Smuggling assets out of one's ownership to avoid creditors, often through gifts or sham sales.
سد الذرائع (sadd al-dhara'i'): Blocking pretexts; a legal principle that prohibits seemingly permissible acts that lead to prohibited outcomes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Definition and Necessity of Guardianship
Defining guardianship and its necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-wilayah (guardianship) and explain why it is necessary for minors who lack full legal capacity.

Core Idea
Guardianship is the universal legal solution to protect minors who cannot manage their own affairs.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of guardianship (al-wilayah) in Islamic law. You will learn who a minor (qasir) is, why minors need guardians, and how jurists define guardianship both linguistically and technically. This sets the stage for understanding the detailed rulings on guardianship that follow.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two categories of minors
The passage divides minors into two types: those completely lacking capacity (non-discerning child) and those with deficient capacity (between discernment and maturity). Grasp that both fall under the term 'qasir'.
The distinction affects the degree of guardianship needed but not the necessity itself.
2
Identify the problem: the minor's inability
Minors need the same necessities as adults but cannot provide for themselves due to physical and mental weakness. This inability is the reason for their deficient civil capacity.
The problem is universal, not specific to Islam.
3
Recognize the universal solution: guardianship
All divine and positive laws have agreed on guardianship as the sole method to ensure the minor's well-being. Understand that this agreement ('ittifaq) gives the concept authority.
This indicates guardianship is a necessary institution, not optional.
4
Define al-wilayah linguistically and technically
Linguistically, wilayah means assistance and authority. Technically among jurists, it is the undertaking of a major adult person over a minor in managing personal and financial affairs. Memorize both definitions.
The technical definition includes both personal (e.g., marriage, education) and financial matters.

Chapter Sixty-Six: Guardianship, or Legal Agency. 1/66 - The human before completing all his capacity of performance is called a minor, whether he is lacking all this capacity like a non-discerning child, or has deficient capacity as in the stage between the beginning of the age of discernment and maturity, as previously explained. The minor in all cases needs what sustains his life like the need of the major adult himself, but he cannot manage these needs on his own due to his complete incapacity in childhood and his physical and mental weakness in the stage of discernment, which necessitated the deficiency of his civil capacity of performance. So how are his needs and the well-being of his life ensured? Therefore, all divine and positive laws agreed on a single method that can undertake that, which is termed in terminology as guardianship (al-wilayah). We will draw upon this occasion the basic outlines and general picture of guardianship, its divisions, and rulings without entering into details. 2/66 - Definition of guardianship and explanation of its reality: Al-wilayah (with fath of the waw, and kasr of the waw in language means assistance, and also means authority and undertaking of affairs. And what is intended by it among jurists is that a major adult person undertakes the management of the personal and financial affairs of a minor person.
Source pages p. 791

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on guardianship (al-wilayah). It first defines the minor (qasir) as someone who has not completed full capacity of performance (ahliyyat al-ada'), whether entirely lacking it (like a young child without discernment) or having deficient capacity (like a child in the stage between discernment and maturity). The author emphasizes that a minor needs the same necessities of life as an adult but is unable to provide for himself due to physical and mental weakness. Thus, all divine and positive laws have agreed on the institution of guardianship as the means to ensure the minor's needs and well-being. The author then gives the linguistic and technical definition of wilayah: linguistically it means assistance and authority; technically it is the undertaking of a major adult person over a minor in managing his personal and financial affairs. This sets the foundation for the rest of the chapter.

Key Terms
قاصر (qasir): A minor who has not completed full capacity of performance, either lacking it entirely (non-discerning child) or having deficient capacity (between discernment and maturity).
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-ada'): Capacity of performance; the legal ability to exercise rights and obligations, which is deficient in minors.
الولاية (al-wilayah): The undertaking of a major adult person over a minor in managing his personal and financial affairs.

Key Takeaway

Definition and Types of Legal Guardianship
Defining guardianship, its types, and its ruling as compulsory representation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define legal guardianship (wilaya), distinguish it from voluntary agency (wakala), and apply the maxim that representation (niyaba) is broader than both.

Core Idea
Wilaya is compulsory representation of a minor by a competent adult, mandated by law or court.

Before a child reaches full legal capacity, he cannot manage his own affairs. Divine and positive laws have all agreed on a single solution: guardianship. This lesson unpacks the definition of wilaya, its two forms, and how it fits within the broader concept of representation (niyaba).

Guided Path
1
Identify the need for guardianship
Recognize that a minor (qasir) lacks full legal capacity and requires someone to act for him.
The minor includes both the non-discerning child and the discerning minor before rushd.
2
Define wilaya linguistically and terminologically
Wilaya in language means help or authority; in fiqh it means a mature adult managing the personal and financial affairs of a minor.
The guardian must be adult and sound of mind.
3
Distinguish niyaba from wakala and wilaya
Niyaba (representation) is the general category; it has two forms: voluntary (wakala) and compulsory (wilaya). Wakala is contractual; wilaya is imposed by law or court.
Niyaba is broader than both.
4
Identify the ruling and effect of wilaya
Since wilaya is compulsory agency, the guardian acts as the legal representative of the minor in all matters that accept representation.
The guardian's actions bind the minor.
5
Note exceptions to representation
Matters like taking an oath (yamin) do not admit representation; thus if an oath is needed from a minor, it is postponed until maturity.
This is a key exception to the general rule.

Chapter Sixty-Six: Guardianship, or Legal Agency
1/66 – A person before completing his full legal capacity is called a minor (qasir), whether he lacks this capacity entirely like a non-discerning child, or has deficient capacity as in the stage between the beginning of discernment and maturity, as previously explained. The minor in all cases needs what sustains his life just as the adult mature person needs, but he cannot manage these needs himself due to total incapacity in infancy and physical and mental weakness in the stage of discernment, which caused deficiency in his civil legal capacity. So how are his needs and well-being ensured? Therefore, divine and positive laws have agreed on a single method that can undertake that, called in terminology guardianship (wilaya). We will outline below the basic lines and general picture of guardianship, its divisions and rulings without entering into details.
2/66 – Definition of guardianship and explanation of its reality: Wilaya (with fatha on the waw and also kasra) linguistically means help or authority and assuming command. According to jurists, it means a mature adult person undertaking the management of the personal and financial affairs of a minor. So guardianship in essence is a type of agency (niyaba) whose general meaning is one person standing in the place of another in disposing on his behalf. Agency is of two forms: voluntary and compulsory. The voluntary is wakala (proxy), whose subject is delegating disposal to another. The compulsory is guardianship, where the law or the court delegates disposal for the benefit of the minor by proxy to another person. That guardian-proxy is considered the legal representative of the minor, standing in his place in all affairs that admit proxy, such as contracts, actions, and litigation over rights (1). It becomes clear from this that agency in its general sense is broader than guardianship, because agency can be contractual by mere choice – that is wakala, which is concluded by a contract between principal and agent – and it can be legal or statutory, imposed by applicable legislation, such as the guardian's proxy over the minor by virtue of law, or judicial proxy such as the executor (wasi) appointed by the judge over the minor to manage his wealth, or the curator (qayyim) appointed by the judge over an absent adult in some cases to manage his wealth.
3/66 – Ruling and effect of guardianship: Since guardianship is compulsory agency, it necessarily entails a general ruling.
Source pages p. 791

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of guardianship (wilaya) in Islamic law. The author begins by explaining the need for guardianship: a minor (qasir) lacks full legal capacity and cannot manage his affairs. Divine and positive laws have agreed on guardianship as the solution. He then defines wilaya linguistically (as help or authority) and terminologically as a mature adult managing the personal and financial affairs of a minor. He clarifies that guardianship is a type of compulsory agency (niyaba ijbariyya), as opposed to voluntary agency (wakala). Voluntary agency is contractual, while compulsory agency is imposed by law or court for the benefit of the minor. The guardian is the legal representative of the minor in all matters that admit representation, except those that do not, like taking an oath (yamin). An example: if someone claims a right against a deceased person's estate and needs to administer an oath to the heirs, if the heir is a minor, the oath is postponed until he reaches maturity. The passage also distinguishes between types of compulsory agency: guardianship proper (by law), executorship (by court appointment), and curatorship (for absent adults). The ruling (hukm) is that guardianship, being compulsory, has a general effect which is not elaborated here but implies the guardian's actions bind the minor.

Key Terms
الولاية (al-wilaya / al-walaya): قيام شخص كبير راشد على شخص قاصر في تدبير شؤونه الشخصية والمالية
النيابة (al-niyaba): قيام شخص مقام آخر في التصرف عنه
الوكالة (al-wakala): النيابة الاختيارية التي تتم بعقد بين الموكل والوكيل
القاصر (al-qasir): الإنسان قبل استكماله أهلية الأداء كلها، سواء كان فاقداً أو ناقص الأهلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Wakala is only for adults, while wilaya is only for minors.
D) Wakala requires a guardian, while wilaya does not.

Key Takeaway
Guardianship (wilaya) is compulsory representation of a minor by a competent adult, distinct from voluntary agency (wakala).

Definition of Guardianship and Its Distinction from Agency
Defines guardianship and distinguishes from agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define guardianship (wilaya) as compulsory representation, distinguish it from agency (wakala), and identify its legal effect and types.

Core Idea
Guardianship is compulsory representation imposed by law for a minor's benefit; its acts are binding and non-revocable.

This passage defines guardianship within the broader concept of representation. It clarifies that while representation can be voluntary (agency) or compulsory (guardianship), guardianship is a legal imposition for the protection of minors. Understanding this distinction is essential for muftis dealing with family and property matters of wards.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Genus and Differentia
Locate the definition of guardianship. Recognize that it is a type of representation (niyaba) with the specific characteristic of being compulsory (ijbariyya) and imposed by Sharia or judiciary.
The genus is 'representation' and the differentia is 'compulsory imposition for the minor's benefit.'
2
Distinguish the Two Forms of Representation
Identify the two forms: voluntary (agency, wakala) and compulsory (guardianship, wilaya). Note that agency is contractual and revocable, while guardianship is non-contractual and binding.
Representation (niyaba) is the broader term encompassing both.
3
Analyze the Legal Effect of Guardianship
Extract the ruling that the guardian's acts, if for the minor's benefit and meeting legal conditions, are binding on the minor even after majority. The minor cannot rescind them.
This binding effect is a key consequence of compulsory representation.
4
Identify the Types of Guardianship
Note the division into guardianship over the person (e.g., marriage, education) and guardianship over property (e.g., contracts, financial management).
This bipartite division is foundational in Hanafi jurisprudence.
5
Determine Who Qualifies as Guardian
Identify the default order: the father, then the paternal grandfather, based on natural care and close lineage. Recognize that the guardian must possess care and ability.
Exceptions like oath-taking are not delegable, even to a guardian.

٤/٦٦ - الولاية على النفس والولاية على المال :
والولاية تتضمن سلطة ذات فرعين :
أحدهما : سلطة على شؤون القاصر المتعلقة بشخصه ونفسه، كالتزويج والتعليم والتطبيب والتشغيل.
والآخر : سلطة على شؤونه المالية من عقود وتصرفات وحفظ وإنفاق.

٥/٦٦ - من هو الولي؟
يتضح مما تقدم أن الولاية ذات ارتباط وثيق بنظام الأسرة ومصالحها، وأن عمادها حرص الولي وقدرته على رعاية القاصر وصيانة حقوقه. فيجب أن يكون الولي ممن يتوافر فيهم هذا الحرص والقدرة في العادة. ولذا كان الأصل في الولاية شرعاً أن يتولاها من الأسرة أقرب الناس نسباً إلى القاصر، كأب الصغير وابن المجنون.
وإذا كان الأب رب أسرته وهو أحرص الناس عادة على أولاده ومستقبلهم، ويليه الجد أبو الأب في هذه العاطفة، أقر الشرع للأب ومن يليه من الأولياء.

ومثله ما لو ادعى بحق على الصغير مباشرة واحتيج إلى تحليفه.
(١) ومن ثم يعرفون الولاية بأنها تنفيذ القول على الغير شاء أم أبي) (ر: تعريفات السيد الجرجاني والدر المختار أول باب الولي في النكاح ورسالة الأهلية للشيخ أحمد إبراهيم ص/ ٧٧).
وهذا تعريف غير سديد، لأنه يعرف الولاية ببيان حكمها لا بشرح حقيقتها فلذا عرفناها في الفقرة ٢/٦٦ تعريفاً آخر.
Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. Guardianship in its essence is a type of representation which in its general meaning is: one person standing in the place of another in disposition on his behalf. Representation is of two forms: voluntary and compulsory. Voluntary representation is agency (wakala) whose subject is delegating disposition to another. As for compulsory representation, it is guardianship (wilaya) in which the Law or the judiciary delegates disposition for the benefit of a minor on his behalf to another person. That guardian representative is considered the legal representative of the minor, standing in his place in all matters that admit representation, such as contracts, acts, and litigation in rights. From this it becomes clear that representation in its general concept is more general than guardianship, because representation can be contractual by mere choice – that is agency which is completed by a contract between the principal and agent – and it can also be legal or statutory imposed by effective legislation, which is the guardianship of a guardian over a minor by virtue of law, or it can be judicial, which is the representation of a testamentary guardian (wasi) appointed by the judge over a minor to manage his property, or the representation of a curator (qayyim) appointed by the judge over an absent adult in some cases to manage his property. 3/66 - The ruling of guardianship and its effect: Since guardianship is compulsory representation, it necessitates a general ruling that the acts of the guardian performed on account of the minor and for his benefit are binding on the minor compulsorily, provided they fulfill their legal conditions. The minor after reaching majority and maturity cannot rescind any of them. 4/66 - Guardianship over the person and guardianship over property: Guardianship comprises authority with two branches: one is authority over the minor's personal affairs such as marriage, education, medical treatment, and employment; the other is authority over his financial affairs such as contracts, dispositions, preservation, and expenditure. Hence guardianship is divided into two types: guardianship over the person and guardianship over property. 5/66 - Who is the guardian? It becomes clear from the foregoing that guardianship is closely linked to the family system and its interests, and its foundation is the guardian's care and ability to protect the minor and safeguard his rights. So the guardian must be someone in whom this care and ability are ordinarily present. Therefore, the principle in Sharia is that guardianship should be undertaken by the closest relatives in lineage to the minor, such as the father of a young child and the son of an insane person. And since the father is the head of his family and ordinarily the most caring for his children and their future, followed by the paternal grandfather in this affection, the Sharia has established guardianship for the father and those following him among the guardians.
Source pages p. 792

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory section on guardianship (wilaya) within the broader theory of legal capacity. The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by placing guardianship as a form of representation (niyaba). Representation has two forms: voluntary (agency) and compulsory (guardianship). The key distinction is that agency is contractual and based on mutual consent, while guardianship is imposed by law or judiciary for the benefit of a minor. The author emphasizes that representation in general is broader than guardianship, because representation includes agency, legal guardianship, and judicial guardianship. He then explains the legal effect of guardianship: the guardian's acts performed for the minor's benefit and meeting legal conditions are binding on the minor; the minor cannot later rescind them. This is the binding nature of compulsory representation. Next, he divides guardianship into two types: guardianship over the person (e.g., marriage, education, medical care, employment) and guardianship over property (contracts, financial management). Finally, he discusses who qualifies as a guardian: the closest relatives by blood, starting with the father, then the paternal grandfather, based on natural care and affection. The footnote clarifies exceptions to representation, such as oath-taking (yamin), which cannot be delegated even to a guardian; the minor must take the oath personally after reaching maturity. The author also critiques a common definition of guardianship as 'enforcement of one's word over another whether he likes it or not,' saying it describes the legal effect rather than the essence.

Key Terms
النيابة (al-niyāba): قيام شخص مقام آخر في التصرف عنه
الولاية (al-wilāya): النيابة الإجبارية التي يفرضها الشرع أو القضاء لمصلحة القاصر
الوكالة (al-wakāla): النيابة الاختيارية بعقد بين الموكل والوكيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal effect of a guardian's acts performed for the minor's benefit and meeting Sharia conditions?
A) They are valid only if the minor later ratifies them.
B) They are binding on the minor and cannot be rescinded after majority.
C) They are voidable at the minor's discretion.
D) They require court approval after the fact.

Key Takeaway
Guardianship is compulsory representation: its acts are binding on the ward if for his benefit and meeting legal conditions, and cannot be rescinded after majority.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Definition and Types of Guardianship (Wilayah) in Hanafi Law
Defines guardianship, types, and guardian priority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of wilayah as binding authority, distinguish between wilayat al-nafs and wilayat al-mal, and apply the priority order of guardians according to Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Guardianship is binding authority; its priority follows the inheritance order among agnates.

Guardianship (wilayah) is a central concept in Islamic family law, protecting minors and those lacking legal capacity. This lesson unpacks the Shari'i definition, the two distinct spheres of authority (personal and financial), and the detailed hierarchy of who is entitled to be a guardian, starting with the closest agnates. Understanding this structure is essential for muftis and legal practitioners when determining responsibility for dependents.

Guided Path
1
Define wilayah and its legal effect
Study the opening paragraph: the guardian's actions are binding on the minor if performed correctly. Note the author's critique of a definition that focuses only on coercive effect, and his preferred substantive definition.
The legal effect is binding (nafidh), but the essence is the authority (tasallut) to act on behalf.
2
Distinguish the two types of wilayah
Identify the two branches: guardianship over the person (wilayat al-nafs) covers marriage, education, medical treatment; guardianship over property (wilayat al-mal) covers contracts and financial management.
These branches can be separated when the primary guardians are absent.
3
Identify the primary guardians with full authority
The father has complete authority over both person and property. After him, the agnatic grandfather (father's father) holds the same comprehensive authority.
Author prefers term 'agnatic grandfather' over 'true grandfather' to avoid pejorative implication for maternal grandfather.
4
Apply the priority order among 'asabat
When father and agnatic grandfather are absent, guardianship over the person goes to the closest agnate (asaba bi-nafsihi) according to the order of inheritance: son of the incapable, then grandson, then brother, then uncle. Full siblings take priority over half-siblings.
This order mirrors the inheritance hierarchy (fara'id).
5
Recognize the transition to mother and uterine kin
If no 'asaba exists, guardianship over the person transfers to the mother, then to other uterine kin (dhawi al-arham) as detailed in the marriage guardianship chapter.
This transition is a minority view? Actually it's Hanafi, but the text implies a specific order.

:
Chapter (66): Guardianship or Shari'i Agency. In it is that the actions of the guardian carried out on behalf of the minor and for his benefit are binding on the minor by force, if they fulfill their Shari'i conditions. So the minor after reaching adulthood and having discernment cannot invalidate any of them (1). 4/66 - Guardianship over the person and guardianship over property: And guardianship includes authority with two branches: One of them: authority over the minor's affairs related to his person and self, such as marriage, education, medical treatment, and employment. And spending. Property. And the other: authority over his financial affairs, such as contracts, transactions, and preservation. Hence guardianship is divided into two types: guardianship over the person, and guardianship over property. 5/66 - Who is the guardian? It becomes clear from the above that guardianship is closely linked to the family system and its interests, and its foundation is the guardian's care and ability to protect the minor and safeguard his rights. So the guardian must be one in whom this care and ability are normally present. Therefore, the principle in Shari'a for guardianship is that it should be undertaken by the closest relative of the minor by lineage, such as the father of the child and the son of the insane person. And since the father is the head of his family and is normally the most caring for his children and their future, followed in this affection by the grandfather (father's father), the Shari'a has established for the father and after him for the agnatic grandfather (1) (father's father) complete authority over both personal and financial powers. And in the absence of the father and the agnatic grandfather, guardianship over the person is separated from guardianship over property: (2) A - As for guardianship over the person, it is undertaken by the closest then closest of the minor's agnatic relatives by themselves (asabat) according to the order of inheritance and exclusion: whoever among them is prioritized over others in inheritance is prioritized in guardianship. For example, the son of the insane man and insane woman, then their son's son however far down, he is the guardian over their persons, prioritized over their ascendants from the father upwards however high. And the father of the child or the insane person is prioritized over the branches of the father, who are brothers. And these brothers are prioritized over the branches of the grandfather, who are paternal uncles. And when there are multiple 'asabat of the same degree, like brothers, the full brother is prioritized over the half-brother by father only. If none of the 'asabat exist, guardianship over the person transfers to the mother, then to other relatives and uterine kin (dhawi al-arham), according to a specific detailed order in the chapter on the guardian from the book of marriage. (Ref: Personal Status Law of Qadri Pasha, articles 35-36/ and al-Durr al-Mukhtar 2/295.) (1) To distinguish between the two grandfathers, the convention in the science of inheritance (fara'id) is to call the father's father the 'true grandfather' and the mother's father the 'defective grandfather'. And it is not hidden that the expression 'true' and 'defective' in this context is an unfortunate expression. Hence I have approved here to call the father's father an 'agnatic grandfather', because he is among the 'asabat in the terminology of the science of fara'id, and to call the mother's father a 'uterine grandfather', because he is counted among the uterine kin. Then we introduced this terminology into the new Personal Status Law. (2) The 'asabat from relatives in the Islamic inheritance system are three types: 'asaba by itself, 'asaba by another, and 'asaba with another, as is known in the science of fara'id. And 'asaba by itself is every male relative from male offspring, i.e. in whose lineage there is no female, and this is what is meant here. 846:
Source pages p. 793

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa presents a detailed exposition of the concept of guardianship (wilayah) in Islamic law. The author begins by defining the legal effect of a guardian's actions: they are binding on the minor if performed correctly. He critiques a common definition that focuses solely on the effect (enforcing on others willy-nilly) and instead provides a substantive definition in an earlier paragraph (2/66). The passage then classifies guardianship into two types: guardianship over the person (wilayat al-nafs) and guardianship over property (wilayat al-mal). The first concerns personal affairs like marriage, education, and medical care, while the second covers financial transactions and preservation of property. The author then addresses who is entitled to be a guardian. The Shari'a gives priority to the closest agnatic relative, beginning with the father, then the paternal grandfather (al-jadd al-'asabi), who has complete authority over both person and property. In the absence of these, the two aspects of guardianship are separated: guardianship over the person goes to the nearest 'asaba (agnatic relative) in the order of inheritance, with detailed rules such as the full brother being preferred over the half-brother. If no 'asaba exists, guardianship over the person passes to the mother and then to other relatives and uterine kin, as detailed in the chapter on guardianship in marriage. The author also clarifies terminology regarding the two types of grandfathers, preferring the terms 'agnatic grandfather' (jadd 'asabi) and 'uterine grandfather' (jadd rahimi) over the traditional 'true' and 'defective' labels. This passage is foundational for understanding how Islamic law structures the protection of minors and those lacking legal capacity.

Key Terms
الولي (al-wali): The guardian who has legal authority over a minor or legally incapable person, responsible for personal and financial affairs
العصبة (al-'asaba): Agnatic relative who inherits by virtue of male lineage; specifically 'asaba bi-nafsihi (male descendant through males) is the referent here
الجد العصبي (al-jadd al-'asabi): Paternal grandfather (father's father), considered an agnate with full guardianship authority over both person and property

Key Takeaway
Wilayah is a binding authority, divided into personal and financial spheres, with a clear priority order starting from the father, then agnatic grandfather, then closest agnates, and finally mother and uterine kin.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
The Principle: Guardianship Begins at Birth
Establishes the principle that guardianship starts at birth and discusses exceptions for the fetus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that guardianship begins at birth to determine the legal status of a fetus in property transactions and explain the exception for the four rights.

Core Idea
Guardianship starts at birth; a fetus has no guardian, only four limited rights.

This passage establishes a foundational maxim of Hanafi and majority juristic thought: legal guardianship over a person begins only at birth. This principle has direct consequences for property transactions involving a fetus. While the fetus is not a legal person for guardianship purposes, necessity grants it four specific rights (inheritance, bequest, lineage, and waqf). The author contrasts this with the Zaydi Shia position and then shows how modern legislation (Egyptian and Syrian) has cautiously allowed appointment of an executor for the fetus's property, consistent with the Hanafi framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The passage states clearly that legal guardianship (al-wilayah al-shar'iyyah) over persons begins only from birth. This is a general rule.
Memorize this as a legal maxim: الولاية الشرعية على الأشخاص إنما تبدأ منذ ولادتهم.
2
Examine the examples
The author gives two examples: a purchase and a gift by the father for the fetus. In both cases, ownership does not transfer to the fetus even if born alive.
This illustrates the rule: no guardianship means no capacity to receive property.
3
Understand the exception
Despite the rule, four rights are established for the fetus out of necessity: inheritance, bequest, lineage, and waqf benefits.
These are passive rights that do not require a guardian's active management.
4
Contrast with Zaydi view
The Zaydi Shia school allows full guardianship over the fetus, including marriage, which the author rejects as excessive.
The Hanafi position is more restrictive, limiting fetal rights to the four necessities.
5
Apply to modern legislation
The passage notes that Egyptian and Syrian laws allow appointment of an executor (wasi) for the fetus's property, not for the fetus itself. This aligns with the Hanafi principle by managing property without asserting guardianship over the person.
This is a practical application of the maxim with a narrow exception for financial management.
