٦/٦ - مبدأ الولاية : الولاية الشرعية على الأشخاص إنما تبدأ منذ ولادتهم. فلا ولاية لأحد على الجنين قبل أن يولد هذا ما عليه جمهور الفقهاء المجتهدين. وقد تقدَّم أن والد الحمل إذا اشترى له شيئاً لا يدخل في ملكية الحمل ولو ولد حياً. ومثل ذلك ما لو وهبه شيئاً فإنَّ الهبة لا تصح ولا يملك الجنين المال الموهوب ؛ وإنما تثبت للجنين الحقوق الأربعة التي تقدم بيانها فقط للضرورة (ر: ف ٣/٦٠ - ٤) . وفي فقه الشيعة الزيدية ما يفيد ثبوت الولاية على الجنين في النفس وفي المال. فقد نصوا في كتبهم، كشرح الأزهار» وغيره، على جواز تزويج الجنين !! وفي هذا من الغلو ما ينبو عنه الفكر التشريعي. على أن إثبات ولاية مالية فقط على مال الجنين له وجه شرعي، لأن المال الذي يكون محجوزاً للجنين يحتاج إلى حفظ وإدارة. ومن ثم شرعت الحكومة المصرية في قانون المجالس الحسبية (وهي تقابل مجالس الأيتام المؤلفة برئاسة قضاة الشرع لدينا) جواز تعيين وصيّ للحمل المستكن. وقد عللت وزارة العدل المصرية ذلك في منشورها الصادر في ٢١ من كانون الأول سنة ۱۹۱۱ م بأنّ عدم تعيين وصيّ للحمل فيه ضرر بمصلحته، لما يترتب عليه من ترك أمواله بلا حصر وتأجير (هذا اللفظ خطأ شائع اليوم، والصواب أن يقال: إيجار» إذ ليس في العربية «أجر» بتشديد الجيم)، وإنما هو أجر أجراً ثلاثي مجرد وآجر إيجاراً بزيادة الهمزة، وزان أفعل فهو مؤجر كما في المصباح والقاموس.) وقبض وغير ذلك من الأمور الوقتية التي تقتضي السرعة . وفي هذا، كما يقول الأستاذ الشيخ أحمد إبراهيم بك، احتياط حسن تتقبله المبادىء الفقهية (ر : رسالة الأهلية ص / ٧ - (۸)(۲). (أخذنا أخيراً بهذا الحكم في قانون الأحوال الشخصية السوري الجديد. فقد أجازت المادة / ۱۷۷ / منه للقاضي إقامة وصي على أموال الجنين. وقد كنت أنا من جملة أعضاء اللجنة التي اختيرت لوضع مشروعه عام ١٩٥٣ م.) ٧/٦٦ - قوة الولاية وضعفها : النظر في الأحكام الفقهية المتعلقة بأنواع الولاية وسلطاتها يفيد أن
6/6 - The Principle of Guardianship: Legal guardianship over persons begins only from their birth. Thus no one has guardianship over a fetus before it is born; this is the view of the majority of mujtahid jurists. It has preceded that if the father of the fetus buys something for it, it does not enter the ownership of the fetus even if it is born alive. Similarly, if he gifts something to it, the gift is not valid and the fetus does not own the gifted property; only the four rights that have been previously explained are established for the fetus out of necessity (ref: F 3/60-4). In the fiqh of the Zaydi Shia, there is what indicates the establishment of guardianship over the fetus in person and property. They have stated in their books, such as 'Sharh al-Azhar' and others, the permissibility of marrying off a fetus!! In this there is an exaggeration that legal thought rejects. However, establishing only financial guardianship over the property of the fetus has a legal basis, because the property that is reserved for the fetus needs preservation and management. Hence the Egyptian government in the law of 'al-Majalis al-Hisbiyyah' (which correspond to the orphan councils headed by Sharia judges in our country) legislated the permissibility of appointing an executor for the hidden pregnancy. The Egyptian Ministry of Justice justified this in its memorandum issued on 21 December 1911, stating that not appointing an executor for the fetus harms its interest, as it leads to leaving its property without inventory and leasing (this wording is a common mistake today; the correct is to say 'ijar' because in Arabic there is no 'ajjar' with shadda on jim; rather it is 'ajara' triple root and 'ajara' ijar with alif, analogous to afala, so it is 'mu'jir' as in al-Misbah and al-Qamus.) and receipt and other urgent matters that require speed. In this, as Professor Shaykh Ahmad Ibrahim Bik says, it is a good precaution that fiqh principles accept (ref: Risalah al-Ahliyyah p. 7-8). (We have recently adopted this ruling in the new Syrian Personal Status Law. Article 177 thereof permits the judge to appoint an executor over the fetus's property. I was among the members of the committee chosen to draft its proposal in 1953 AD.) 7/66 - The Strength and Weakness of Guardianship: Examining the fiqh rulings related to types of guardianship and their powers indicates that.
Source pages p. 795

Commentary
This passage defines the principle that legal guardianship over a person begins only at birth, not before. This is the position of the majority of mujtahids (including the Hanafi school). The author supports this with two examples: purchases and gifts made by the father for the fetus do not transfer ownership, even if the child is born alive. Only four specific rights are established for the fetus out of necessity (these include inheritance, bequest, parentage, and endowment for the fetus — explained earlier). The author then contrasts this with the Zaydi Shia view, which allows guardianship over the fetus, including marriage, which he calls excessive. However, he acknowledges that financial guardianship over property reserved for the fetus may be permissible on a charitable basis, as it requires management. This is the basis for modern Egyptian and Syrian laws allowing appointment of an executor for the fetus. The footnote corrects a common linguistic error (تأجير instead of إيجار). The passage ends with the beginning of a new section on the strength and weakness of guardianship, which is incomplete in this window.

Key Terms
الجنين (al-janin): The unborn child in the womb; a fetus that has not yet been born.
الحمل المستكن (al-haml al-mustakinn): The hidden or concealed pregnancy; refers to a fetus that is not yet visible but known to exist.
الولاية الشرعية (al-wilayah al-shar'iyyah): Legal authority or guardianship granted by Islamic law over persons or property.

Checkpoint

Types of Guardianship (Wilaya) Over Minors
Classifies guardianship by strength over person and property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any guardian of a minor into one of four categories based on strength of authority over person and property, and identify the powers and limitations for each type.

Core Idea
Guardianship over minors varies in strength over person and property, determining the guardian’s exact powers.

In Hanafi fiqh, not all guardians have the same authority over a minor. The strength of guardianship differs depending on whether it concerns the minor's person (nafs) or property (māl). This lesson unpacks a four‑fold classification that is essential for a mufti to determine who can authorize medical treatment, contract a marriage, trade with the minor’s wealth, or sell real estate. Mastering this classification avoids serious legal errors in daily practice.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish person vs property guardianship
Understand that wilāyah can be over the minor's person (care, marriage, medical decisions) or over property (management, trade, sale). These are separate domains.
The same guardian may have different strength in each domain.
2
Identify the four strength categories
The text divides guardianship into: strong in both, weak in both, strong in one and weak in the other. Memorize these four boxes.
Strength determines what the guardian can do independently.
3
List powers for each category
Strong over person: compulsory marriage, discipline, surgery. Weak over person: only care and education. Strong over property: trade and authorization to trade. Weak over property: preservation, necessary spending, sale of movables only.
Selling real estate is never allowed without a judge, even for strong property guardians.
4
Map guardians to categories
Father and paternal grandfather → strong in both. Other agnates (son, brother) → strong over person, weak over property. Executors (waṣī) → strong over property, weak over person. Others → weak in both.
This mapping is critical for real‑life inquiries.
5
Note the real estate exception
No guardian, regardless of strength, can sell the minor's real estate. Only the judge may authorize such a sale in cases of necessity.
This is a common point of error.

فإنَّ التدارك هنا يعتبر مأذوناً به شرعاً وعرفاً وإن للظروف الاستثنائية أحكاماً استثنائية بحسب قواعد الشريعة .. ذلك ؛

Chapter 66: Legal guardianship or representation. Guardians whose original guardianship is confined only to the person also have weak authority over the property of the minor by way of dependency due to need. Likewise, guardians of property only also have weak authority over the person of the minor. As for guardians of both person and property together, their authority is strong in both personal and financial aspects, and they are the son, father, and agnatic grandfather as explained. Accordingly, guardianship over minors is divided in Islamic jurisprudence according to its strength and weakness into four types: strong in person and property, weak in both, strong in one and weak in the other. 1. Strong guardianship over the person grants its holder the authority of compulsory marriage, discipline, circumcision, medical treatment by cauterization and surgery. Whoever does not have strong guardianship over the minor's person has no right to require a doctor to perform surgery or circumcision; if he does and harm or death results, he is liable for compensation or blood money. It appears that this applies to normal cases, not exceptional ones requiring rapid assistance, such as if the child is hit or run over, and delaying treatment and waiting for the guardian would endanger his life, especially in today's car accidents. 2. Weak guardianship over the person does not grant its holder any of that, but merely care, shelter, discipline, education, hiring him out, or putting him in a suitable trade for profit, and the like. 3. Strong guardianship over property grants in principle the right to trade with the minor's property for profit or loss, and the right to authorize him to trade. 4. Weak guardianship over property does not grant the right to trade but merely preservation of the minor's property, accepting gifts or charity for him, necessary spending, purchasing necessities, and selling movable property for its preservation. As for selling the minor's real estate, none of these legal representatives may do so, even if the minor needs its sale for maintenance or debt repayment; only the judge may authorize the sale of real estate in cases of necessity. Section 8/66: Distribution of these types of guardianship over the guardians. These four types of guardianship are distributed in a particular order as follows: a. Strong guardianship over both person and property is the guardianship of the father, then the paternal grandfather even if high. b. Strong over person and weak over property is the guardianship of agnates other than the father and grandfather, then other relatives, according to the order mentioned earlier (5/66), such as the son, brother, and their sons. c. Strong over property and weak over person is the guardianship of executors. d. Weak over both person and property is the guardianship of those who are...
Source pages p. 797

Commentary
This passage is a systematic classification of legal guardianship (wilaya) over minors in Hanafi fiqh. The author divides guardianship into four types based on whether the authority over the minor's person and property is strong or weak. Strong guardianship over the person includes rights to compulsory marriage, discipline, circumcision, and medical procedures; weak guardianship only covers care and education. Strong guardianship over property allows trading and authorizing the minor to trade; weak guardianship only allows preservation and necessary expenses. Importantly, selling the minor's real estate is reserved for the judge, not even the guardian can do it without judicial authorization. The passage then maps these four types to specific guardians: the father and paternal grandfather have strong authority over both; other agnates have strong over person but weak over property; executors have strong over property but weak over person; and other relatives have weak over both. This classification is foundational for determining who can act for a minor and to what extent, with practical implications for liability in medical treatment and property transactions.

Key Terms
ولاية (wilāyah): Legal authority or guardianship over another person, especially a minor, to act on their behalf.
ولاية قوية (wilāyah qawiyyah): Strong guardianship that grants the guardian full authority to make decisions without needing court approval.
ولاية ضعيفة (wilāyah daʿīfah): Weak guardianship that limits the guardian to basic care and preservation, without independent decision-making power.

Key Takeaway
Guardianship over minors in Hanafi fiqh is classified into four types based on strength over person and property, each with specific powers and limitations.

The Four Types of Guardianship: A Conceptual Framework
Classifying guardianship into four types based on strength over person/property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the four types of wilaya (strong/weak over person/property) and state the scope of each type.

Core Idea
Guardianship over a minor has four distinct types, each with different powers over person and property.

In Hanafi law, guardianship (wilaya) over a minor is not a single blanket authority. The jurists classified it into four categories based on two axes: strength (qawiyya vs da'ifa) and domain (nafs vs mal). Understanding this classification is essential for determining what a guardian can and cannot do.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two axes of classification
The classification uses two dimensions: strength (strong/weak) and subject (person/property). Combine them to get four types.
2
Define 'strong guardianship over person'
What does strong wilaya over nafs include? According to the text, it includes authority over care, education, and employment of the minor.
This is held by father and grandfather.
3
Define 'weak guardianship over person'
Weak wilaya over nafs is limited to basic care, shelter, and discipline but does not extend to major decisions.
Examples: stepfather, foundling caretaker.
4
Define 'strong guardianship over property'
This authorizes trading with the minor's property and permitting the minor to trade.
Held by father, grandfather, and executors.
5
Define 'weak guardianship over property'
This is custodial: preserving property, accepting gifts, necessary spending, and selling movable goods only. No real estate sale.
Even if the minor needs to sell real estate for maintenance, only the judge can authorize.

(1) يلحظ في هذا المقام أن نظام إدارة أموال الأيتام العثماني وبعده قانون الأحوال الشخصية الجديد لدينا في المادة / ۱۸۲ / منه قد حدًا كثيراً من سلطة الأوصياء، ومنعاهم من التجارة للقاصر بأمواله لكثرة الخيانات في الوصاية . ويلحظ أيضاً أن قانون الأحوال الشخصية الجديد لدينا (في المادتين ١٦٤ - ١٦٥ منه) قد جعل حق الإذن للقاصر بالتجارة محصوراً بالقاضي الشرعي، كما جعل للقاضي حق تسليم الصغار المميزين بعد إتمامهم الخامسة عشرة من العمر، والسفهاء المحجورين جانباً من أموالهم لإدارتها على سبيل التمرين والتجربة .

Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship.
So, rectification here is considered permitted by Sharia and custom, and exceptional circumstances have exceptional rulings according to the rules of Sharia.
That is;
2. Weak guardianship over the person does not give its holder anything but mere care of the minor, shelter, discipline, education, leasing him out, or placing him in a craft suitable for his peers from which he benefits, and the like.
3. Strong guardianship over property is that which originally authorizes trading with the minor's property for his account, profit and loss, and the right to permit him to trade.
4. Weak guardianship over property does not authorize trading, but merely preserving the minor's property, accepting gifts or charity for him, necessary spending on him, purchasing necessities, and selling movable property to preserve it.
As for selling the minor's real estate, none of these legal representatives may do so, even if the minor needs to sell it for maintenance or debt repayment; rather, the judge alone may authorize the sale of real estate in cases of necessity.
66/8 - Distribution of these types of guardianship among guardians:
These four types of guardianship are distributed in a specific order as follows:
a. Strong guardianship over both person and property is the guardianship of the father, then the paternal grandfather (father's father) even if elevated.
b. As for strong guardianship over person and weak over property, it is the guardianship of those other than the father and grandfather from the 'asaba (agnatic relatives), then from other relatives, according to the order we mentioned earlier in (66/5), such as the son, brother, and their sons.
c. As for strong guardianship over property and weak over person, it is the guardianship of executors (wasi).
d. As for weak guardianship over both person and property, it is the guardianship of one who has the minor under his care and dependent from strangers, or from relatives who have not reached the role of guardianship over him. So much so that whoever picks up a foundling child of unknown family and takes care of raising him under his care and dependent, this weak guardianship over his person and property is established for him within the limits we mentioned in the preceding paragraph.
Likewise, the guardianship of the stepfather (husband of the mother) over his minor stepson whom he includes in his dependents.
It becomes clear from this that no one may authorize a discerning minor to trade except the guardian with strong guardianship over property, namely the father, then the grandfather, and the executors. As for the guardian of the person with weak guardianship over property, he does not have this authorization, because trade does not permit authorization. (Radd al-Muhtar 5/611)
66/9 - The Sultan or Judge is the Guardian of One Who Has No Guardian:
It is worthy of note that all the powers of legal representation of guardians and executors transfer to the Sultan in their absence by virtue of his general guardianship; he exercises them himself or through someone he authorizes for the minor's interest until he appoints an executor to exercise them. The judge acts on behalf of the Sultan in that.
The general fiqh principle in this regard is: "The Sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian." This is a text narrated in the prophetic hadith. (See: end of the book of al-Luqit in Radd al-Muhtar)
(1) Note: The Ottoman orphanage administration system and after it our new Personal Status Law in Article 182 have greatly restricted the authority of executors and prevented them from trading for the minor with his property due to frequent betrayals in executorship. Also note that our new Personal Status Law in Articles 164-165 has restricted the right to authorize a minor to trade exclusively to the Sharia judge, and has given the judge the right to hand over to discerning minors after completing fifteen years of age, and to interdicted prodigals, a portion of their property to manage as training and experimentation.
(2) Narrated by Abu Dawud (2083), al-Tirmidhi (1102), Ibn Majah (1879) from the hadith of Aisha; al-Tirmidhi declared it hasan, and it was authenticated by Ibn Hibban (1248) and al-Hakim (2/168); al-Bayhaqi discussed it in al-Sunan (7/105-107), and al-Hafiz in al-Talkhis (3/156).
Source pages p. 798

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm presents the Hanafi theory of guardianship (wilaya) over minors. The author classifies guardianship into four types based on two axes: person (nafs) and property (mal), each being either strong (qawiyya) or weak (da'ifa).

First, weak guardianship over the person (no. 2) is limited to care, upbringing, education, and arranging for the minor's work or training. It does not extend to property matters.

Second, strong guardianship over property (no. 3) authorizes trading with the minor's assets for profit or loss and the power to permit the minor to trade. This is significant because trading involves risk and requires managerial authority.

Third, weak guardianship over property (no. 4) is merely custodial: preserving assets, accepting gifts, spending for necessities, and selling movable goods to prevent loss. Notably, selling the minor's real estate is beyond the power of any legal representative except the judge, even if needed for maintenance or debt — this is a special safeguard.

The distribution (no. 66/8) assigns these types to specific guardians:
- Strong over both person and property: Father, then paternal grandfather.
- Strong over person but weak over property: Other agnatic relatives (asaba) like sons and brothers.
- Strong over property but weak over person: Executors (awsiya').
- Weak over both: Strangers or relatives not in the guardian line (e.g., a foundling's caretaker, stepfather). The text illustrates with the foundling (laqit) and the stepfather (zawj al-umm) as examples of weak guardianship.

Finally, the principle that "the Sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian" (al-sultan wali man la wali lahu) is a foundational maxim, based on a hadith. This means that if no specific guardian exists, the ruler (or his deputy the judge) assumes all guardianship powers until an executor is appointed. The footnote discusses modern legal modifications (Ottoman and Syrian) that restrict executors' powers due to abuse.

The passage is purely descriptive of the Hanafi classification; no ikhtilaf with other schools is mentioned here.

Key Terms
الولاية القوية على النفس (al-wilāya al-qawiyya ʿalā al-nafs): Guardianship that includes authority over the minor's person, such as care, discipline, education, and arranging employment.
الولاية الضعيفة على النفس (al-wilāya al-ḍaʿīfa ʿalā al-nafs): Guardianship limited to basic care and shelter, without authority over the minor's person for major decisions.
الولاية القوية في المال (al-wilāya al-qawiyya fī al-māl): Guardianship that includes authority to trade with the minor's property and permit the minor to trade.
الولاية الضعيفة في المال (al-wilāya al-ḍaʿīfa fī al-māl): Guardianship limited to preserving the minor's property, collecting gifts, and spending for necessities, without authority to trade or sell real estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following guardians has weak guardianship over both person and property?
A) The father
B) The executor (wasi)
C) The stepfather
D) The paternal grandfather

Key Takeaway
Hanafi guardianship is not monolithic; it is split into two dimensions (person/property) each with strong or weak powers, determining what a guardian may do.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Distribution of Guardianship Types to Legal Guardians
Distribution of guardianship types among legal guardians

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which category of guardian holds each of the four types of wilaya and apply the distribution order to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Each guardian's powers are determined by a specific distribution: father and grandfather get strongest, others get weaker forms.

The four types of guardianship are not assigned arbitrarily; Hanafi jurists have a precise hierarchy. The father and paternal grandfather hold the most comprehensive authority (strong over both person and property), while other agnatic relatives have strong over person but weak over property. Executors have strong over property but weak over person, and strangers or distant relatives have weak over both. This lesson unpacks that distribution.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four types of wilaya
From the previous lesson, you know the four types: strong/weak over person/property. Now see how they are assigned.
2
Identify the top tier: father and grandfather
The text states that strong over both person and property is for the father, then paternal grandfather (even elevated).
They have the most comprehensive authority.
3
Identify the second tier: other agnates
Sons, brothers, and their sons have strong over person but weak over property.
They can care for the minor but cannot trade with his property.
4
Identify the third tier: executors
Executors (wasi) have strong over property but weak over person.
They can manage finances but not make personal decisions like marriage.
5
Identify the fourth tier: others
Strangers, foundling caretakers, stepfathers have weak over both.
They are custodians only, with minimal property powers.

وهو نص مروي في الحديث النبوي (٢) (ر: آخر كتاب اللقيط في رد المحتار)
(1) يلحظ في هذا المقام أن نظام إدارة أموال الأيتام العثماني وبعده قانون الأحوال الشخصية الجديد لدينا في المادة / ۱۸۲ / منه قد حدًا كثيراً من سلطة الأوصياء، ومنعاهم من التجارة للقاصر بأمواله لكثرة الخيانات في الوصاية . ويلحظ أيضاً أن قانون الأحوال الشخصية الجديد لدينا (في المادتين ١٦٤ - ١٦٥ منه) قد جعل حق الإذن للقاصر بالتجارة محصوراً بالقاضي الشرعي، كما جعل للقاضي حق تسليم الصغار المميزين بعد إتمامهم الخامسة عشرة من العمر، والسفهاء المحجورين جانباً من أموالهم لإدارتها على سبيل التمرين والتجربة .
(۲) أخرجه أبو داود (۲۰۸۳) والترمذي (۱۱۰۲) وابن ماجه ،(۱۸۷۹)، من حديث عائشة وحسنه الترمذي وصححه ابن حبان (١٢٤٨) والحاكم ١٦٨/٢. وبسط الكلام عنه البيهقي في السنن ١٠٥/٧ - ۱۰۷ ، والحافظ في التلخيص ١٥٦/٣.
Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship.
So, rectification here is considered permitted by Sharia and custom, and exceptional circumstances have exceptional rulings according to the rules of Sharia.
That is;
2. Weak guardianship over the person does not give its holder anything but mere care of the minor, shelter, discipline, education, leasing him out, or placing him in a craft suitable for his peers from which he benefits, and the like.
3. Strong guardianship over property is that which originally authorizes trading with the minor's property for his account, profit and loss, and the right to permit him to trade.
4. Weak guardianship over property does not authorize trading, but merely preserving the minor's property, accepting gifts or charity for him, necessary spending on him, purchasing necessities, and selling movable property to preserve it.
As for selling the minor's real estate, none of these legal representatives may do so, even if the minor needs to sell it for maintenance or debt repayment; rather, the judge alone may authorize the sale of real estate in cases of necessity.
66/8 - Distribution of these types of guardianship among guardians:
These four types of guardianship are distributed in a specific order as follows:
a. Strong guardianship over both person and property is the guardianship of the father, then the paternal grandfather (father's father) even if elevated.
b. As for strong guardianship over person and weak over property, it is the guardianship of those other than the father and grandfather from the 'asaba (agnatic relatives), then from other relatives, according to the order we mentioned earlier in (66/5), such as the son, brother, and their sons.
c. As for strong guardianship over property and weak over person, it is the guardianship of executors (wasi).
d. As for weak guardianship over both person and property, it is the guardianship of one who has the minor under his care and dependent from strangers, or from relatives who have not reached the role of guardianship over him. So much so that whoever picks up a foundling child of unknown family and takes care of raising him under his care and dependent, this weak guardianship over his person and property is established for him within the limits we mentioned in the preceding paragraph.
Likewise, the guardianship of the stepfather (husband of the mother) over his minor stepson whom he includes in his dependents.
It becomes clear from this that no one may authorize a discerning minor to trade except the guardian with strong guardianship over property, namely the father, then the grandfather, and the executors. As for the guardian of the person with weak guardianship over property, he does not have this authorization, because trade does not permit authorization. (Radd al-Muhtar 5/611)
66/9 - The Sultan or Judge is the Guardian of One Who Has No Guardian:
It is worthy of note that all the powers of legal representation of guardians and executors transfer to the Sultan in their absence by virtue of his general guardianship; he exercises them himself or through someone he authorizes for the minor's interest until he appoints an executor to exercise them. The judge acts on behalf of the Sultan in that.
The general fiqh principle in this regard is: "The Sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian." This is a text narrated in the prophetic hadith. (See: end of the book of al-Luqit in Radd al-Muhtar)
(1) Note: The Ottoman orphanage administration system and after it our new Personal Status Law in Article 182 have greatly restricted the authority of executors and prevented them from trading for the minor with his property due to frequent betrayals in executorship. Also note that our new Personal Status Law in Articles 164-165 has restricted the right to authorize a minor to trade exclusively to the Sharia judge, and has given the judge the right to hand over to discerning minors after completing fifteen years of age, and to interdicted prodigals, a portion of their property to manage as training and experimentation.
(2) Narrated by Abu Dawud (2083), al-Tirmidhi (1102), Ibn Majah (1879) from the hadith of Aisha; al-Tirmidhi declared it hasan, and it was authenticated by Ibn Hibban (1248) and al-Hakim (2/168); al-Bayhaqi discussed it in al-Sunan (7/105-107), and al-Hafiz in al-Talkhis (3/156).
Source pages p. 798

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm presents the Hanafi theory of guardianship (wilaya) over minors. The author classifies guardianship into four types based on two axes: person (nafs) and property (mal), each being either strong (qawiyya) or weak (da'ifa).

First, weak guardianship over the person (no. 2) is limited to care, upbringing, education, and arranging for the minor's work or training. It does not extend to property matters.

Second, strong guardianship over property (no. 3) authorizes trading with the minor's assets for profit or loss and the power to permit the minor to trade. This is significant because trading involves risk and requires managerial authority.

Third, weak guardianship over property (no. 4) is merely custodial: preserving assets, accepting gifts, spending for necessities, and selling movable goods to prevent loss. Notably, selling the minor's real estate is beyond the power of any legal representative except the judge, even if needed for maintenance or debt — this is a special safeguard.

The distribution (no. 66/8) assigns these types to specific guardians:
- Strong over both person and property: Father, then paternal grandfather.
- Strong over person but weak over property: Other agnatic relatives (asaba) like sons and brothers.
- Strong over property but weak over person: Executors (awsiya').
- Weak over both: Strangers or relatives not in the guardian line (e.g., a foundling's caretaker, stepfather). The text illustrates with the foundling (laqit) and the stepfather (zawj al-umm) as examples of weak guardianship.

Finally, the principle that "the Sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian" (al-sultan wali man la wali lahu) is a foundational maxim, based on a hadith. This means that if no specific guardian exists, the ruler (or his deputy the judge) assumes all guardianship powers until an executor is appointed. The footnote discusses modern legal modifications (Ottoman and Syrian) that restrict executors' powers due to abuse.

The passage is purely descriptive of the Hanafi classification; no ikhtilaf with other schools is mentioned here.

Key Terms
العصبات (al-ʿaṣabāt): Agnatic relatives, male relatives through the male line, such as sons, brothers, uncles, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the distribution, who holds strong guardianship over property but weak over person?
A) The father
B) The paternal grandfather
C) The executor (wasi)
D) The stepfather

Key Takeaway
The distribution of wilaya types follows a clear hierarchy: father and grandfather have the most comprehensive powers, while others have limited authority.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Sultan as Residual Guardian: The Maxim and Its Application
The principle that the ruler is guardian of those without a guardian

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-sultan wali man la wali lahu' to scenarios where no specific guardian exists and state its evidentiary basis.

Core Idea
When no guardian is available, the ruler (or judge) assumes full guardianship over the minor.

A foundational principle in Hanafi law is that the state, represented by the ruler or judge, steps in as the default guardian for minors who have no specific legal guardian. This maxim, rooted in a prophetic hadith, ensures that no minor is left without protection. This lesson explores the maxim, its scope, and its modern application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its wording
The maxim is 'السلطان ولي من لا ولي له' (The Sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian). Find it in the text.
It appears at the end of the passage.
2
Recognize the evidence
The text cites a hadith narrated by Aisha (RA). Check the footnote for sources.
The hadith is hasan and supports the maxim.
3
Understand when the maxim applies
It applies when there is no father, grandfather, executor, or any other relative with guardianship.
The text says 'in their absence'.
4
Understand who exercises this power
The Sultan (ruler) himself or his deputy, the judge, exercises these powers until an executor is appointed.
The judge acts on behalf of the Sultan.
5
Apply to a scenario
Example: A foundling with no known relatives. Under this maxim, the ruler/judge becomes the guardian.
The text mentions the foundling (laqit) as an example.

66/8 - Distribution of these types of guardianship among guardians:
These four types of guardianship are distributed in a specific order as follows:
a. Strong guardianship over both person and property is the guardianship of the father, then the paternal grandfather (father's father) even if elevated.
b. As for strong guardianship over person and weak over property, it is the guardianship of those other than the father and grandfather from the 'asaba (agnatic relatives), then from other relatives, according to the order we mentioned earlier in (66/5), such as the son, brother, and their sons.
c. As for strong guardianship over property and weak over person, it is the guardianship of executors (wasi).
d. As for weak guardianship over both person and property, it is the guardianship of one who has the minor under his care and dependent from strangers, or from relatives who have not reached the role of guardianship over him. So much so that whoever picks up a foundling child of unknown family and takes care of raising him under his care and dependent, this weak guardianship over his person and property is established for him within the limits we mentioned in the preceding paragraph.
Likewise, the guardianship of the stepfather (husband of the mother) over his minor stepson whom he includes in his dependents.
It becomes clear from this that no one may authorize a discerning minor to trade except the guardian with strong guardianship over property, namely the father, then the grandfather, and the executors. As for the guardian of the person with weak guardianship over property, he does not have this authorization, because trade does not permit authorization. (Radd al-Muhtar 5/611)
66/9 - The Sultan or Judge is the Guardian of One Who Has No Guardian:
It is worthy of note that all the powers of legal representation of guardians and executors transfer to the Sultan in their absence by virtue of his general guardianship; he exercises them himself or through someone he authorizes for the minor's interest until he appoints an executor to exercise them. The judge acts on behalf of the Sultan in that.
The general fiqh principle in this regard is: "The Sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian." This is a text narrated in the prophetic hadith. (See: end of the book of al-Luqit in Radd al-Muhtar)
(1) Note: The Ottoman orphanage administration system and after it our new Personal Status Law in Article 182 have greatly restricted the authority of executors and prevented them from trading for the minor with his property due to frequent betrayals in executorship. Also note that our new Personal Status Law in Articles 164-165 has restricted the right to authorize a minor to trade exclusively to the Sharia judge, and has given the judge the right to hand over to discerning minors after completing fifteen years of age, and to interdicted prodigals, a portion of their property to manage as training and experimentation.
(2) Narrated by Abu Dawud (2083), al-Tirmidhi (1102), Ibn Majah (1879) from the hadith of Aisha; al-Tirmidhi declared it hasan, and it was authenticated by Ibn Hibban (1248) and al-Hakim (2/168); al-Bayhaqi discussed it in al-Sunan (7/105-107), and al-Hafiz in al-Talkhis (3/156).
Source pages p. 798

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm presents the Hanafi theory of guardianship (wilaya) over minors. The author classifies guardianship into four types based on two axes: person (nafs) and property (mal), each being either strong (qawiyya) or weak (da'ifa).

First, weak guardianship over the person (no. 2) is limited to care, upbringing, education, and arranging for the minor's work or training. It does not extend to property matters.

Second, strong guardianship over property (no. 3) authorizes trading with the minor's assets for profit or loss and the power to permit the minor to trade. This is significant because trading involves risk and requires managerial authority.

Third, weak guardianship over property (no. 4) is merely custodial: preserving assets, accepting gifts, spending for necessities, and selling movable goods to prevent loss. Notably, selling the minor's real estate is beyond the power of any legal representative except the judge, even if needed for maintenance or debt — this is a special safeguard.

The distribution (no. 66/8) assigns these types to specific guardians:
- Strong over both person and property: Father, then paternal grandfather.
- Strong over person but weak over property: Other agnatic relatives (asaba) like sons and brothers.
- Strong over property but weak over person: Executors (awsiya').
- Weak over both: Strangers or relatives not in the guardian line (e.g., a foundling's caretaker, stepfather). The text illustrates with the foundling (laqit) and the stepfather (zawj al-umm) as examples of weak guardianship.

Finally, the principle that "the Sultan is the guardian of one who has no guardian" (al-sultan wali man la wali lahu) is a foundational maxim, based on a hadith. This means that if no specific guardian exists, the ruler (or his deputy the judge) assumes all guardianship powers until an executor is appointed. The footnote discusses modern legal modifications (Ottoman and Syrian) that restrict executors' powers due to abuse.

The passage is purely descriptive of the Hanafi classification; no ikhtilaf with other schools is mentioned here.

Key Terms
السلطان ولي من لا ولي له (al-sulṭān walī man lā walī lahu): A legal maxim meaning that the ruler (or judge) assumes guardianship over a minor who has no specific guardian appointed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
The maxim 'al-sultan wali man la wali lahu' is based on which source?
A) Quranic verse
B) Ijma' of the companions
C) Hadith of the Prophet (PBUH)
D) Qiyas

Key Takeaway
The ruler (or judge) is the residual guardian for any minor without a specific guardian, ensuring no child falls through the cracks.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Refining the Definition of Impediments to Legal Capacity
Critical refinement of 'awarid al-ahliyya definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the traditional list of 'awarid al-ahliyya and apply al-Zarqa's refined definition to distinguish true impediments from mere legal exemptions.

Core Idea
Not every legal exception is an impediment: a true 'arid must intrude upon capacity, not just excuse from obligation.

Classical usul al-fiqh texts typically enumerate many conditions as 'awarid al-ahliyya (impediments to legal capacity), including childhood, forgetfulness, menstruation, and others. Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa challenges this tradition, arguing that these are not true impediments because they do not affect the foundation of legal personality—intellect and awareness. Instead, they are either natural stages, normal mental occurrences, or shariah-based exemptions. Understanding this refinement sharpens our analytical tools for legal capacity theory and prevents conflating different categories of legal effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Traditional List
List the nine conditions that classical authors include as 'awarid al-ahliyya: childhood, forgetfulness, death, menstruation, postpartum bleeding, travel, ignorance, mistake, and jest.
These appear in many usul works; note that five are 'celestial' (divinely ordained) and four are 'acquired'.
2
Understand al-Zarqa's Objection
Al-Zarqa's core argument: a true 'arid must be an intrusive abnormality or affliction that affects the legal personality itself. Natural states (childhood) and normal occurrences (forgetfulness) do not qualify.
He contrasts these with conditions like insanity or bankruptcy, which genuinely impair capacity.
3
Analyze the Specific Arguments
Examine his reasoning for childhood (natural stage), forgetfulness (normal, excused by hadith), and menstruation/postpartum bleeding (shariah preventives that leave capacity intact).
For each, note the distinction between having a legal effect and being an impediment to capacity.
4
Extend the Refinement
Apply the same logic to the other conditions (travel, ignorance, mistake, jest). Ask: do these affect the person's underlying legal personality or merely adjust obligations?
Travel may allow concessions but does not reduce capacity; ignorance is a separate issue; mistake and jest have specific rulings but are not capacity defects.
5
Evaluate the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: how does this refinement change the teaching of ahliyya theory? It clarifies terminology and prevents overextension of the term 'arid.
This is a methodological improvement that leads to more precise fiqh reasoning, especially in contemporary fatwa.

مآخذ على العرض الفقهي التقليدي لعوارض الأهلية
اعتاد المؤلفون في أصول الفقه أن يذكروا في جملة عوارض الأهلية حالات أخرى كثيرة غير التي ذكرناها هنا. فهم يضيفون على العوارض السماوية غير ما تقدم خمسة أخرى هي: الصغر، والنسيان والموت، وفي النساء خاصة الحيض والنفاس . ويضيفون على العوارض المكتسبة أربعة أخرى هي: السفر والجهل،
والخطأ، والهزل.
والباحثون المعاصرون الذين كتبوا في الأهلية كالأستاذ العلامة الشيخ أحمد إبراهيم رحمه الله قد تابعوا هذا العرض التقليدي المأثور لنظرية الأهليات وعوارضها ونقلوا أحكام هذه الحالات الأخرى على أنها جميعاً من جملة عوارض الأهلية .

ويحسن أن نوضح هنا ملاحظاتنا على كل من هذه الحالات التي طويناها، ليظهر للقارىء أنها ليس فيها معنى عوارض الأهلية، فلا يصح أن تحشر معها :

١ - فالصغر هو الطور الطبيعي السابق من حياة كل إنسان، فهو من الأحوال الأصلية في موضوع الأهلية وليس من العوارض. وقد اعترض بعض علماء الأصول هذا الاعتراض وأجيب عنه بما لا يقنع (ر: كشف الأسرار ٤/٢٦٣).

Appendix to Chapter Eight: Objections to the Traditional Fiqh Presentation of Impediments to Legal Capacity. The authors of Usul al-Fiqh are accustomed to mention in the list of impediments to legal capacity many other cases besides those we have mentioned here. They add to the celestial impediments five other than what preceded: childhood, forgetfulness, death, and specifically for women menstruation and postpartum bleeding. And they add to the acquired impediments four others: travel, ignorance, mistake, and jest. Contemporary researchers who have written on legal capacity, such as the eminent scholar Sheikh Ahmad Ibrahim (may Allah have mercy on him), have followed this traditional transmitted presentation of the theory of legal capacities and their impediments and transmitted the rulings of these other cases as all being among the impediments to legal capacity. But we see that these other nine cases cannot be considered impediments to legal capacity at all, even though they have a preventive effect in some legal obligations and exceptional rulings. Therefore, we omitted them and confined ourselves to the cases we mentioned, because they alone are correctly called impediments to the legal capacity of persons. It is appropriate that we explain here our observations on each of these cases that we omitted, so that it becomes clear to the reader that they do not have the meaning of impediments to legal capacity, so they should not be grouped with them: 1) Childhood is the prior natural stage of every human's life; it is among the original states in the subject of legal capacity, not among the impediments. Some Usul scholars have objected to this objection and were answered unconvincingly (see Kashf al-Asrar 4/263). 2) Forgetfulness is a normal state that occurs to memory, not an affliction that afflicts a person's body, mind, or legal status; it does not contradict legal capacity in the least nor diminish it, but it is considered a legal excuse that removes accountability for neglecting some religious obligations or conditions, out of mercy for people and removal of hardship, due to the prophetic hadith: 'Indeed Allah has removed from my nation error, forgetfulness, and what they are coerced into.' That is like one who forgets the prayer at its time – he is not considered negligent, and like one who forgets to mention Allah's name upon the slaughtered animal – it does not become unlawful, and like one who eats in Ramadan forgetfully – he does not break the fast. 3 & 4) Menstruation and postpartum bleeding in women are legal preventives that prevent the obligation of some religious acts of worship that require purity or prevent their practice. The menstruating woman and the woman with postpartum bleeding abandon prayer and fasting, then make up the fasts of Ramadan they missed but not the prayers; likewise, they avoid entering mosques and reciting the Quran; and other exceptional rulings. But the capacity for obligation and capacity for performance with their religious and civil branches – i.e., capacity for worship and capacity for disposal – are complete in them. As for capacity for performance, its basis is intellect and awareness; thus it is established for the discerning minor within limited bounds as previously stated. This intellect and awareness are fully present in the menstruating and postpartum woman, so menstruation and postpartum bleeding have no effect on their capacity for performance. (1) See its verification in F 2/10 B in the footnote.
Source pages p. 801

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa of the traditional classification of 'awarid al-ahliyya (impediments to legal capacity). The author identifies nine cases that are commonly listed as impediments by classical usul al-fiqh authors: childhood, forgetfulness, death, menstruation, postpartum bleeding, travel, ignorance, mistake, and jest. He argues that none of these are true impediments to legal capacity, though they may have certain legal effects. He then explains his reasoning for the first four (childhood, forgetfulness, menstruation, and postpartum bleeding) from the nine. Childhood is a natural developmental stage, not an external impediment. Forgetfulness is a normal mental occurrence, not a defect, and the hadith of 'raf' al-qalam' (lifting of the pen) shows it is an excuse, not an incapacity. Menstruation and postpartum bleeding are shariah-based preventives that temporarily lift some obligations but do not affect the essential elements of capacity – the intellect and awareness remain intact. Thus, the capacity for obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) and capacity for performance (ahliyyat al-ada') remain complete in women experiencing these conditions. The author intends to purify the theory of ahliyya from foreign elements and restrict the term 'arid (impediment) to conditions that truly affect the underlying foundation of legal personality, such as insanity, idiocy, or bankruptcy.

Key Terms
صغر (sighar): Childhood; the natural early stage of human life, not a legal impediment.
نسيان (nisyan): Forgetfulness; a normal memory occurrence, not an affliction, but a legal excuse.
حيض (hayd): Menstruation; a shariah preventive (mani') for certain acts of worship, not an impediment to capacity.
نفاس (nifas): Postpartum bleeding; similar to hayd in legal effect.

Key Takeaway
A true 'arid al-ahliyya must be an intrusive abnormality that affects the legal personality itself, not just a condition that alters obligations.

Distinguishing True Impediments from Excuses: Travel, Ignorance, Mistake
Critical analysis of travel, ignorance, and mistake as alleged impediments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi definition of impediments to legal capacity (عوارض الأهلية) to distinguish between genuine impediments and mere excuses such as travel, ignorance, and mistake.

Core Idea
True impediments attack capacity; travel, ignorance, and mistake only affect obligation or accountability.

In this passage, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa critically examines common candidates for impediments to legal capacity. He argues that travel, ignorance, and mistake do not impair a person's inherent capacity (أهلية); rather, they are external factors that modify obligations or provide excuses. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying Hanafi legal theory correctly.

Guided Path
1
Define the technical criteria of عارض الأهلية
Identify from the passage that a true impediment must attack the constituents of capacity while the person remains extant.
The author implies that factors like insanity or minority are true impediments.
2
Examine travel: does it affect capacity or only obligations?
Analyze the author's reasoning that travel brings concessions (رخصة) such as skipping Friday prayer or delaying fasting, but does not impair ahliyyah.
Travel does not delete the obligation; it merely allows flexibility.
3
Examine ignorance: excuse or impairment?
Consider examples: a new Muslim ignorant of duties, an agent ignorant of dismissal. The author shows ignorance is an excuse that prevents accountability, not an impairment of capacity.
Ignorance does not diminish the capacity itself; it only affects liability.
4
Examine mistake: is it a defect in capacity or a description of action?
Mistake (خطأ) is defined as occurrence contrary to intention; it negates intent, not faculties. It may lift or mitigate accountability.
Mistake is about the action, not the person's capacity.
5
Synthesize the criterion
Conclude that these three are not true impediments; they only modify obligations or provide excuses. The basis of capacity (أهلية) remains intact.

بهذا المعنى.
Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. 5 - Travel: The Law only considered the hardship in it as a cause for concession in some religious obligations. Some duties were made optional for the traveler, such as Friday prayer, the two Eid prayers, and the sacrifice; and some were permitted for the traveler to delay from their specific time, like fasting Ramadan. So in travel, as you see, nothing impairs any of a person's capacity for obligation or for performance from a religious or civil perspective. 6 - Ignorance also does not impair any of a person's capacity and abilities; rather it arises for the person in some situations as a legal excuse preventing him from being held accountable for negligence, like one who embraces Islam while far from the Abode of Islam, ignorant of the obligations that Islam imposes on him. Indeed, his ignorance in such a case is considered an excuse preventing him from being held accountable for neglecting religious duties, and like the agent's ignorance of his principal's dismissal; his actions remain effective upon the principal until the news of dismissal reaches him. In this issue, as you see, knowledge is what removes the agent's authority, while ignorance preserves it; so there is no impairment of capacity in ignorance, it is merely an excuse. 7 - Mistake likewise: they defined it as the occurrence of something contrary to the intention of the one from whom it occurred. There is no diminution of a person's faculties and abilities in it, nor any connection or relation to them. Rather it is a description of actions issuing from a person that negates intent from them, which normally exists in the case of premeditation, and thus necessitates lifting or mitigating accountability. - And furthest of all from the meaning of impediments to legal capacity is jest!! For jest is the opposite of seriousness; its locus is verbal transactions, and it is playing with words without intending their meanings and rulings. According to the jurists, it results in the invalidity or corruption of contracts and transactions, with a preceding disagreement about that, due to the absence of the true intention of the contracting party therein, not due to loss or deficiency of the capacity of the contracting party. Evidence is that the Law considered jest like seriousness in some transactions as an exception, namely marriage, divorce, and manumission, as previously mentioned in volume 6, page 33. 856 First Appendix to Chapter Eight: Objections Regarding Impediments to Legal Capacity. So the reality of the matter in jest is the absence of the pillar of the verbal transaction, because its pillar is a resolute expression of intention; by jest in it, the indication of this expression to intention is negated. If it were acceptable to consider jest as an impediment to legal capacity, it would also be necessary to consider the absence of all other conditions of offer and acceptance in contracts as impediments to legal capacity!! 9 - As for death, there truly is the demolition of all capacities. From this aspect, it might be possible to consider it from the impediments to legal capacity. Word. But from another aspect, it is seen that death is not an impediment that directly attacks the constituents of legal capacity; rather it falls upon the life of the person and removes him from existence. As for the demolition of capacities by it, it is consequent upon the removal of the person who is the locus, because capacity is a quality of the person, and there is no survival of the quality without the described. And the apparent meaning of 'impediments to legal capacity' is that they are impediments that attack its constituents and affect them while the person remains, such that the person loses due to this impediment what he should have enjoyed of capacity; so he remains a person who is not legally capable. And this is not attainable in the case of death due to the removal of personality as such; hence death is not from the impediments to legal capacity in this sense.
Source pages p. 804

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical examination of what constitutes a true impediment to legal capacity (عوارض الأهلية). The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, systematically argues that several factors commonly mistaken as impediments are not genuine bars to capacity. For travel (safar), the Law grants concessions due to hardship but does not affect a person's capacity to be obligated or to perform. Ignorance (jahl) is only an excuse excusing accountability in specific contexts, such as a new Muslim unaware of duties or an agent unaware of dismissal; it does not impair inherent capacity. Mistake (khata) is simply the absence of intent and does not diminish faculties; it may lift or mitigate accountability. Jest (hazl) is the farthest from being an impediment: it involves verbal transactions without real intent. The Hanafi school holds that jest can invalidate contracts, but it is due to missing the pillar of resolute expression, not due to deficient capacity. Indeed, in marriage, divorce, and manumission, jest is treated as serious (istithna'). Finally, death (mawt) indeed destroys all capacity, but the author clarifies that death is not an 'impediment' in the technical sense because it annihilates the person himself; an impediment should affect capacity while the person remains. Thus, death removes the subject of capacity, not merely its constituents. The passage refines the definition of impediments to legal capacity by excluding external factors that only affect obligations, accountability, or the very existence of the person.

Key Terms
أهلية (ahliyyah): Legal capacity; the quality of a person that makes them susceptible to rights and obligations and able to perform legal acts.
عوارض الأهلية (awāriḍ al-ahliyyah): Impediments that attack the constituents of legal capacity and affect them while the person remains extant.
هزل (hazl): Jest; verbal transactions performed without genuine intention to their meanings and legal effects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is ignorance not considered an impediment to legal capacity?
A) Because ignorance only occurs in cases of new Muslims, not generally.
B) Because ignorance is an excuse that prevents accountability, not an attack on capacity itself.
C) Because ignorance is a form of mistake which is not a defect in capacity.
D) Because the Hanafi school considers ignorance to be irrelevant to legal rulings.

Key Takeaway
Travel, ignorance, and mistake are not true impediments to legal capacity; they only affect the obligation or accountability.

Jest and Death: Boundary Cases in the Theory of Legal Capacity
Critical analysis of jest and death as alleged impediments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate why jest (هزل) is not considered an impediment to legal capacity despite its invalidating effect on contracts, and why death is only technically excluded from the definition of impediments.

Core Idea
Jest negates intention, not capacity; death removes the person, not just capacity.

The author now tackles two extreme candidates: jest (هزل) and death (موت). He argues that jest is the furthest from being an impediment because it involves playing with words without intent; the issue is the missing pillar of resolute expression, not impaired capacity. Death indeed destroys all capacities, but it is not a true impediment because it removes the person entirely, whereas impediments should affect capacity while the person remains.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Hanafi treatment of jest (هزل)
Jest is defined as playing with words without intending their legal meanings. Its locus is verbal transactions.
The author distinguishes between the absence of intent and the absence of capacity.
2
Analyze why jest invalidates contracts
Jest invalidates contracts because the pillar (ركن) of the transaction – a resolute expression of intention – is missing. The deficiency is in the act, not the actor's capacity.
If jest were a capacity issue, then every omitted condition of offer and acceptance would be an impediment.
3
Examine the exception: jest is treated as serious in marriage, divorce, and manumission
In these three areas, the Law considers jest as serious (al-jidd) due to their gravity. This exception reinforces that jest is not about capacity but about legal treatment.
The exception shows the Law's policy, not a change in capacity.
4
Analyze death: how it demolishes capacities but does not fit the definition
Death destroys all capacities because it removes the person. However, the definition of impediment requires the person to remain; an impediment attacks capacity while the subject exists.
Death is thus excluded terminologically, even though it has a more radical effect.
5
Synthesize the refined definition
True impediments are conditions that affect the constituents of capacity while the person lives. Jest and death fail this criterion for opposite reasons: jest affects only the expression, death removes the person.

Chapter Eight: Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. 5 - Travel: The Law only considered the hardship in it as a cause for concession in some religious obligations. Some duties were made optional for the traveler, such as Friday prayer, the two Eid prayers, and the sacrifice; and some were permitted for the traveler to delay from their specific time, like fasting Ramadan. So in travel, as you see, nothing impairs any of a person's capacity for obligation or for performance from a religious or civil perspective. 6 - Ignorance also does not impair any of a person's capacity and abilities; rather it arises for the person in some situations as a legal excuse preventing him from being held accountable for negligence, like one who embraces Islam while far from the Abode of Islam, ignorant of the obligations that Islam imposes on him. Indeed, his ignorance in such a case is considered an excuse preventing him from being held accountable for neglecting religious duties, and like the agent's ignorance of his principal's dismissal; his actions remain effective upon the principal until the news of dismissal reaches him. In this issue, as you see, knowledge is what removes the agent's authority, while ignorance preserves it; so there is no impairment of capacity in ignorance, it is merely an excuse. 7 - Mistake likewise: they defined it as the occurrence of something contrary to the intention of the one from whom it occurred. There is no diminution of a person's faculties and abilities in it, nor any connection or relation to them. Rather it is a description of actions issuing from a person that negates intent from them, which normally exists in the case of premeditation, and thus necessitates lifting or mitigating accountability. - And furthest of all from the meaning of impediments to legal capacity is jest!! For jest is the opposite of seriousness; its locus is verbal transactions, and it is playing with words without intending their meanings and rulings. According to the jurists, it results in the invalidity or corruption of contracts and transactions, with a preceding disagreement about that, due to the absence of the true intention of the contracting party therein, not due to loss or deficiency of the capacity of the contracting party. Evidence is that the Law considered jest like seriousness in some transactions as an exception, namely marriage, divorce, and manumission, as previously mentioned in volume 6, page 33. 856 First Appendix to Chapter Eight: Objections Regarding Impediments to Legal Capacity. So the reality of the matter in jest is the absence of the pillar of the verbal transaction, because its pillar is a resolute expression of intention; by jest in it, the indication of this expression to intention is negated. If it were acceptable to consider jest as an impediment to legal capacity, it would also be necessary to consider the absence of all other conditions of offer and acceptance in contracts as impediments to legal capacity!! 9 - As for death, there truly is the demolition of all capacities. From this aspect, it might be possible to consider it from the impediments to legal capacity. Word. But from another aspect, it is seen that death is not an impediment that directly attacks the constituents of legal capacity; rather it falls upon the life of the person and removes him from existence. As for the demolition of capacities by it, it is consequent upon the removal of the person who is the locus, because capacity is a quality of the person, and there is no survival of the quality without the described. And the apparent meaning of 'impediments to legal capacity' is that they are impediments that attack its constituents and affect them while the person remains, such that the person loses due to this impediment what he should have enjoyed of capacity; so he remains a person who is not legally capable. And this is not attainable in the case of death due to the removal of personality as such; hence death is not from the impediments to legal capacity in this sense.

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical examination of what constitutes a true impediment to legal capacity (عوارض الأهلية). The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, systematically argues that several factors commonly mistaken as impediments are not genuine bars to capacity. For travel (safar), the Law grants concessions due to hardship but does not affect a person's capacity to be obligated or to perform. Ignorance (jahl) is only an excuse excusing accountability in specific contexts, such as a new Muslim unaware of duties or an agent unaware of dismissal; it does not impair inherent capacity. Mistake (khata) is simply the absence of intent and does not diminish faculties; it may lift or mitigate accountability. Jest (hazl) is the farthest from being an impediment: it involves verbal transactions without real intent. The Hanafi school holds that jest can invalidate contracts, but it is due to missing the pillar of resolute expression, not due to deficient capacity. Indeed, in marriage, divorce, and manumission, jest is treated as serious (istithna'). Finally, death (mawt) indeed destroys all capacity, but the author clarifies that death is not an 'impediment' in the technical sense because it annihilates the person himself; an impediment should affect capacity while the person remains. Thus, death removes the subject of capacity, not merely its constituents. The passage refines the definition of impediments to legal capacity by excluding external factors that only affect obligations, accountability, or the very existence of the person.

Key Terms
هزل (hazl): Jest; verbal transactions performed without genuine intention to their meanings and legal effects.
موت (mawt): Death; the cessation of life which removes the person from existence.
ركن (rukn): Pillar; an essential element of an act or contract, without which the act is invalid.
أهلية (ahliyyah): Legal capacity; the quality of a person that makes them susceptible to rights and obligations and able to perform legal acts.

Refining the Definition of ‘Awarid al-Ahliyya: Why Jest and Death Are Not True Impediments
Critical refinement of the definition of ‘awarid al-ahliyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi definition of ‘awarid al-ahliyya to distinguish true impediments (like insanity) from cases that negate the act or the person (jest and death).

Core Idea
True ‘awarid affect capacity while the person remains; jest negates the act, death negates the person.

In this passage, we refine a key concept in Hanafi legal capacity: what exactly is an ‘arid (impediment) to ahliyya? The author probes two borderline cases—jest (hazl) and death (mawt)—to clarify that they do not belong to the category. By understanding why they fail the definition, we sharpen our grasp of what true ‘awarid are and how they operate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of ‘awarid al-ahliyya
From the passage, extract the implicit definition: ‘awarid are impediments that affect the components of legal capacity while the person remains alive, causing partial or total loss without removing the person.
True ‘awarid include insanity, idiocy, sleep, etc.
2
Analyze jest (hazl) against the definition
Jest does not affect capacity; it negates the pillar of verbal disposition (definitive expression of will). The person retains full capacity, but the act lacks an essential element. If jest were an ‘arid, then every missing condition of offer and acceptance would be an ‘arid—absurd.
Thus, jest is a defect in the act, not in the person.
3
Analyze death (mawt) against the definition
Death does not merely impede capacity; it removes the person entirely. Capacity is an attribute of the person; without the subject, no attribute persists. Since true ‘awarid require the person to exist (though incapacitated), death falls outside the concept.
Death terminates all capacities, but it is not an ‘arid in the technical sense.
4
Synthesize the refined ruling
Combine the two analyses: the definition of ‘awarid al-ahliyya excludes any factor that negates an act element (like hazl) or negates the person (like mawt). Only factors that impair existing capacities without destroying the person qualify.
This is a crucial dabt (precision) in usul al-fiqh.

The truth of the matter regarding jest is that it is the negation of the pillar of verbal disposition, because its pillar is a definitive expression of will; by jest in it, the indication of this expression of will is negated. If jest were to be considered an impediment of legal capacity, then the negation of all other conditions of offer and acceptance in contracts would also have to be considered impediments of legal capacity!! As for death, indeed it demolishes all capacities. From this aspect, it could be considered an impediment of legal capacity. But from another aspect, it is seen that death is not an impediment that directly affects the components of legal capacity; rather it falls upon the life of the person and removes him from existence. The demolition of capacities by it is subsidiary to the removal of the person who is their locus, because legal capacity is an attribute of the person, and an attribute does not persist without the described. The intended meaning of 'impediments of legal capacity' is that they are impediments that occur to its components and affect them while the person persists, whereby the person loses due to this impediment what he should have enjoyed of legal capacity, so he remains a person not fully capacitated. This does not obtain in the case of death because of the removal of the personality as such; thus death is not from the impediments of legal capacity in this sense.
Source pages p. 805

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the concept of 'awarid al-ahliyya (impediments to legal capacity). The author examines two contested cases: hazl (jest) and mawt (death). First, regarding jest: the author argues that jest does not affect the capacity itself; rather it negates the contractual pillar of verbal expression (rukn al-tasarruf al-qawli). Since a contract requires a definitive expression of will, a jesting statement fails to indicate true will. If we were to consider jest an impediment to capacity, we would have to similarly treat the absence of any condition of offer and acceptance (e.g., sanity, intent) as an impediment, which is incorrect. Thus jest is not an 'arid of ahliyya but a defect in the act. Second, regarding death: death indeed destroys all capacities, so from one angle it might be considered an impediment. However, the author clarifies that death removes the person himself, not merely a capacity component. Since ahliyya is an attribute of the person, it cannot exist without its subject. The proper definition of 'arid al-ahliyya requires that the person remains but loses part of his capacity. Death is therefore not an 'arid in this technical sense. This analysis is based on the Hanafi understanding of ahliyya as a legal attribute attached to the living person.

Key Terms
الهزل (al-hazl): Jest; speaking without serious intent, which negates the contractual requirement of a definitive expression of will.
الموت (al-mawt): Death; the end of life, which removes the person entirely, and thus ahliyya as an attribute of the person ceases.
عوارض الأهلية (‘awarid al-ahliyya): Impediments that affect the components of legal capacity while the person remains alive, causing partial or total loss of capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because jest is a sin and thus not relevant to capacity
B) Because jest negates the pillar of verbal disposition, not the capacity itself
C) Because jest only occurs in contracts, not in other areas
D) Because jest is allowed in Islamic law

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi concept of ‘awarid al-ahliyya requires that the impediment affect capacity while the person remains, excluding both jest (which affects the act) and death (which removes the person).

Five-Stage Division of Legal Capacity: Separating Bulugh and Rushd
Proposal for a refined taxonomy of ahliyya stages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the traditional four-stage division of ahliyya, explain why the author separates bulugh from rushd, and apply the five-stage scheme to a hypothetical case.

Core Idea
Puberty triggers obligation; prudence triggers full dispositional capacity — they are two distinct stages.

In traditional Hanafi usul, legal capacity stages are often divided into four: fetus, non-discerning child, discerning child, and the combined stage of puberty/prudence. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, argues that this conflation obscures crucial legal distinctions. He proposes a five-stage model that separates puberty (bulugh) as the threshold for taklif and penal responsibility from prudence (rushd) as the threshold for full financial discretion. This new division resolves several problematic rulings in ahliyya.

Guided Path
1
Recall the traditional Hanafi stages of ahliyya
List the typical four stages: (1) janin (fetus), (2) tifl ghayr mumayyiz (non-discerning child), (3) tifl mumayyiz (discerning child), (4) baligh/rashid (pubert and prudent combined). Identify that in this scheme, bulugh and rushd are merged into one stage.
Some later Hanafi jurists already distinguished between bulugh and rushd in practice, but the author claims the stage classification was conflated.
2
Identify the author's critique of the traditional division
The author argues that merging bulugh and rushd fails to account for the distinct legal functions: bulugh is required for taklif (obligation) and penal responsibility, while rushd is required for full dispositional capacity in financial matters, which requires practical experience.
The author's critique is based on an analysis of the rulings themselves, not on a different interpretation of sources.
3
Understand the five-stage proposal
The author proposes: (1) janin, (2) tifl ghayr mumayyiz, (3) tifl mumayyiz (age of discernment), (4) baligh ghayr rashid (pubert but not yet prudent), (5) baligh rashid (pubert and prudent). Stages 4 and 5 replace the single combined stage.
Stage 4 is recognized in Hanafi law for limited capacity: the minor after puberty can perform some transactions with guardian approval.
4
Analyze the legal implications of separating rushd
Rushd is not automatic at puberty; it requires demonstrated financial judgment. The guardian may withhold property until rushd is confirmed, even after bulugh. This distinction clarifies rulings on interdiction (hajr) and the validity of transactions by minors.
The Hanafi school already allows testing the minor's financial acumen after puberty before handing over wealth, which supports the author's separation.
5
Apply the five-stage scheme to a concrete scenario
Consider a 16-year-old who has reached puberty but has no financial experience. Under the five-stage model, they are in stage 4: they are obligated to pray and fast, but their financial transactions are subject to guardian oversight until rushd is established. This aligns with Hanafi fiqh.
The five-stage model simply explicates what is implicit in the rulings.
