الملحق الثاني بالباب الثامن مزية تركيزنا العلمي الجديد لنظرية الأهلية ١٢/٦٦ - يجد القارىء في نظرية الأهلية بالصورة التي حررناها بحثاً وتحقيقاً جديداً يختلف في بعض الأمور عن المأثور في المباحث التقليدية للأهلية في مصادرها الأصلية من كتب الأصول، وفيما نقله المؤلفون المعاصرون عنها. وقد دعانا إلى ذلك أننا وجدنا بعض عقد مشكلة في مسائل الأهلية لا يحلها إلا فهم جديد في تقسيمها وترتيبها وتوزيع أحكامها. فمن التحقيقات الجديدة التي يراها القارىء هنا : أ - أننا جعلنا مراحل الأهلية بحسب أطوار نشأة الإنسان خمساً، بينا أنَّ علماء أصول الفقه والمؤلفين المعاصرين الذين نقلوا عنهم نقلاً تقليدياً، يقسمونها إلى أربع مراحل، إذ يدمجون البلوغ والرشد فيجعلون منهما مرحلة رابعة أخيرة تكمل فيها أهلية الأداء. لكننا رأينا من النظر والتحقيق في الأحكام الشرعية أنَّ البلوغ محطة تشترط للتكليف الشرعي، وأن الرشد محطة لكمال أهلية التصرف التي تعتمد خبرة عملية مالية فوق إدراك الخير والشر الكافي للتكليف ولحمل المسؤولية العقابية . فيجب أن يعتبر بين سنّ التمييز الذي تبدأ فيه أهلية الأداء، وبين الرشد الذي تتكامل فيه مرحلتان لا واحدة فقط البلوغ ثم الرشد كما تقدم .
Appendix 2 to Chapter Eight: The Merit of Our New Scientific Focus on the Theory of Legal Capacity. 12/66 – The reader will find in the theory of legal capacity in the form we have written a new research and investigation that differs in some matters from what is transmitted in the traditional discussions of legal capacity in its original sources from books of usul, and from what contemporary authors have transmitted from them. We were led to this because we found some problematic knots in issues of legal capacity that cannot be resolved except by a new understanding of its division, arrangement, and distribution of its rulings. Among the new investigations that the reader sees here: A) We have made the stages of legal capacity according to the developmental phases of a human being five, whereas the scholars of usul al-fiqh and contemporary authors who transmitted from them traditionally divide them into four stages, as they merge puberty and prudence into a single fourth final stage in which the capacity for execution is completed. But we saw, through examination and investigation of the legal rulings, that puberty is a station required for legal obligation, and that prudence is a station for the completion of the capacity for disposition, which depends on practical financial experience beyond the perception of good and evil sufficient for legal obligation and bearing penal responsibility. So it is necessary to consider between the age of discernment, at which the capacity for execution begins, and prudence, at which it is completed, two stages, not just one: puberty, then prudence, as mentioned.
Source pages p. 806

Commentary
This passage is a methodological proposal from the author regarding the classification of ahliyya (legal capacity) stages. The traditional division in Hanafi usul al-fiqh recognizes three or four stages: (1) the fetus (janin), (2) infancy and non-discerning childhood, (3) the age of discernment (tamyiz) up to puberty, and (4) puberty (bulugh) and prudence (rushd) combined as the final stage where full capacity is attained. The author argues that this conflates two distinct legal phenomena: bulugh is the threshold for taklif (legal obligation) and criminal responsibility, while rushd is the threshold for full dispositional capacity in financial matters, requiring practical experience. Therefore, in the author's revised scheme, there are five stages: (1) fetus, (2) non-discerning child, (3) child of discernment (mumayyiz), (4) adolescent after puberty but before rushd, and (5) adult with rushd. This new division aims to resolve certain problematic issues in ahliyya rulings, such as the validity of a minor's transactions after puberty but before rushd, which Hanafi law allows with guardian oversight. The author's analysis is based on a nuanced reading of shariah rulings and is presented as an improvement over traditional classifications.

Key Terms
البلوغ (al-bulugh): Puberty; the stage marking the onset of legal obligation (taklif) and capacity for punishment.
الرشد (al-rushd): Prudence; the stage of mature judgment in financial matters, required for full dispositional capacity.
مراحل الأهلية (marahil al-ahliyya): Stages of legal capacity according to human development; the author proposes five stages.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What change does the author propose to the traditional division of ahliyya stages?
A) He adds a stage for old age
B) He separates puberty from prudence as distinct stages, making five total
C) He merges discernment and puberty into one stage
D) He reduces the stages from four to three

Key Takeaway
Puberty and prudence are distinct legal thresholds: bulugh for obligation and penal capacity, rushd for full financial disposition — a separation that clarifies many rulings on legal capacity.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Bipartite Division of Capacity: Religious and Civil
Presents a novel subdivision of capacity of performance into religious and civil components

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Zarqa's distinction between religious and civil capacity and apply it to determine the validity of a discerning minor's acts of worship and financial transactions.

Core Idea
Capacity of performance splits at discernment: religious capacity is full, civil capacity is deficient until maturity.

The traditional Hanafi theory of legal capacity (أهلية الأداء) treats it as a single capacity that gradually develops from childhood to adulthood. Mustafa al-Zarqa, a modern Hanafi legal scholar, proposes a fundamental rethinking: capacity of performance consists of two distinct components—religious capacity (أهلية التعبد) and civil capacity (أهلية التصرف). This lesson examines the evidence and implications of this novel classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the traditional view
Recall that classical usul scholars divide capacity into four stages: non-discerning child, discerning child (with deficient capacity), adolescent after puberty (full capacity for worship but deficient for transactions), and mature adult. They do not explicitly separate religious and civil capacity.
Traditional view: one capacity, gradual completion.
2
Understand the problem al-Zarqa identifies
Al-Zarqa finds that the traditional four-stage model leaves unresolved issues. He argues that puberty (بلوغ) alone is insufficient for full capacity of disposition; maturity (رشد) is a separate station requiring practical financial experience.
Problem: merging puberty and maturity into one stage.
3
Grasp the proposed bipartite division
Al-Zarqa splits capacity of performance into religious capacity (for acts of worship) and civil capacity (for financial transactions). Religious capacity is complete from the start of discernment (تمييز), while civil capacity begins deficient and only becomes complete with maturity.
Two capacities: religious (full early) and civil (deficient until rushd).
4
Examine the evidence from fiqh rulings
The proof lies in the rulings for the discerning minor: acts of worship (e.g., prayer) are valid and binding without guardian permission, even if the guardian forbids them. Financial transactions are void or voidable unless approved by the guardian. The Prophet's hadith commanding children to pray at seven confirms the validity of worship.
Evidence: hadith of prayer at seven; fiqh rules on minor's transactions.
5
Apply to a concrete case
Consider a discerning minor (age 10) who performs prayer and also sells a valuable item. Apply the bipartite division: the prayer is valid due to full religious capacity; the sale is non-binding, subject to guardian approval.
Application: distinguish between worship and transactions.

وقد دعانا إلى ذلك أننا وجدنا بعض عقد مشكلة في مسائل الأهلية لا يحلها إلا فهم جديد في تقسيمها وترتيبها وتوزيع أحكامها.

Appendix 2 to Chapter 8
The merit of our new scientific focus on the theory of legal capacity.
12/66 - The reader finds in the theory of legal capacity in the form we have edited a new research and investigation, differing in some matters from what is transmitted in the traditional studies of capacity in their original sources from books of usul, and from what contemporary authors have transmitted from them.
We were led to this because we found some problematic knots in issues of capacity that can only be solved by a new understanding in its division, arrangement, and distribution of its rulings.
Among the new investigations that the reader sees here: A - We made the stages of capacity according to the phases of human development five, whereas the scholars of usul al-fiqh and contemporary authors who transmitted from them traditionally divide it into four stages, merging puberty and maturity into a fourth final stage in which capacity of performance is completed.
But we saw from consideration and investigation of the legal rulings that puberty is a station required for legal obligation, and maturity is a station for the completion of capacity of disposition, which depends on practical financial experience beyond the discernment of good and evil sufficient for obligation and bearing penal responsibility.
So there must be considered between the age of discernment in which capacity of performance begins and maturity in which it is completed two stages, not just one: puberty then maturity, as stated.
Chapter 8: Theory of Capacity and Guardianship
B - We split capacity of performance into two parts: religious and civil, or capacity of worship and capacity of disposition. We have shown that the first begins complete at the start of the age of discernment. As for the second, it begins deficient at the age of discernment, then is completed by maturity, not merely by sane puberty sufficient for legal obligation.
This division of capacity of performance and the separation between its religious and civil parts in the stages of completion is not explicitly stated by the jurists and usul scholars in the division of capacity; rather they say that the discerning minor enjoys deficient capacity of performance until he reaches puberty. But we have deduced this division and separation from the rulings that they establish for the discerning minor, for they indicate it:
1. It is established in fiqh that the deficiency of capacity of performance in a person makes his transaction that is ambivalent between benefit and harm non-binding, but subject to the permission of his guardian or executor; and that the completion of this capacity frees him from this subjection and makes his actions binding and productive of effects by his mere will.
2. And we have seen by tracing and induction that what is subject to permission from the acts of the discerning minor is only his financial dispositions; they are those that are either void or voidable due to deficiency of capacity.
As for the acts of worship of the discerning minor, they are valid and binding without being contingent on his guardian's permission. Even if his guardian forbids him from prayer and he prays a prayer fulfilling its legal conditions, it is valid despite the prohibition.
3. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: Command your children to pray when they are seven years old, as stated.
Hence we saw that the statement of the jurists and usul scholars about the deficiency of capacity of performance in the discerning minor refers to his capacity for civil dispositions. As for his capacity for bodily acts of worship,
Source pages p. 806

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical reformulation of the traditional Hanafi theory of legal capacity (أهلية). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, introduces an appendix to Chapter 8, presenting his new scientific focus. He argues that the traditional division of capacity into four stages (non-discerning child, discerning child, adolescent, mature adult) is insufficient. He proposes five stages, separating puberty (بلوغ) and maturity (رشد) into distinct stages. More importantly, he splits capacity of performance (أهلية الأداء) into two components: religious capacity (أهلية التعبد/الدينية) and civil capacity (أهلية التصرف/المدنية). The religious capacity is complete from the age of discernment (تمييز), evidenced by the validity of a discerning minor's worship without guardian permission, as per the hadith commanding children to pray at seven. The civil capacity begins deficient and reaches completion only with maturity (رشد), not merely puberty. This distinction, though not explicit in classical Hanafi works, is deduced from their rulings: a minor's financial transactions are subject to guardian approval, while acts of worship are not. The author presents this as a novel contribution, claiming it solves problematic knots in the traditional theory.

Key Terms
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-ada'): Capacity of performance: the legal capacity to perform acts that have legal consequences. The author splits it into religious (عبادات) and civil (تصرفات).
الصغير المميز (al-saghir al-mumayyiz): The discerning minor: a child who has reached the age of discernment (usually around 7) but not yet puberty.
الرشد (rushd): Maturity: the ability to manage financial affairs prudently, based on practical experience. Required for full civil capacity.
أهلية التعبد (ahliyyat al-ta'abbud): Religious capacity: the capacity to perform acts of worship validly.
أهلية التصرف (ahliyyat al-tasarruf): Civil capacity: the capacity to conduct financial transactions and other legal dispositions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Al-Zarqa's bipartite division of capacity of performance into religious and civil components clarifies why a discerning minor's worship is valid while financial transactions require guardian approval, providing a more coherent theoretical framework for Hanafi rulings.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

The Division of Legal Capacity: Religious vs. Civil
Arguing for a division of capacity of performance into religious and civil components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the division of ahliyyat al-ada' into religious and civil capacity to distinguish between the validity of a discerning minor's acts of worship and the conditionality of their financial transactions, and explain how this resolves the apparent contradiction between puberty and rushd.

Core Idea
A discerning minor's worship is valid without permission, but his financial transactions require guardian approval until rushd—capacity is not uniform.

Classical Hanafi scholars speak of 'deficient capacity of performance' for discerning minors, but a closer look reveals that this deficiency only applies to civil transactions. Acts of worship are fully valid from the age of tamyiz. In this lesson, we explore al-Zarqa's refinement of the theory of ahliyyah, showing how separating religious from civil capacity resolves a key tension in the fiqh of legal capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of capacity of performance
The author divides ahliyyat al-ada' into religious (capacity for worship) and civil (capacity for transactions). Note the Arabic terms: أهلية الأداء الدينية and أهلية الأداء المدنية.
Religious capacity is also called 'capacity for worship' (أهلية التعبد) and civil capacity 'capacity for transaction' (أهلية التصرف).
2
Trace the starting points for each component
Religious capacity begins complete at tamyiz (age of discernment, around 7). Civil capacity begins deficient at tamyiz and becomes complete with rushd (sound judgment), not mere puberty.
Rushd is required per Quran 4:6: 'And test the orphans until they reach the age of marriage; then if you perceive in them sound judgment, release their property to them.'
3
Examine the evidentiary basis for the division
The author infers this from fiqh rulings: a discerning minor's worship is valid without guardian permission, but financial transactions are subject to permission (void or voidable). Also, the hadith 'Command your children to pray at seven' supports religious capacity from tamyiz.
Hadith: 'مروا أولادكم بالصلاة وهم أبناء سبع' (Abu Dawud).
4
Apply the division to resolve the problem
The apparent contradiction is that puberty is said to complete capacity for obligation, yet financial capacity requires rushd after puberty. Solution: religious capacity is complete from tamyiz (acts of worship valid, though not obligatory until puberty), while civil capacity requires rushd. Capacity qualifies but does not obligate.
Thus, the classical statement 'capacity is complete at puberty' refers to religious capacity, not civil.

أما عبادات الصغير المميز فهي صحيحة نافذة دون توقف على إجازة وليه. حتى إنه لو منعه وليه من الصلاة فصلى صلاة مستوفية لشرائطها الشرعية كانت صحيحة رغم منعه .

ملحق ثاني بالباب الثامن: مزية تركيزنا لنظرية الأهلية
فتبدأ كاملة منذ التمييز وإن كان غير مكلف شرعاً بهذه العبادات لضعفه، رفقاً من الشرع به .
وعلى هذا قسمنا أهلية الأداء ذلك التقسيم إلى دينية ومدنية بالمعنى المتقدم البيان .
وبذلك يندفع إشكال مهم في موضوع الأهلية، وهو أن الفقهاء يجعلون البلوغ مبدأ للتكليف وحمل الواجبات والمسؤوليات، مما يوهم كمال الأهليات. وقد يصرحون بهذا الكمال في البلوغ، بينما يصرحون في مواطن أخرى بأن مال القاصر لا يُسَلَّم إليه ولا يطلق له التصرف فيه بمجرد البلوغ بل بظهور الرشد، مع البلوغ أو بعده وفقاً للآية القرآنية. فبهذا التمييز الذي ميزنا فيه بين أهلية الأداء المدنية والدينية يندفع الإشكال، إذ يتضح :
1 - أنَّ كمال أهلية الأداء المدنية مناطه الرشد المالي لا مجرد البلوغ. ۲ - وأن أهلية الأداء الدينية تبدأ كاملة منذ التمييز وتستمر. فتصح بها العبادات، وإن كان الشخص غير مكلف بها، لأنّ الأهلية إنما تؤهل ولا توجب كما تقدم. فخطاب التكليف الشرعي بالواجبات يتوقف على أمور غير الأهلية في طليعتها البلوغ. ج - أننا أخرجنا من عوارض الأهلية طائفة كبيرة اعتيد عدها منها وهي لا تمت بصفة أصلاً إلى معنى العوارض، وقد دعمنا رأينا هذا تقليداً، بالحجج المفصلة.
وبهذا النظر الجديد في نظرية الأهلية قد ركزت فروعها في مواقعها من الجذع وانضبط فيها تفريع الأحكام وزال كل غموض واشتباه كان يراه الباحث في آفاق الأهلية ومراحلها.
Chapter Eight: The Theory of Legal Capacity and Guardianship. B - We have divided capacity of performance into two parts: religious and civil, or capacity for worship and capacity for transaction, and we have shown that the first of them begins complete at the start of the age of discernment. As for the second, it begins deficient at the age of discernment, then becomes complete with sound judgment, not merely with rational puberty sufficient for legal responsibility. This division of capacity of performance and the differentiation between its two parts, religious and civil, in the stages of completion, is not explicitly stated by jurists and usul scholars in their division of capacity; rather, they state generally that a discerning minor possesses deficient capacity of performance until he reaches puberty. But we have inferred this division and differentiation from the rulings they establish for the discerning minor, for they indicate it: 1 - It is established in fiqh that deficiency of capacity of performance in a person makes his transaction that oscillates between benefit and harm non-binding, rather subject to the permission of his guardian or executor; and that completeness of this capacity liberates him from this subjection and makes his actions binding and productive of their effects by his own volition. 2 - And we have seen through investigation and induction that what is subject to permission from the actions of the discerning minor is only his financial transactions, for among them are void and voidable due to deficiency of capacity. As for the acts of worship of the discerning minor, they are valid and binding without being conditional upon the permission of his guardian. Even if his guardian prevents him from prayer and he prays a prayer fulfilling its legal conditions, it is valid despite the prevention. 3 - And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Command your children to pray when they are seven years old' as mentioned before. Hence we saw that the statement of jurists and usul scholars about the deficiency of capacity of performance in the discerning minor is meant for his capacity for civil transactions. As for his capacity for physical acts of worship, it begins complete from discernment, even if he is not legally obligated to these acts of worship due to his weakness, as a mercy from the Lawgiver to him. And on this basis we divided capacity of performance into religious and civil in the sense previously explained. And thereby an important problem in the subject of capacity is resolved, namely that jurists make puberty the beginning of obligation and of bearing duties and responsibilities, which suggests the perfection of capacities. They may explicitly state this perfection at puberty, while they state elsewhere that the property of a minor is not handed over to him nor is he allowed to dispose of it merely by puberty, but by the appearance of sound judgment, with puberty or after it according to the Quranic verse. Thus, by this distinction we have made between civil and religious capacity of performance, the problem is resolved, for it becomes clear: 1 - That the perfection of civil capacity of performance is contingent on financial sound judgment, not merely puberty. 2 - That religious capacity of performance begins complete from discernment and continues. So acts of worship are valid by it, even if the person is not obligated to them, because capacity only qualifies and does not obligate, as previously mentioned. For the address of legal obligation of duties depends on matters other than capacity, foremost among them puberty. C - That we have removed from the impediments of capacity a large group that was customarily counted among them, and they have no relation at all to the meaning of impediments, and we have supported this opinion of ours, following tradition, with detailed proofs. And by this new view of the theory of capacity, its branches have been anchored in their positions on the trunk, and the derivation of rulings has been regulated, and all ambiguity and confusion that the researcher used to see in the horizons of capacity and its stages has been removed.
Source pages p. 807

Commentary
This passage is a theoretical exposition refining the Hanafi theory of legal capacity (ahliyyah). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, proposes a division of capacity of performance (ahliyyat al-ada') into religious (dini) and civil (madani) components. He argues that classical jurists and usul scholars did not explicitly make this distinction but they implicitly recognized it by treating the acts of worship of a discerning minor as valid without guardian permission, while financial transactions remain subject to permission. The author uses this division to resolve a apparent contradiction: puberty (bulugh) is considered the point of full capacity for religious obligations, but financial capacity requires rushd (sound judgment) even after puberty. By separating the two, he shows that religious capacity is complete from discernment (tamyiz), while civil capacity completes only with rushd. He also claims to have removed many items from the list of impediments (awarid) of capacity. This is a modern scholar's attempt to systematize the classical Hanafi rulings. The argument is based on inductive inference (istiqra') from established fiqh rulings and the hadith about commanding children to pray at seven.

Key Terms
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-ada'): The legal capacity to perform actions that produce legal effects; divided by the author into religious and civil.
التمييز (al-tamyiz): The age of discernment, typically around seven, when a child can understand the basic meaning of actions and speech.
الرشد (al-rushd): Sound judgment and financial maturity, distinct from mere biological puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the author's division of capacity of performance into religious and civil?
A) Direct statements from classical Hanafi jurists.
B) Inference from the rulings they established for the discerning minor.
C) A hadith from the Prophet about financial transactions.
D) The consensus of all Islamic legal schools.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi theory of legal capacity is refined by differentiating religious capacity (complete from tamyiz) from civil capacity (complete only with rushd), resolving the apparent tension between puberty and financial maturity.

Distinction Between Religious and Civil Legal Capacity
Theoretical refinement of Hanafi capacity theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the difference between religious and civil legal capacity and apply the distinction to resolve the apparent contradiction between puberty and rushd in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Religious capacity begins at tamyiz, civil capacity requires rushd—the two are not the same.

This passage by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa presents a key refinement in the Hanafi theory of legal capacity (ahliyya). Many students are puzzled by the fact that puberty is said to bring full capacity, yet a pubescent minor cannot freely dispose of property. Al-Zarqa resolves this by distinguishing between religious capacity (for acts of worship) and civil capacity (for financial transactions). This distinction harmonizes the rulings and clarifies the stages of capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
Puberty is declared as the start of full obligation and capacity, yet the law restricts financial disposition until rushd. Recognize this tension as the motivation for the distinction.
The Quranic verse (4:6) explicitly ties financial independence to rushd, not just puberty.
2
Understand the two types of capacity
Religious capacity (ahliyyat al-ada' al-diniyya) begins complete from tamyiz, validating acts of worship even before obligation. Civil capacity (ahliyyat al-ada' al-madaniyya) is only complete upon rushd.
Al-Zarqa's division is a modern formulation but rooted in classical Hanafi rulings.
3
Apply the distinction to a scenario
Take a discerning minor (sabi mumayyiz) who performs salah or buys a small item. The salah is valid (religious capacity) even if not obligatory; the purchase is valid only if he has rushd (civil capacity).
4
Recognize the removal of false impediments
Al-Zarqa notes that many conditions traditionally listed as impediments (awarid) are not true impediments. This pruning is part of his systematization.
This step is more theoretical but shows the broader impact of his approach.

Second appendix to Chapter Eight: The advantage of our focus on the theory of legal capacity. It begins complete from the age of discernment (tamyiz), even if he is not legally obligated for these acts of worship due to his weakness, out of compassion from the Lawgiver. And upon this we divided legal capacity for performance into religious and civil in the sense previously explained. And by this an important difficulty in the subject of legal capacity is removed, namely that jurists make puberty the beginning of obligation and bearing duties and responsibilities, giving the impression of completeness of capacity. And they may explicitly state this completeness at puberty, while in other places they state that the property of a minor is not handed over to him nor is he allowed to dispose of it merely upon puberty, but upon the appearance of sound judgment (rushd), with puberty or after it, according to the Quranic verse. So by this distinction we made between civil and religious legal capacity, the difficulty is removed, it becomes clear: 1. That the perfection of civil legal capacity depends on financial sound judgment, not merely puberty. 2. That religious legal capacity begins complete from discernment and continues. Thus acts of worship are valid through it, even if the person is not obligated for them, because capacity only qualifies and does not obligate, as previously stated. So the address of legal obligation for duties depends on matters other than capacity, foremost among them puberty. 3. That we have removed from the impediments of capacity a large group that was customarily counted among them, while they are not at all related to the meaning of impediments, and we have supported our opinion with detailed proofs. And with this new view of the theory of capacity, its branches have been fixed in their positions on the trunk, the derivation of rulings has become precise, and all ambiguity and confusion that the researcher used to see in the horizons and stages of capacity have disappeared.
Source pages p. 808

Commentary
This passage is a theoretical discussion by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa on the theory of legal capacity (ahliyya). He presents his own classification of capacity for performance (ahliyyat al-ada') into two types: religious (diniyya) and civil (madaniyya). The purpose is to resolve an apparent contradiction in Hanafi fiqh: while puberty is said to mark the beginning of full legal capacity and obligation, the law does not allow a minor who has just reached puberty to freely dispose of his property; rather, financial maturity (rushd) is required. By distinguishing between religious capacity (which starts from tamyiz and is complete from that point, validating acts of worship even without obligation) and civil capacity (which requires rushd for full perfection), the contradiction is resolved. Al-Zarqa also notes that many conditions typically listed as impediments (awarid) to capacity are not truly impediments, and he has removed them based on detailed proofs. This analysis aims to systematize the fiqh rulings on capacity and remove ambiguity.

Key Terms
أهلية الأداء (ahliyyat al-ada'): Legal capacity for performance: the fitness of a person for the validity of his actions, whether they are acts of worship or transactions.
أهلية الأداء الدينية (ahliyyat al-ada' al-diniyya): Religious capacity for performance: the capacity to validly perform acts of worship, which starts from the age of discernment (tamyiz) and is complete from then on, even if the person is not yet obligated.
أهلية الأداء المدنية (ahliyyat al-ada' al-madaniyya): Civil capacity for performance: the capacity to conduct financial transactions and exercise property rights, which becomes complete only upon reaching financial maturity (rushd), not merely puberty.
الرشد (al-rushd): Sound judgment in financial matters; the ability to manage property properly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Puberty (bulugh) alone
B) The age of discernment (tamyiz)
C) Financial sound judgment (rushd)
D) Reaching the age of fifteen

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi theory of capacity distinguishes between religious capacity (starting at tamyiz) and civil capacity (requiring rushd), resolving the apparent contradiction between puberty and financial maturity.

Origin and Definition of Habit and Custom
Psychological and social origin of habit and custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define habit (al-'ādah) and custom (al-'urf) and explain how custom emerges from individual habits through imitation and social spread.

Core Idea
Custom is the collective habit that emerges when an individual habit is imitated and spreads among the majority.

Before we explore how custom ('urf) operates as a subsidiary source of law in the Hanafi school, we need to understand what it is and where it comes from. This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm gives us a clear psychological and sociological foundation: every voluntary action has a motive, and when repeated with comfort, it becomes a personal habit. When that habit is imitated by others and spreads widely, it becomes the custom of the group. This same process applies to both actions and speech, forming the basis for language and technical terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the motive behind voluntary actions
Recognize that every voluntary action has a driving force (al-bā'ith), which can be external (e.g., perceived benefit) or internal (e.g., emotions like revenge or shyness).
This step sets the foundation for understanding why actions are initiated.
2
Trace the formation of personal habit (al-'ādah)
When a person feels comfortable with an action and repeats it due to that motive, it becomes a personal habit. Repetition is key.
Habit is individual and rooted in personal comfort.
3
Follow the emergence of custom (al-'urf) from habit
If another person imitates that habit out of the natural tendency to imitate, and the imitation spreads among most individuals, the personal habit becomes a group habit—that is, custom.
Custom is essentially the habit of the group (ʿādah al-jamāʿah).
4
Extend the model to speech
The same process applies to language: humans develop words for communication (habit of speech), and specialized fields coin or reuse terms through repeated usage, forming technical custom.
This shows custom operates in both actions and statements.

Chapter Sixty-Seven: The Origin of Habit (adah) and Custom (urf), Their Definitions, and the Relationship Between Them. First Section: The Origin of Habit and Custom. 3/67 - Every voluntary action must have a motive. This motive is either external, like the appearance of benefit from a thing or action, or internal and psychological, like the love of revenge that drives one to take vengeance, or extreme shyness that prompts silence or veiling in certain situations from certain people. When a person feels comfortable with the action towards which he is inclined by that motive, and repeats it, it becomes a habit for him by repetition. Then, if another person imitates him in that action driven by the love of imitation, and this imitation is repeated and spreads among most individuals, thereby custom is formed, which is in reality the habit of the group. 4/67 - And just as this occurs in actions, it also occurs in statements. A: Man is forced to communicate with those among whom he lives. Communication through gestures and the like is difficult and slow. Therefore, humans resorted to the method of expression through words, which are sounds they become accustomed to using until they become a common language among them. B: Then, with the expansion of industries and civil sciences, expression in the original common language in these industries and sciences becomes difficult, lengthy, and gives rise to ambiguity. Therefore, the people of professions, sciences, or religions resort to using special terms that they agree upon by way of initial coinage or by way of repeated usage to indicate specific meanings.
Source pages p. 811

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm defines the origin of habit (adah) and custom (urf). The author explains that every voluntary action has a motive, either external (like perceived benefit) or internal (like emotions). When a person repeats an action due to comfort with it, it becomes a personal habit. When others imitate that habit and it spreads widely, it becomes a custom – essentially the habit of the group. This applies to both actions and speech. For speech, the need for communication leads to the development of language, and specialized fields develop their own terminology through coinage or repeated usage. In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, custom ('urf) is recognized as a subsidiary source of law, and this passage lays the psychological and sociological foundation for its authority.

Key Terms
العادة (al-'ādah): A personal habit formed by repetition of an action after finding comfort in it.
العرف (al-'urf): The habit of the group, formed when a personal habit is imitated and spreads among most individuals.
الباعث (al-bā'ith): The motive or driving force behind a voluntary action, either external or internal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Custom ('urf) in Hanafi law originates from the imitation and spread of a personal habit, and once established, it becomes a subsidiary source of law when it is consistent and does not contradict primary texts.

From Individual Habit to Social Custom: The Genesis of 'Urf
Explains the psychological and social genesis of 'ada and 'urf in actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the process by which an individual habit ('ada) transforms into a social custom ('urf) according to Hanafi legal theory.

Core Idea
A personal habit becomes a binding custom only when imitated by the community — 'urf is the habit of the group.

This lesson traces the psychological and social roots of 'urf, showing that it is not arbitrary but emerges from human nature and necessity. Understanding this process is key to knowing when 'urf can be a source of law in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the role of motive
Every voluntary action has a motive, either external (benefit) or internal (emotion). This motive initiates the action.
Motive is the seed of 'ada.
2
Trace the formation of individual habit
When a person feels comfortable with an action and repeats it, the action becomes a personal habit ('ada).
Repetition is essential.
3
Explain the shift to social custom
Others imitate the habit due to love of imitation; the imitation repeats and spreads among the majority, forming 'urf.
Imitation and spread are key.
4
Define 'urf as group habit
The passage defines 'urf as 'ada al-jama'ah (habit of the group). It is the collective version of individual habit.
This ties 'urf to social consensus.

Chapter Sixty-Seven: The Origin of Custom ('Ada) and 'Urf, Their Definitions, and the Relationship Between Them - First Section: The Origin of 'Ada and 'Urf
3/67 - Every voluntary action must have a motive. This motive is either external, like the appearance of benefit from a thing or action, or internal psychological, like the desire for revenge driving to retaliation, or extreme shyness causing silence or veiling in certain situations from certain persons.
When a person feels comfortable with the action he inclined to due to that motive, and repeats it, it becomes through repetition a habit ('ada) for him.
Then if another imitates him in it due to the motive of love of imitation, and this imitation is repeated and spreads among the majority of individuals, then at that point 'urf emerges, which is in reality the habit of the group.
4/67 - And just as this occurs in actions, it also occurs in statements.
A - Man is compelled to communicate with those among whom he lives. Communication through gestures and the like is difficult and slow. Therefore humans resorted to the method of expression through words, which are sounds they become accustomed to using until they become a general language among them.
B - Then with the expansion of industries and civil sciences, expression in the original general language in these industries and sciences becomes difficult, lengthy, and causing confusion. Therefore the people of crafts, sciences, or religions resort to using special terms they coin by new invention or by repeated circulation to signify meanings and things that are easily understood from these technical terms, which cannot be replaced in signification except by lengthy explanation.
Often the origin of customary terms is linguistic metaphors from which the intended meaning is not understood except with a context; then their usage is repeated so they become famous metaphors; then the commonness of usage increases until the intended meaning is understood from them without context, and their original meanings are abandoned until they are not understood except with a context. At that point the situation reverses: what was a linguistic metaphor becomes a customary reality, and what was a linguistic reality becomes a customary metaphor.
5/67 - Habits that spread in countries or among specific classes of people do not arise from single motives and in a single way.
But most habits arise from need: a specific situation occurs to people calling them to a specific action, so the action is repeated and becomes widespread until it becomes a prevailing 'urf, as in the emergence of bay' al-wafa' (sale with right of redemption) and the endowment of types of movable wealth such as funeral equipment and books.
These needs differ according to natural environments and their facilities, and according to social environments and their systems and characteristics such as beliefs, religious rituals, morals, system of governance, institutions of learning and industries, arts, freedom of thought, etc.
Source pages p. 811

Commentary
This passage is a theoretical explanation of the genesis of 'ada (individual habit) and 'urf (social custom). The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by stating that every voluntary action requires a motive. Motives can be external (like perceiving benefit) or internal (like revenge or shyness). When a person repeatedly performs an action they are comfortable with, it becomes a personal habit. When others imitate that habit due to the human tendency to imitate, and this imitation spreads throughout a community, it becomes an 'urf—essentially, the habit of the group.

The same process applies to speech. Language itself begins as habitual sounds used for communication, as gestures are inefficient. As fields like industry and science develop, specialized terminology arises through new coinage or repeated usage, becoming technical 'urf. A key observation is that many customary terms start as linguistic metaphors that require a context to be understood. Through repeated use, they become so common that the metaphorical meaning is understood without context, and the original literal meaning is forgotten. Thus, the metaphorical meaning becomes the customary reality (haqiqa 'urfiyya), and the original literal meaning becomes obsolete or metaphorical itself.

Finally, the author notes that habits arise from diverse motives, but most originate from necessity. When a need arises, a specific action is performed repeatedly, eventually becoming a prevailing custom. Examples include bay' al-wafa' (a sale with a condition of repurchase) and endowments of movable goods like funeral equipment. The specific customs that emerge depend on natural and social environments, including beliefs, religious practices, morals, governance, education, arts, and intellectual freedom.

This analysis is foundational for the Hanafi theory of 'urf as a source of law, showing that 'urf is not arbitrary but emerges from human nature and social necessity.

Key Terms
العادة (al-‘ādah): An individual's repeated action that becomes habitual due to a motive, whether internal or external.
العرف (al-‘urf): A widespread practice among the majority of people in a community, arising from imitation and repetition of a habit.
الحقيقة العرفية (al-ḥaqīqah al-‘urfiyyah): A term that originally was a linguistic metaphor but due to widespread customary usage conveys its meaning without need for context, overriding the original literal meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary mechanism by which an individual habit becomes a social custom ('urf)?
A) Government legislation
B) Scholarly consensus (ijma')
C) Imitation by others and repetition until it spreads among the majority
D) Divine command

Key Takeaway
Custom ('urf) is not an arbitrary imposition but emerges naturally from individual habits that are imitated and spread due to human tendencies and needs.

From Linguistic Metaphor to Customary Reality: The Genesis of Linguistic 'Urf
Explains the development of linguistic 'urf, especially the shift from metaphor to customary reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how linguistic 'urf develops, especially the transformation of metaphorical expressions into customary realities (haqiqa 'urfiyya).

Core Idea
Frequent use turns a metaphor into a custom; the customary meaning overrides the literal.

Just as actions can become customs, so can words. This lesson explores how specialized terminology and metaphorical expressions evolve through repeated use into customary language, a key concept in Hanafi legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the need for communication
Humans need to communicate efficiently; gestures are slow, so language emerges from habitual sounds.
Language itself is an 'urf.
2
Trace the emergence of technical terms
As fields develop, the general language becomes insufficient; specialists coin new terms or repurpose old ones via repeated use.
This creates 'urf khas (specific custom).
3
Analyze the metaphor-to-reality shift
Many customary terms begin as metaphors that need context. Through frequent use, the metaphorical meaning becomes understood without context, and the literal meaning fades.
The metaphor becomes haqiqa 'urfiyya.
4
Apply to fiqh interpretation
Hanafi usul gives weight to 'urf in interpreting contracts and oaths; the 'urfiyya meaning is preferred over the literal in case of conflict.
This affects fatwa.

Chapter Sixty-Seven: The Origin of Custom ('Ada) and 'Urf, Their Definitions, and the Relationship Between Them - First Section: The Origin of 'Ada and 'Urf
3/67 - Every voluntary action must have a motive. This motive is either external, like the appearance of benefit from a thing or action, or internal psychological, like the desire for revenge driving to retaliation, or extreme shyness causing silence or veiling in certain situations from certain persons.
When a person feels comfortable with the action he inclined to due to that motive, and repeats it, it becomes through repetition a habit ('ada) for him.
Then if another imitates him in it due to the motive of love of imitation, and this imitation is repeated and spreads among the majority of individuals, then at that point 'urf emerges, which is in reality the habit of the group.
4/67 - And just as this occurs in actions, it also occurs in statements.
A - Man is compelled to communicate with those among whom he lives. Communication through gestures and the like is difficult and slow. Therefore humans resorted to the method of expression through words, which are sounds they become accustomed to using until they become a general language among them.
B - Then with the expansion of industries and civil sciences, expression in the original general language in these industries and sciences becomes difficult, lengthy, and causing confusion. Therefore the people of crafts, sciences, or religions resort to using special terms they coin by new invention or by repeated circulation to signify meanings and things that are easily understood from these technical terms, which cannot be replaced in signification except by lengthy explanation.
Often the origin of customary terms is linguistic metaphors from which the intended meaning is not understood except with a context; then their usage is repeated so they become famous metaphors; then the commonness of usage increases until the intended meaning is understood from them without context, and their original meanings are abandoned until they are not understood except with a context. At that point the situation reverses: what was a linguistic metaphor becomes a customary reality, and what was a linguistic reality becomes a customary metaphor.
5/67 - Habits that spread in countries or among specific classes of people do not arise from single motives and in a single way.
But most habits arise from need: a specific situation occurs to people calling them to a specific action, so the action is repeated and becomes widespread until it becomes a prevailing 'urf, as in the emergence of bay' al-wafa' (sale with right of redemption) and the endowment of types of movable wealth such as funeral equipment and books.
These needs differ according to natural environments and their facilities, and according to social environments and their systems and characteristics such as beliefs, religious rituals, morals, system of governance, institutions of learning and industries, arts, freedom of thought, etc.
Source pages p. 811

Commentary
This passage is a theoretical explanation of the genesis of 'ada (individual habit) and 'urf (social custom). The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by stating that every voluntary action requires a motive. Motives can be external (like perceiving benefit) or internal (like revenge or shyness). When a person repeatedly performs an action they are comfortable with, it becomes a personal habit. When others imitate that habit due to the human tendency to imitate, and this imitation spreads throughout a community, it becomes an 'urf—essentially, the habit of the group.

The same process applies to speech. Language itself begins as habitual sounds used for communication, as gestures are inefficient. As fields like industry and science develop, specialized terminology arises through new coinage or repeated usage, becoming technical 'urf. A key observation is that many customary terms start as linguistic metaphors that require a context to be understood. Through repeated use, they become so common that the metaphorical meaning is understood without context, and the original literal meaning is forgotten. Thus, the metaphorical meaning becomes the customary reality (haqiqa 'urfiyya), and the original literal meaning becomes obsolete or metaphorical itself.

Finally, the author notes that habits arise from diverse motives, but most originate from necessity. When a need arises, a specific action is performed repeatedly, eventually becoming a prevailing custom. Examples include bay' al-wafa' (a sale with a condition of repurchase) and endowments of movable goods like funeral equipment. The specific customs that emerge depend on natural and social environments, including beliefs, religious practices, morals, governance, education, arts, and intellectual freedom.

This analysis is foundational for the Hanafi theory of 'urf as a source of law, showing that 'urf is not arbitrary but emerges from human nature and social necessity.

Key Terms
العادة (al-‘ādah): An individual's repeated action that becomes habitual due to a motive, whether internal or external.
العرف (al-‘urf): A widespread practice among the majority of people in a community, arising from imitation and repetition of a habit.
الحقيقة العرفية (al-ḥaqīqah al-‘urfiyyah): A term that originally was a linguistic metaphor but due to widespread customary usage conveys its meaning without need for context, overriding the original literal meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When it appears in classical dictionaries

Key Takeaway
Linguistic custom can transform metaphors into primary meanings, and Hanafi law prioritizes these customary meanings in legal interpretation.

Custom in Hanafi Legal Theory: Origin, Definition and Legal Weight
Explains the formation and definition of custom in Islamic legal theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sound ('urf sahih) and corrupt ('urf fasid) customs, and apply the Hanafi principle that sound custom serves as a source of law (dalil) in the absence of explicit textual evidence.

Core Idea
Custom's legal authority depends on its origin: only customs rooted in genuine need and wisdom are acceptable; harmful customs must be abolished.

Customs and 'urf pervade every society, but not all carry legal weight. In this lesson, we explore how customs arise—whether by ruler's decree or hereditary transmission—and how Islamic jurisprudence, particularly the Hanafi school, evaluates them. Building on the work of Mustafa al-Zarqa, we will distinguish the good from the evil and understand the psychological and legal power of entrenched practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Origins of Custom
Read the first paragraph: customs may be initiated by a ruler's order (e.g., Fatimid celebration of Mawlid) or be purely hereditary from ancestors without rational need (e.g., pre-Islamic Arab customs). Identify which origin each example falls under.
The source also gives the Syrian solar-year lease custom as an example of ruler-initiated custom.
2
Understand the Psychological Power of Custom
The passage explains that custom becomes 'second nature' and its removal causes hardship. Note the quote from Ibn 'Abidin and the principle that prophets gradually wean people from harmful customs, as illustrated by 'A'isha's report.
This explains why Islamic legislation was revealed gradually.
3
Distinguish Good from Evil Customs
The author lists evil customs (debt slavery, female infanticide, sati, etc.) and emphasizes that these must be combated. Recognize that not all customs are valid—only those based on sincere need and wise benefit.
This distinction is crucial for fatwa: a mufti must evaluate the underlying need and wisdom.
4
Grasp the Linguistic and Technical Definition of 'Aadah
The word 'adah derives from repetition ('awd). In usul al-fiqh, it is defined as a repeated practice that becomes easy. Note that this definition is neutral—it covers both good and bad customs.
The full technical definition is cut off in this passage, but the key elements are repetition and ease.

Chapter (67): Origin of Custom ('Aadah) and 'Urf and Their Definitions. Customs and 'urf may arise by the order of the ruler or by his desire and direction, like the custom of celebrating the Prophet's birthday which was initiated by the Shi'a Fatimid rule, then spread and was adopted by other Islamic countries (1). Customs and 'urf may be purely hereditary from ancestors without a real need calling for them, as in many customs of the Jahiliyyah connected to their beliefs before the dominance of Islam in the Arab lands. 6/67 - Customs and 'urf have authority over souls and control over minds. When a custom becomes entrenched, it is considered a necessity of life, because action, as psychologists say, by frequent repetition becomes familiar to nerves and organs, especially if need requires it. Hence they say: 'Custom is a second nature.' And our jurists say: 'Removing people from their customs involves great hardship.' (See: Treatise 'Nashr al-'Urf' by Ibn 'Abidin in his collection of treatises (1152 and 120). Therefore, prophets and reformers endure many difficulties and terrors, sometimes taking people by force and sometimes gradually, to change them from their customs and 'urf. In this sense, 'A'isha, the Mother of the Believers (may Allah be pleased with her) described the policy of Islamic legislation: 'The first thing that was revealed of the Qur'an was a surah mentioning Paradise and Hell, until when people turned to Islam, the halal and haram were revealed. If the first thing revealed was: 'Do not drink wine' and 'Do not commit zina', they would have said: 'We will never give up wine and zina.' From the transactional customs that arose in our Syrian lands by the order of the rulers, people today deal with leasing real estate by the solar Christian year, since the French Mandate law was issued requiring that the lease year for the tenant is 365 days in any case, whereas before that people's custom was leasing by the lunar Hijri year. (Reported by al-Bukhari r: History of Islamic Legislation by the professors al-Sayyis, al-Subki, and al-Barbari p. 39)(1). 7/67 - It becomes clear from this that customs and 'urf include good and bad, since not everything that people get accustomed to and come to know arises from a sincere need and wise benefit that the customary matter is an easy means for them. People may become accustomed to customs based on inherited ignorances and misguidances that make society miserable and in which there is no benefit, such as enslaving an insolvent debtor among the Romans and in the Arab Jahiliyyah, burying baby girls alive, internal raids, and the sa'iba (2) in Jahiliyyah, burying the wife alive with her husband when he dies among the pagan Hindus, burying precious possessions with their dead owners among the ancient Egyptians, and the guardians of girls taking their dowries when marrying them according to the custom of the Bedouin Arabs in our Syrian lands and elsewhere to this day. All this and its like are evil customs and 'urf that must be fought with education, legislation, and regulations enacted and issued by those in authority. The Second Section: Definition of 'Aadah. 8/67 - 'Aadah in the language is derived from al-'awd or al-mu'awadah meaning repetition. It is a noun for the repetition of action and reaction until its practice becomes easy like a natural disposition. Therefore it is said: 'Custom is a second nature' (Mufradat al-Qur'an by al-Raghib al-Isfahani). The scholars of Usul al-Fiqh have defined custom as: (1) See also the treatise 'Al-'Urf wa al-'Aadah fi Ra'yi al-Fuqaha' by Professor Ahmad Fahmi Abu Sunnah, teacher at the Faculty of Shari'ah at Al-Azhar, pp. 13-17; it is a valuable treatise by which its author attained the rank of professor in Shari'ah. It has recently been published during this third edition of our book, and we have summarized from it much of this research on the origin of customs and 'urf. (2) The meaning of sa'iba among the Arabs has preceded in the discussion of characteristics of ownership, so see (f 6/25).
Source pages p. 813

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical and historical introduction to the concept of custom ('adah) and 'urf in Islamic legal theory by the contemporary Hanafi scholar Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It opens by explaining the origin of customs: they may be initiated by a ruler's order or desire (e.g., the Fatimid-initiated celebration of the Prophet's birthday, or the French Mandate's imposition of the solar year for leases in Syria), or they may be purely hereditary from forefathers without rational need, like many pre-Islamic Arab customs. The author then asserts the psychological power of custom, citing modern psychology and classical Hanafi jurists (Ibn 'Abidin) that entrenched custom becomes a 'second nature' and its removal causes great hardship, which is why prophets and reformers gradually wean people from harmful customs. The example from 'A'isha (RA) illustrates the gradual approach of Islamic legislation. Next, al-Zarqa distinguishes good from evil customs, listing examples of oppressive pre-Islamic and non-Islamic practices (debt slavery, female infanticide, sati, etc.) that must be combated by education and law. Finally, he moves to the linguistic definition of 'adah as repetition (from 'awd), and introduces the technical definition used by Usul al-Fiqh scholars (though the full definition is cut off here). The passage serves as foundational material for understanding the role of custom in Hanafi fatwa methodology, emphasizing that not all customs are valid – only those arising from genuine need and wisdom are acceptable, while harmful customs must be reformed.

Key Terms
العادة (al-'adah): In language: repetition of an action/response until it becomes easy like a nature. In Usul: habitual practice of a people, whether good or evil.
العرف (al-'urf): Custom that is recognized as good and accepted by sound minds; often used interchangeably with 'adah but sometimes distinguished as a good custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary condition for a custom to be considered legally valid?
A) It must be practiced by the majority.
B) It must be based on genuine need and wisdom.
C) It must be inherited from forefathers.
D) It must be initiated by a ruler.

Key Takeaway
Custom in Islamic law is a tool, not a master: it is accepted only when rooted in genuine need and wisdom, and must be rejected when it perpetuates harm or contradicts shari'ah.

Definition of 'Adah and Its Distinction from Rational Necessity
Defines 'adah and distinguishes from rational necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'adah and distinguish it from rational necessity (talazum 'aqli).

Core Idea
'Adah is a recurring matter without rational relation; rational necessity is not custom.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of 'adah (custom) in Hanafi legal theory. We will explore its precise definition as 'a recurring matter without a rational relation' and understand why rational cause-effect sequences are excluded. This distinction is crucial because 'adah, unlike rational necessity, can influence fatwa and legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the phrase 'al-amr al-mutakarrar min ghayr 'ilaqa 'aqliyya' in the Arabic text. This is the core definition of 'adah.
This definition appears at the beginning of the passage.
2
Identify the exclusion
Note the condition 'min ghayr 'ilaqa 'aqliyya'. If recurrence arises from a rational cause-effect relationship, it is not 'adah but rational necessity (talazum 'aqli).
Example: moving a ring with a finger is rational necessity, not custom.
3
Understand the basis
Recognize that 'adah is based on habit, inclination, or natural factors, not logical necessity. The recurrence must be contingent, not inevitable.
This is why jurists consider 'adah a valid source for rulings.

الفصل (٦٧) منشأ العادة والعرف وتعريفهما
الأمر المتكرر من غير علاقة عقلية
فإذا كان التكرر ناشئاً عن علاقة عقلية، وهي التي يحكم فيها العقل بهذا التكرار لم يكن عندئذ من قبيل العادة، بل من قبيل التلازم العقلي. وذلك كتكرر حدوث الأثر كلما حدث مؤثره بسبب أن المؤثر علة لا يتخلف عنها معلولها كتحرك الخاتم بحركة الإصبع، وتبدل مكان الشيء بحركته. فهذا لا عادة مهما تكرر لأنه ناشيء عن تلازم وارتباط في العلة والمعلول يقضي به العقل وليس ناشئاً عن ميل أو طبع أو عامل طبيعي.
(ر) التقرير والتحبير لابن أمير الحاج شرح التحرير لابن الهمام ٢/ ٩/٦٧ - يتبين من هذا التعريف أن العادة في الاصطلاح لها مفهوم شامل واسع الحدود، لأن قولهم في تعريفها: «الأمر المتكرر» يشمل كل حادث يتكرر من حيث ان لفظ «الأمر» كلفظ الشيء» من أوسع ألفاظ اللغة عموماً وشمولاً:
١ - فالعادة تطلق تارة على ما يعتاده الفرد من الناس في شؤونه الخاصة، كعادته في نومه، وأكله ونوع مأكوله، وحديثه، وكثير أفعاله. وهذه هي العادة الفردية.
٢ - وتطلق تارة أخرى على ما تعتاده الجماعات والجماهير، مما ينشأ في الأصل عن اتجاه عقلي، وتفكير حسناً كان أم قبيحاً. وهذه ترادف العرف، كما سيأتي بيانه
(١) يقول الفقهاء في بعض المواطن إن العادة تتحقق بتكرر الفعل مرتين أو ثلاثاً، لأنها مأخوذة من العود أو المعاودة (ر) الأشباه والنظائر لابن نجيم، الفن الأول تحت القاعدة السادسة ١٢٨/١). ولا يخفى أن مرادهم بذلك عادة الفرد أما عادة الجماعة التي هي: عرف الجمهور، فلا ينطبق عليها هذا الحد، لأن العادة لا تشيع وتنتشر بين الجماهير إلا بعد أن تتكرر بين الناس مرات لا تحصى.
الباب التاسع: نظرية العرف
٣ - وتطلق العادة أيضاً بوجه عام على كل حالة متكررة، سواء أكانت ناشئة:
أ - عن سبب طبيعي، كإسراع بلوغ الأشخاص، ونضج الثمار في الأقاليم الحارة، وإبطائه في الباردة وكثرة الأمطار في بعض الأقاليم صيفاً، وفي بعضها شتاءً بحسب الموقع الجغرافي والعوامل الطبيعية.
ب - أو ناشئة عن الأهواء والشهوات وفساد الأخلاق كالتقاعس عن فعل الخيرات والسعي بالضرر والفساد وتفشي الكذب وأكل المال بالباطل والفسق والظلم، مما يسميه الفقهاء: فساد الزمان.
ج - أو ناشئة عن حادث خاص، كفشو اللحن الناشيء من اختلاط العرب بالأعاجم.
فكل ذلك يعتبر في نظر الفقهاء من قبيل العادات وقد راعاها المجتهدون في الفتيا والقضاء، وقرر لها الفقهاء ما يناسبها من أحكام.
المطلب الثالث: تعريف العرف
٦٧/١٠ - قدمنا في بحث مصادر الفقه تعريف العرف المعتبر فقهاً بأنه: عادة جمهور قوم في قول أو فعل.
وقد وضعنا هذا التعريف مستوحى من التعاريف والشرائط التي يذكرها الفقهاء والأصوليون وتحرينا فيه أن يكون كاشفاً عن حقيقة العرف في نظر الفقهاء بأوضح صورة، وبأدق الحدود، لأنا وجدنا التعاريف المأثورة غامضة الصورة ومشتبهة الحدود بالنسبة إلى المنهاج التعليمي التبسيطي الذي نبني على أساسه، ونسير في ظله.
Chapter (67): Origin of Custom and Urf and Their Definitions. A recurring matter without a rational relation. If the recurrence arises from a rational relation—that is, where reason judges this recurrence—then it is not custom, but rather a rational necessity. Such as the repeated occurrence of the effect whenever its cause occurs, because the cause is a necessary condition whose effect does not lag behind, like the movement of a ring with the movement of a finger, or the change of place of a thing by its movement. This is not custom, no matter how often it recurs, because it arises from a necessary connection and correlation between cause and effect, decided by reason, and not from inclination, temperament, or a natural factor. (R) Al-Taqrir wa al-Tahbir by Ibn Amir al-Hajj, commentary on Al-Tahrir by Ibn al-Humam, 2/9/67. It becomes clear from this definition that 'adah in terminology has a comprehensive concept with broad boundaries, because their statement in its definition: 'the recurring matter' includes every event that recurs, since the word 'matter' like the word 'thing' is among the broadest words in language generally and comprehensively: 1. Sometimes 'adah is applied to what an individual habitually does in his private affairs, such as his habit in sleeping, eating, type of food, speech, and many of his actions. This is individual custom. 2. And sometimes it is applied to what groups and masses habitually do, which originally arises from a mental direction and thinking, whether good or bad. This is synonymous with 'urf, as will be explained. (1) Jurists in some contexts say that custom is established by the repetition of an act twice or thrice, because it is derived from 'awd or mu'awada. See Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir by Ibn Nujaym, the first art under the sixth maxim, 1/128. It is clear that by this they mean individual custom. As for group custom, which is the 'urf of the public, this limit does not apply to it, because custom does not become widespread among the masses except after recurring countless times. Chapter Nine: Theory of 'Urf. 3. 'Adah is also generally applied to every recurring state, whether arising from: a) a natural cause, such as the acceleration of people's puberty, ripening of fruits in hot regions, and its slowness in cold ones, and abundance of rain in some regions in summer and in others in winter according to geographical location and natural factors. b) or arising from whims, desires, and corruption of morals, such as reluctance to do good deeds, striving for harm and corruption, prevalence of lying, consuming wealth unjustly, immorality, and oppression—what jurists call 'corruption of the time'. c) or arising from a specific event, such as the spread of grammatical mistakes arising from the mixing of Arabs with non-Arabs. All of this is considered by jurists as customs, and the mujtahids have taken them into account in fatwa and judgement, and jurists have established for them appropriate rulings. Third Section: Definition of 'Urf. 67/10. We have presented in the chapter on sources of fiqh the definition of legally considered 'urf as: 'the custom of a group of people in speech or action.' We have formulated this definition inspired by the definitions and conditions mentioned by jurists and usul scholars, and we sought to make it revealing of the reality of 'urf in the view of jurists in the clearest form and with the most precise boundaries, because we found the transmitted definitions to be obscure in form and ambiguous in boundaries relative to the simplified educational approach upon which we base and proceed.
Source pages p. 815

Commentary
This passage from Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Am addresses the origins and definitions of 'adah (custom) and 'urf (custom as a source of law). The author begins by distinguishing 'adah from rational necessity. 'Adah is defined as a recurring matter without rational relation. If recurrence is due to a rational cause-effect relationship, it is not 'adah but rational necessity. This distinction is crucial because 'adah, as a legal source, relies on habitual recurrence, not logical necessity. The author then notes that the term 'adah is broad, encompassing individual habits (such as a person's sleeping or eating patterns) and communal habits (which are synonymous with 'urf). Individual custom can be established by repetition two or three times, as jurists state, but communal custom requires widespread recurrence beyond count. Next, 'adah is further categorized based on its cause: natural (e.g., climatic effects), moral (e.g., corruption of times), or specific events (e.g., spread of grammatical errors). All such recurring states are considered 'adah and have legal implications in fatwa and judgment. Finally, the author defines 'urf as 'the custom of a group of people in speech or action,' which he formulated for clarity. This definition is derived from juristic and usul sources and is intended to be pedagogically accessible. The passage lays the conceptual groundwork for the theory of 'urf in Hanafi law, emphasizing that 'urf is a subset of 'adah—specifically communal custom—and that it must be widespread and consistent. The author also highlights that both 'adah and 'urf are reliable sources for deriving rulings, as long as they do not contradict explicit texts.

Key Terms
العادة (al-'ādah): الأمر المتكرر من غير علاقة عقلية (A recurring matter without a rational relation)
التلازم العقلي (al-talāzum al-'aqlī): ارتباط العلة والمعلول الذي يقضي به العقل (Necessary cause-effect connection decided by reason)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the movement of a ring with a finger not considered 'adah?
A) It occurs too infrequently.
B) It arises from a rational cause-effect relation.
C) It is a natural phenomenon beyond human control.
D) It is not mentioned by jurists.

Key Takeaway
'Adah is defined as recurring without rational necessity; rational cause-effect sequences are excluded from custom.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
10 min
Types of 'Adah: Individual, Communal, Natural, Moral, and Event-Based
Classifies types of 'adah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify different types of 'adah and explain their legal relevance in fatwa and judgment.

Core Idea
'Adah includes individual habits, communal customs, natural patterns, moral states, and historical events.

This lesson explores the classification of 'adah based on its subject and cause. The source passage distinguishes five main types: individual custom (ādah fardiyya), communal custom (synonymous with 'urf), natural custom (e.g., climatic effects), moral custom (e.g., corruption of the time), and event-based custom (e.g., spread of grammatical errors). Each type has different legal implications and levels of authority. Understanding these categories helps a mufti correctly apply the relevant rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five types
Locate in the Arabic text the three numbered categories and the subcategories under 'generally applied'. List them: (1) individual custom, (2) communal custom, (3) natural, moral, and event-based custom.
The passage says 'al-'ādah tuṭlaq tāratan...' indicating different applications.
2
Understand the basis for each type
For each type, note the cause: individual by personal repetition, communal by collective mental direction, natural by natural factors, moral by corrupt inclinations, event-based by specific historical occurrences.
The legal weight varies; communal custom ('urf) is the strongest.
3
Relate to fatwa practice
Consider how a mufti would treat each type. Individual custom may affect personal rulings, communal custom can establish general rules, natural custom may influence time-bound rulings, moral custom may require social reform, event-based custom may be temporary.
The text says jurists have established appropriate rulings for all these.

Definition and Legal Status of 'Urf
Defines 'urf and its legal role

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'urf as a subset of 'adah and list the conditions for its acceptance as a legal source.

Core Idea
'Urf is the widespread custom of a group, authoritative in fatwa when consistent and not contradicting texts.

This lesson focuses on 'urf (custom as a source of law), which the author defines as 'the custom of a group of people in speech or action.' Unlike general 'adah, 'urf is specifically communal and holds greater authority in Hanafi law. The passage notes that this definition was crafted for pedagogical clarity, derived from classical definitions. We will also examine how 'urf relates to 'adah: 'urf is a subset—specifically, communal custom that has become widespread and is considered a binding source in areas where no textual ruling exists.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of 'urf
Find the phrase 'عَادَةُ جُمْهُورِ قَوْمٍ فِي قَوْلٍ أَوْ فِعْلٍ' in the Arabic text. This is the definition of 'urf.
The author says this definition is from the sources but simplified.
2
Understand the relationship to 'adah
Note that 'urf is a specific type of 'adah: it is the custom of the majority (jumhur) of a people, not an individual.
The text says 'urf is synonymous with communal 'adah.
3
Identify conditions for validity
From the definition and surrounding discussion, infer conditions: it must be widespread (jumhur), consistent, and not contradict a clear text (nass).
These conditions are standard in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
